The Minimum Poet
The Red Pape
There are four classes of poets: the poet,
the r.inor poet, the minimum poet and the
minus poet. Few examples of the minus
poet are ever seen, save in the “Answers to
Correspondents” column. But the minimum
poet is distressingly frequent: he, or more
usually she, breaks out in pretty little book-
lets, especially at this season of the year.
Thus C. R. Moore, in Linnet Songs (Mount
Hope, N.S.W. —no publisher stated) knows
how to put words together in metre, but his
words have nothing to say, except, maybe, in
some nice girl’s autograph album. * Rose,
for instance, might blush at this tribute:—
Your eyes are delphinium blue,
Your cheeks are geranium red,
Like a creamy eschscholtzia your skin
And, golden straw sunshine your head. ’
You’re the daisiest flower of them all
The spiciest blossom that blows, ’
And the fairies that came to your birth,
What name could they give you but Rose ?
£chsch&a 0 ? Uldn,t She haVG been christened
And Philip Norman, in A Snrig of Wattle,
and O.F. (Penfold, Sydney), has but this
or interest to remark of his sprig:—
Fairest symbol of my country;
Fairest plant on Austral’s moor;
While love lasteth I will love thee:
Thee I’ll love, or love no more!
Christ tbUS be k eg * ns a minimum poem on
Good old Christmas is here once again,
And we crown him as king of the year.
Not with snow, nor with ice, nor with rain
Does he come ip this bright southern sphere:
But the warmth of his heart, it is plain,
Is expressed by the temperature. ...
Words! Words!
n Wallis Sugden’s Remembrance and
«Vni Ge^rs e Robertson, Melb.), sings thus
or lhe Circular Saws” :
Titanic the labors that tear
The heart from the primeval bush—
Steam power, horse power, and men
With brains and with sinews at push
She also writes “Lines to Lady Barron,
welcoming her as Patroness of the Writers’
Club, Hobart” :
And since good wishes are by nature prayers,
We pray that God may ever guide and bless,
In Council and in State, in hearth and home,
Our gracious and beloved patroness.
There is no reason why she shouldn’t have
wntten these lines, but no one but a minimum
poet would have dared to republish them.
Also, she writes various hymns and other
devotional verses —with not one suggestion
of inspiration in the lot-
Maggots
J. H. If abre, the most celebrated natural-
ist of our times, writes engagingly upon the
insect world—that strange, unhuman under-
world, where the sickening cruelty of Nature
rages unchecked and unheeded; but his pen
becomes rapturous when he deals with mag-
gots, For Fabre loves maggots. The Life
of the Fly, translated by A. T. Mattos
(Hodder and Stoughton, per Dymock’s Li-
brary, Sydney), the second volume of the
authorised edition of the works of J
Henri Fabre, is full of maggots. En-
chanting little beggars they are, too,
and with highly specialised and impor-
tant work to do for the world. The
larva of the Anthrax fly, for instance, does
not chew its food —-another grub—as do the
other carnivorous grubs ; it does not eat, it
inhales. As Fabre delectably exclaims, “Its
attack is a mere kiss ; but what a perfidious
kiss!” And most maggots of the numerous
species of the fly have the valuable power of
liquefying the dead flesh they attack. With-
out this marvellous resource of converting
their food into a digestible liquid, dead flesh
would merely dry up like a mummy. Pro-
tect a dead bird from the access of flies, and
that corps? would merely mummify.
In the hope of awakening us to a proper
sense of our insignificance, pulpit orators
sometimes make an unfair use of the grave
and its worms. Let us put no faith in their
doleful rhetoric. The chemistry of man’s
final dissolution is eloquent enough of our
emptiness; there is no need to add imaginary
horrors. The worm of the sepulchre is an
invention of cantankerous minds, incapable
of seeing things as they are. Covered by
but a few inches of earth, the dead can
sleep their quiet sleep: no fly will ever come
to take advantage of them.
At the surface of the soil, exposed to the
air, the hideous invasion is possible; ay. it
is the invariable rule. For the meiting
down and remoulding of matter, man is no
better, corpse for corpse, than the lowest
of the brutes. Then the fly exercises her
rights and deals with us as she does with
any ordinary animal refuse. Nature treats
us with piagnificent indifference in her great
regenerating-factory: placed in her crucibles,
animals and men, beggars and kings are
one and all alike. There you have true
equality, the only equality in this world of
ours: equality in the presence of the mag-
got.
“The maggot is the primary cause of
dissolution after death ; it is, above all, the
putrefactive chemist.” But there are simple
ways to elude the bluebottle’s cheerful habit
of laying her eggs in our meat. She refuses
to lay her eggs on the thinnest paper cover-
ing ; therefore from Fabre’s investigations
we get the useful hint that the best way to
preserve our meat from the fly—and also
our furs from the moth that corrupts and
gnashes his teeth —is to place each article
within a sealed paper bag.
Interspersed between the chapters in which
this incomparable observer explores this
dreadful underworld are chapters of Fabre’s
autobiography that possess an equal inter-
est. He tells us, in his captivatingly naive
prose, of his youth of poverty, his lack of
schooling, the expedients he resorted to to
acquire knowledge. When he was a poor
under-teacher at a school he taught himself
algebra—by the heroic expedient of teaching
another young man. When the pupil asked
him to teach him algebra Fabre, with all the
fine confidence of his 20 years, refrained
from exposing his own utter ignorance.
An heroic method of learning to swim is
to leap boldly into the sea. Let us hurl
ourselves head first into the algebraical
gulf; and perhaps the imminent danger of
drowning will call forth efforts capable of
bringing me to land. I know nothing of
what he wants. It makes no difference:
let’s go ahead and plunge into the mystery.
I shall learn by teaching.
But he had no book on algebra, nor could
he afford to buy one. A request for the
loan of such a work from his mathematical
colleague would expose him to that poten-
tate’s high ridicule. Fabre simply stole the
book from that teacher. The first chapter
that interested him was headed, “Newton’s
Binomial Theorem.” To Fabre’s astonish-
ment he found that he understood it; and
the rest of algebra looked like plain sailing.
But: “I was to recover from the illusion
later, when the binomial theorem, that light,
crisp biscuit, was followed by heavier and
less digestible fare.” But he learnt algebra
—and his pupil had never a suspicion that
his master was a fellow pupil.
_ln fact, Fabre received in the course of
his life only two lessons of a scientific char-
acter, one in anatomy—the dissecting of a
snail—and one in chemistry, in which the
class was blown up. And he learned chem-
istry by the means that had enabled him to
master algebra: he taught himself by teach-
ing others.
These fragments of autobiography indicate
that when the whole story of his life is
published in English the world will have the
most interesting, the quaintest, the most
modest, the most genially humorous of
viodern autobiographies.

December
The usual batch of dilatory entries for the
sonnet competitions arrived on December 8,
five days too late. These eight sonnets ap-
preciably raise the level of the competi-
tion. M.8.K.. for instance, begins with
fervor thus :
December, Queen of dust and drought and death,
Within whose arms thy father, with’ring, dies—
The fierce light fades not from thy burning eyes,
A blood-red sun thy crown—a sword thy breath,
A slaying sword that flashes from its sheath
Yet sends its wild love paean to the skies;
But the sestette violently introduces an
alien idea. “Ivondar,” “Reka,” and Charles
J. Calhoun are to be commended for their
efforts. H. Russell Bruford fails to come up
to his happy beginning
December wears black ribbons in her hair,
Bewitching, yet such palpable pretence
Of mourning for the old year passing hence,
W hen ’tis her joy the baby year to bear....
And H. S. Allen, before he becomes incom-
prehensible, has some pleasant imagery and
false rimes:—
Old Sultan Year hath reaped a ripe reward:
December, to his harem borne unseen,
O’erlords it warmly, like a desert queen,
The fetich of some hot Arabian horde.
No houri she, but girded with a sword,
Brown-clothed in sackcloth Chidleywise and clean;
Her meat is milk and honeyfruit; and lean,
Loud locusts all her sentiments applaud.
Patricia Murchison’s sonnet, though her
octette refuses to conform with the strict
riming convention, is captivating enough to
print.
There is a certain sweetness in these days.
It is the sun (I said) that makes it so.
It is the wind that makes the grasses flow
In golden glory toward the sunset haze.
There is a light within the light that plays
Across the river, and upon the sea.
December whispers under every tree,
December sings along the white highways.
There are strange gleams that on the hilltops
run,
Holding a glint of laughter and of tears.
There is a shadowed glory in the skies
Is it the wind? (I said.) —Is it the sun?
Is it a promise fdr the future years?
Is it the beauty of a year thafl dies?
So, too, is this by Marie E. J. Pitt
Oh! Spurred and booted, hot-foot rideth she,
Her brown hand twisted in the flying mane
Of some toild stallion, pltmdered from his
plain
To be her charger of emergency —
Fit steed to bear so fierce a majesty
To war upon the dying year's demesne! —
So comes December, in a fiery rain,
Her bright bare falchion flashing at her knee.
Gall on your gods! 0 cities of dismay,
Lest she remember and stretch forth her
hand,
And loose her leaping terror, fold on fold,
The mad red houses never hand can hold.
And all you are be ciphers traced on sand —
The blown, white desert sand of Yesterday!
It may pay these poets to be more punc-
tual in their January entries, which must
reach this page by February 4.
Fiction
Edith Wharton determines to be popular,
but Edith Wharton is a “best seller” with a
difference. In The Custom of the Country
(Macmillan) the author deserts the bleak
tragedies of her Ethan Fromes, and adven-
tures gaily in high society, easily surpassing
Robert Chambers’s agreeable chatter. Un-
dine Spragg was named after a hair-waver
that her father put on the market the week
she was born: “It’s from andoolay, you know,
the French for crimping; father always
thought the name would make it take.”
Undine was a beautiful girl: she wanted
only one thing in the world, and that was
the best. And the easiest way for her to
get it was by means of a husband. So,
when her parents came to New York, after
having made a fortune in Apex, Undine
started out to get into the society where the
right sort of husbands abounded. She mar-
ries a charming, but futile, representative of
the old American aristocracy; but finds that,
after all, she has got only the second-best.
Her husband is not a millionaire. However,
she falls back upon the custom of the coun-
try—divorce; for, as the author remarks, the
American marriage still lias its uses: “One
couldn’t be divorced without it.” Undine’s
next experiment is with a French nobleman;
but now she finds that she has married not
her husband but the whole family. Quietly
she is put in her place by the tribe of her
new relations, and learns that she must live
not for herself but for the historic family,
even to the amount of firewood she is allowed
to burn in her cold, draughty chateau. She
has picked the wrong horse again. Once
more she tries her luck, whitewashed by dU
vorce, and. becomes the wife of the one man
who can manage her —a many times million-
aire. Edith Wharton keeps a secret about
this man till the right moment, with a shrewd
knowledge of its dramatic effect. Yet even
iu her last incarnation Undine, as ever
before, finds there are still heights to which
marriage has not lifted her. The husband
of a friend of hers beeomes Ambassador to
Britain ; and when the spoilt child asks her
husband for this bauble, too, she learns that
it is the one thing that all his millions cannot
get for her. The British Court won’t have
divorced Ambassadresses.
“‘I never heard of anything so insult-
ing !’ she cried, as if the rule had been
invented to humiliate her . . . . Under all the
dazzle a tiny black cloud remained. She
had learned that there was something she
could never get, something that neither
beauty nor influence nor millions could
ever buy for her. She could never be an
Ambassador’s wife; and as she advanced to
welcome her first guests she said to herself
that it was the one part she was really made
for.”
Somehow we feel sorry for Undine, but
more sorry for her son, by one of her early
husbands, who gets confused by the number
of step-fathers his mother has presented him
with ; and in the end we see the latest hus-
band and the son pathetically making friends
with each other, in a common defence against
Undine’s selfishness.
With the life of the several strata of
“society” in New York, to which Undine,
rising on her divorced husbands 'to higher
things, successively penetrates, Mrs. Whar-
ton is as familiar as she shows herself to be
with the jealously-guarded life of the old
French aristocracy. Her specimens of the
American “aristocracy” of wealth are pene-
tratingly and humorously observed; but her
satire becomes malevolent when she shows
us Undine. That girl is only what the
American parent had made her. The author
has, however, a larger theme than divorce:
she shows us the disintegration of the older
aristocracy of culture at the touch of
swiftly-made millions. Over New York is
sweeping a blizzard of wealth. On her
specific subject, however, the author has
something of importance to say. The. weak
point of the American marriage is: “The fact
that the average American looks down on his
wife.”
“ ‘How much does he let her share in the
real business of life? It’s normal for a
man to work hard for a woman —what’s ab-
normal is his not caring to tell her anything
about it.’
“ ‘To tell Undine? She’d be bored to
death if he did !’
“ ‘Just' so; she’d even feel aggrieved. But
why? Because it’s against the custom of
the country. And whose fault is that? The
man’s again.... Why haven’t we taught our
women to take an interest in our work?
Simnly because we don’t take enough inter-
est in them.’
“ ‘The American man doesn’t —the most
slaving, self-effacing, self-sacrificing ?’
“ ‘Yes ; and the most indifferent, there’s the
point In this country the passion for
making money has preceded the knowing how
to spend it, and the American man lavishes
his fortune on his wife because he doesn’t
know what else to do with it.’
“ ‘Then you call it S' mere want of imagi-
nation for a man to spend his monev on his
wife?’
“ ‘Not necessarily—but it’s a wdnt of ima-
gination to fancy it’s alh he owes
her. . . .In America the real crime yassionnet
is a “big steal” —there’s more excitement in
wrecking railways than homes. Isn c that
the key to our easy divorces V If we cared
tor women in the old barbarous, possessive
way do you suppose we’d give them up as
readily as we do? The real paradox is the
tact that the men who make, materially the
biggest sacrifices for their women, should do
the least for tliem ideally and romantically
....Money and motors and clothes are sim-
ply the big bribe a wife is paid for keeping
out of her husband’s way.’ ”
d*
Bertha Garlan, by Arthur Schnitzler,
translated from the German (Max Goschen
per Uymock’s Library), is a characteristic
novel from the Viennese author who has
hitherto been known in English literature
as, a dramatist. Ilis Anatol and The Green
Cockatoo are plays that have rapidly made
their way to the cosmopolitan stage. This
novel shows Schnitzler a craftsman as skil-
ful in fiction as in drama. It is merely the
story of a widow, whose marriage had brought
her little joy, gradually awaking to the pos-
sibilities of a passionate love. With infinite
delicacy and subtlety Schnitzler traces the
revival of her early love for a now celebrated
musician, leading fit last to her writing from
her small country town to him in Vienna.
Insensibly her love grows by the starved
imagination it hungrily feeds upon,-and at
last she meets him in Vienna. There follows
one night of passion. The woman, weak,
simple and loving, gives herself to him; but
the man is an egoist, and has no further use
for the adoring, but to him possibly stupid,
girl from the country. However, he sug-
gests that she might see him at long inter-
vals —thus putting her, contemptuously, in
her place. Then she, who had hoped to marry
him, recognises that she is not the sort of
woman for an intrigue. The death of a
woman friend, owing to an illegal opera-
tion, dramatically shows her her peril; and
she resigns, herself to her dull life in the
country.
t The character of Bertha Garlan is de-
picted with delicate precision, and with =uch
cogent detail as to make of this mere inci-
dent for a short story a novel of absorbing
interest. It is life—not the romantic imita-
tion of life that masquerades in popular
English fiction.
H. G. Wells begins, rather ponderously, a
new futurist novel, The World Set Free, in
the December English Review. He is go-
ing to tell us what will happen when atomic-
engines are invented—cheap machines for
obtaining power by disintegrating the atom
—a subject that the discovery of radium has
made for his hand. In the same issue of
this best of all English magazines, John
Masefield tails off in a poem called “The
River,” and Grant Watson writes a fine
Australian story on the theme of the black
gin and the white wife.
The aboriginal method of obtaining water
from the roots of trees—a method that has
saved the lives of white as well as of black,
and one of which no adult in Australia
should be ignorant—is fully explained, with
illustrations,_ by J. 11. Maiden, in part 1 of
vol. 6 of his valuable publication of The
Forest Flora of N.S. W.
A Literary and Historical Atlas of Africa
and Australasia, by J. G. Bartholomew, in
Dent’s “Everyman’s Library” (Is. 3d.) is
of value to Australians, though Africa has
priority of consideration by the editor. In
the foreword, too, Canberra is mis-spelt,
though in the map the spelling is correct,
►special maps dealing with us include “Early
\ oyages in the Pacific”’ and orograph-ical,
rainfall, vegetation, temperature, economic,
and population maps of Australasia.
Line maps include exploration routes, water
supply and agriculture for Vic. and N.S.W.,
Canberra and the chief cities, on, however,
too small a scale. There is also a page of
reproductions of early Australian coinage.
But Africa, with her plans of battles and
her ancient historic interest takes the bulk
of the space at the editor’s disposal. There
is room for an Australian publisher or the
federal Government to produce a real Aus-
tralian Atlas.
“Jimmie Kiushiu” writes :—■
Will Honorable arbiter of “Red Page” deign to
receive from unworthy but docile Japanese student
a humble paraphrase of competitively supreme sonnet
on November [Bulletin 18/12/’l3] ? To wit:
‘Sublime Australia in November is as a glorious
maternal lady doing as well as can be expected.
Obstetric operation indicated by modest allusion to
‘her flanks still heaving with her travail pain.’
About her is her Hon. and multitudinous progeny,
viz., ten thousand (10,000), more or less, of Hon.
cicadas, ditto Hon. mosquitoes, parrots and crabs, all
doing appropriate stunts. Remarkable lady exhibit-
ing seren; pride in phenomenal offspring. With
deep deference I thank the Hon. sonneteer for pub-
licly declared truth about Australia! Once the
Little Brown Man coveted a place in the s,un there,
but now
Barbara Holliday, by Marion Miller
Knowles (George Robertson, Melb.), is a
dull narrative of dull people. Its only claim
to mention here is that the dull people
treated _of are Australians and that the
writer is herself an Australian. This con-
ventional tale of the conventional love affairs
of a conventional school-mistress is written
in correct school-mistressy English and is
passably readable.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Question.
On being asked to take the bath at Tottenham,
London, a man started to say the Lord’s Prayer.
—Joyful news item.
Some of us pray for health,
And freedom from all pain;
Some of us pray for wealth,
And others pray for rain;
For water men have prayed,
And some still pray for beer;
And prayers for bread are made
Where bread is scarce and dear.
But here, O Lord, is one
Who feared not drought or pain;
An erring, unwashed son
Who dirty would remain—
Who, by lights low and dim,
Yet saw the Heavenly Hope—
Didst Thou deliver him
From water and from soap?
N.S.W.
O.C.C.
Oases.
Gazing upon the tale of wasted days
That mar the sweetness of my backward
way,
Here and again I glimpse one little day
Of profit, spent in worthier deed or praise—
One small oasis seen through mirk and haze
Of things ill-done in fever. Who shall say
But in the end these rare oases may
Atone for all the desert’s dust and blaze?
A narrow path and rugged I must keep
Now, till I pass yon hills into the Night;
But still the thought is precious that may be
The fair oases yet shall plead for me,
Who crave one boon amid this fading light,
God’s gracious immortality of sleep.
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
The Cheery Chiel.
By plain and hill, come good or ill,
Wherever Fortune’s footsteps strayed,
In woe or weal, the Cheery Chiel
Was welcomed for a rollick blade;
He wore a grin through thick and thin,
Nor ever drew the visage long,
But night and day withal was gay,
And lusty was his blithesome song:
Ho, fill the pan, and pass the can,
And thank our stars there's more to follow!
Good meat and ale shall never fail,
For earth holds more than man may swal-
low.
Then twang the string, and dance the fling,
And thank our stars there’s joy in pran-
cing;
Give over grumps and doleful dumps,
And set the lads and lasses dancing!"
As near he drew the tidings flew,
And crabbed Dulness slunk away;
While Jolly Jinks, with quips and winks,
Set up his court of cronies gay.
With jocund reel the Cheery Chiel
Drew these and sundry to his train;
Nor any heart might mope apart,
Or brood in tribulations vain.
“Come, lilt the rhyme, and trip the time,
For laughter is our largest treasure;
There is no dearth of this on earth;
Then let no niggard stint the measure.
This ivisdom fits all jovial wits,
And only churls shun merry-making;
Let none repine while love and wine
And goodly fare are his for taking!"
The whiles he flung, to old and young,
The largess of his friendly grin ;
Till, mean or high, they hailed him by
With answered mirth, through thick and
thin.
For, foul or fair, he hummed an air,
And tossed a jest to every jar;
To joyance leal, the Cheery Chiel
Spread mellow magic wide and far!
“Then deck the hoard and clear the sward,
While summer makes the pulses tingle;
And hey! for nights, when winter kites,
Of quips and stories round the ingle.
Old gallant Cheer is always here,
With humor in his mien and raiment,
To draw his best from every guest —
He asks hut welcome as repayment!"
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES
Winds of Home.
When westerly winds from the mountains
Come galloping down,
Over the plains and the rivers—
Over the turreted town.
And I close my eyes, I can see the hills
Of Wellington, dry and brown,
And smell the summer grasses
That are brushed by the wind’s blue gown.
When boisterous winds from ihe mountains
Roar over the trees —
Over the masts and the water,
Raising the ripples to seas —
And I dream awhile, I can hear the song
Of a shouting northerly breeze,
And, deep in the shady gorse-blooms,
The drone of the drowsy bees.
When winds from the wide, Blue Mountains
Dash down to the sea,
And the city abandons her labor
To chafe at the mad winds’ glee—
A vision of high, bare wintry hills
Comes, vividly clear, to me.
Oh! the sting of the keen, clean winter,
In the land where my heart would be.
Oh! winds blowing down from the moun-
tains,
Mad winds that roam,
And scream through the trees and the rig-
ging,
And over high turret and dome —
When I hear their song, as a deeper note
Sung low to my heart you come,
Sweet from the skies and the grasses,
Wonderful winds of home.
N.S.W.
WILL LAWSON.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable. acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Neivspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 22, 1914.
WANTED, A DEMI-GOD—OR POS-
SIBLY A PLAIN COVE WILL DO.
The Northern Territory—the place that
everybody talks about, but where hardly
anybody does anything except appoint Fed-
eral officials —had another advertisement
recently. Judge Bevan came down from
the empty province, and propounded his
views—about the 700th set of views to date.
Some other opinions have been recently fur-
nished by Inspector-of-Mines Oliver ; also
Dr. Richard Jones has contributed. Then
Prime Minister Cook, in one of the intervals
of doing nothing, by reason of an insufficient
majority, digested these statements, especi-
ally the most depressing ones, and gave his
verdict on them in this fashion :
THE MAN WANTED.
FOR THE NORTH.
WHERE IS HE COMING FROM?
Melbourne, Friday.—The views which have been
expressed by recently-returned travellers from the
Northern Territory do not, in the opinion of the
Prime Minister, assist much in the solution of the
difficult problem of peopling this latest possession of
the Commonwealth.
“I have read all the statements very carefully,”
said Mr. Cook to-day, “including those made by
Judge Bevan, Mr. Oliver (the inspector of mines)
and Dr. Richard Jones. Take the latter’s state-
ments. They are the same as those made by a num-
ber of medical gentlemen, but they do not help
much. Dr. Jones, in laying down the conditions
for successful residence in the Territory, says a man
must be constitutionally strong and be in perfect
health. His habits must be perfect; he must be
temperate and moral. In other words, he must be a
picked man. The question is, can we pick enough
men to people the Territory, and find them sufficient
inducement to go up there under the present condi-
tions? It is very good to say that you must have
perfect hygienic conditions and physically perfect
men, but that does not touch the very serious
economic questions which have to be considered.
After you have admitted all that, the great problem
has still to be solved, and I hope that by concentrat-
ing the united ability and patriotism of the nation
on it we may find the solution, and that speedily.”
Now, The Bulletin has no desire to be
unpleasant to any man with an insufficient
majority, but it is dead weary of this mas-
sive way of putting the case. The habit
of using elephantine language about the
Territory merely obscures the issue. Heavy
people discuss it as if it were Sheol, or the
Decalogue, or the Great Pyramid, or a per-
fectly new disease, or the remains of the
Holy Roman Empire, and as if none of the
old rules or precedents applied to it. In
other words, they partly disguise their in-
capacity or laziness by pretending that there
is a mystery where there is none, just as the
Knight of the Round Table, when he came
home badly licked, might explain his defeat
by stating that the enemy was 16ft. high and
had a habit of breathing flames from his
stomach.
The “problem” of the Territory was once
the problem of all Australia. It is still the
problem of Northern Westralia, and in a
much less degree of Northern and Central
Queensland, and in a very big degree of more
than half of New South Wales —the half
which, by reason of the Sydney congestion
policy, is steadily losing population. It is
furthermore the problem of a part of Vic-
toria—the part which is losing population by
reason of the Melbourne congestion policy.
The name of the trouble is Partial or Com-
plete Emptiness, and the remedy is to grow
or acquire more good white people to occupy
the country, and to give them the necessary
facilities for getting there. The case may
be set out in a handful of propositions:—
(1) Where a country contains only 11- persons
per square mile, a good many square miles are sure
to be vacant.
(2) As the population increases the number of
vacant square miles is liable to diminish.
(3) As few people walk when they can go by
train, settlement follows the lines of railroad when-
ever possible, provided there is a reserve of popula-
tion at one end of the railroad and good available
land beside it or at the other end; and assuming
that the railway management is sane and the land
system isn’t quite mad. This tendency is increased
by the fact that when there is a reserve of popula-
tion at one end of the line there are generally
facilities for the sale or export of produce. As the
settler doesn’t grow produce out of sheer cussedness
or lack of faith in a Hereafter, this outlet is im-
portant.
(4) If a railway starts where there is hardly any-
body and ends where there is nobody, it does little
good for settlement for lack of people to settle, and
because the complete Robinson Crusoe act, under-
taken for pure love of RoBiNSON-ing, appeals to but
few.
(5) When there is no railway at all, things are
apt to be a darned sight worse than as specified in
paragraph 4. This fact has also been hinted at in
paragraph 1.
(6) Putting the case into a condensed form, the
two things which retard settlement are want of
facilities to bring people and lack of the pressure
of great crowds behind to shove people.
And these simple propositions ought to be
pasted in the hat.
* * * *
Australia started to solve the problem of
the North according to these same simple
propositions. There were hitches and breaks
and intervals and discrepancies, but the gen-
eral policy was sound and steady. Settle-
ment began at the south-east corner of the
continent, because the dangerous northern
seas were too tangled for the helpless old
sailing ships, and the Barrier Reef was a
huge difficulty. Then, probably by instinct
or accident, rather than by close reasoning,
the pioneers began to spread northward, ac-
cording to certain logical principles. They
went step by step, except when they made
mistakes. The first attempt to create a
colony of North Australia and to establish
an isolated settlement at Gladstone failed,
probably because the proposed capital was
too long a stride from the old centres. It
was even a worse failure than the still more
isolated Port Darwin. But settlements
were afterwards formed one by one at mod-
erate intervals, and as each was furnished
with its little bit of railway to connect it
with the West, it began to look as if these
places had come to stay. Then the main line
of railway which connected the North with
the South was steadily pushed ahead, and
the new settlements took firmer root, and
became nailed down and fixed. So far as
swift and regular and fairly cheap means of
communication were provided it proved that
the great problem of the North was no
problem at all. The old thing simply solved
itself as it went along. And even some dis-
tance ahead of the main railway the problem
—though the term wasn’t invented then-
solved itself on the strength of the assurance
that better means of communication were
coming. There was no special search for
settlers who were “constitutionally strong
and in perfect health,” “picked men,” “tem-
perate and moral,” and of almost super-
human, not to say heavenly, habits. Nobody
hunted for demi-gods to populate Rockhamp-
ton and Townsville and pther places. Even
at Bowen feathers and a harp weren’t in-
sisted upon. Any decent sort of cove who
wanted to go there just went there, and with
reasonable facilities there was no serious
lack of coves.
* * * *
Of course there are failures —or rather
places where success is postponed. The
scrap of line which extends inland from
Cooktown hasn’t greatly boomed Cooktown
as yet. It is so very far away from any
prospect of connection with the big centres
which have population to spare that its
boom is necessarily hung up. The isolated
bit of railway between Normanton and
Croydon is an even worse case of a lone-
some fragment of civilisation crying in the
wilderness. The Darwin-Pine Creek road is
just as bad. These are the lines which, sd
far, lead from almost nobody to hardly any-
body, and they do that because it is hardly
worth anybody’s while to go there while
there are so many more accessible places
fpr somebody to go to. They are as bad as,
but not much worse than, a certain isolated
line which N.S.W. once possessed in its
Northern Rivers , country—an enterprise
wind! pervaded a rich locality, yet for lack of
a definite purpose hardly paid for axle-grease,
Incidentally, the really great problem of the
north is not the Territory but the northern
section of Westralia, which, by reason of
similar causes, is the Territory over again,
but without many of its natural advantages,
Politically the North is divided into three
barts - Queensland is showing how to do it.
The Territory and Northern Westralia are
showing how not_ to do it. The one State
winch has done it to a fair extent simply
pushed its railways north, did its best in the
way of joining the places which wanted
people with the places which had a few
people to spare, then let things adjust them-
selves without talking about the “problem.”
it Queensland had started its trunk railway
from the far end southward instead of from
Lrisbane northward, it might have made the
same ha.sh of its chances as its next-door
neighbor has done. The work would have
been for many years an unpayable thing
without a purpose. The futile globe-trotter
would have landed for an hour or so at,
perhaps, Cairns, as_ he now does at Port
Darwin, and have mistaken the place for all
Queensland as he now mistakes Darwin for
the whole _ Northern Territory, and would
nave said in haste—;as David of Israel put
the matter—that it was an impossible sort
°t spot. Then there would have been the
® ame sort of discussion about this section of
jme North as there is about the, one next
door, and the same foolish talk about the
need or finding some immaculate settler
with wings and a golden crown to take up
land there unless the place was to be aban-
doned as hopeless. Seeing that all the sue-
cess which Australia has achieved with its
iNorth has been done by making the attack
rrom_ the comparatively well-peopled South,
it might be well to stick to the old habits,
and invade the Territory from the south
also at the Adelaide end, where the hiffh,
cooi. lairly-well-watered tableland is. On
these methods, as shown by Queensland’s
experience, a Demi-god isn’t essential as
pioneer. The work can be done in some
sort ot fashion by a Cove.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Weak Navy Insanity.
From “The Old Soldier”
thp Dr fnli ßT 3 Uß h ?5- bee " bleating afresh, I see, about
the folly of anything but a species of semi-Defence
Austra f ha ' how would Australia
stand, in the event of a European war, if its navy
were weak? What security would it have against a
cruising, commerce-destroying war? The Arthur
type of strategist will answer that the British navy
is supreme, has always protected Australia, and wiil
F’rTvn'Lo 0 S °h> Ye , t , 1 ?° years a &° did not protect
r LINDERS, with all his charts and journals, from
capture and a long imprisonment at Mauritius. More
tnan that, the British navy was practically supreme
m the seas round the British Islands during the
almost continuous series of wars from 1688 to 1814
and yet it could not save merchant ships in the
Channel and the North Sea from capture by hun-
dreds. A writer in Blackwood says of one period:
During the war from 1688 to 1697, the privateers
ot St. Malo alone claimed to have taken 162 war-
ships and 3384 merchantmen of one sort and another
—an average of more than a merchantman a day for
that one town for nine years.”
Not only single ships were taken, but sometimes
large convoys with some of the warships escorting
them. Here is an instance:—-
“In May, 1707, a convoy of 50 merchant ships
sailed from the Downs escorted by the Royal Oak, of
74 guns, and the Grafton and Hampton Court, both
of 70 guns. The French captain, Forbin, was wait-
ing for them at Dunkirk with three ships of over
50 guns each, six of over 40 guns each, and four
corvettes. Borbin came up with the convoy off
Brighton. The captains of the Grafton and Hamp-
ton Court were killed before their ships were sur-
rendered.”
The latter ship had 200 of her crew killed and
wounded, and 22 of the convoy were taken. In
October of the same year what was called the Portu-
gal fleet sailed with a powerful escort, consisting of
two 80-gun ships, a 74, and two 50’s. This was what
happened:—
“Forbin and Duguay-Trouin swooped down on the
convoy. The former had eight of the nine large
ships with which he had taken tge Grafton and
Hampton Court in May. DuguayArouin led out
from Brest a 72, a 64, two 50’s, and two smaller
shins of over 30 guns. The result was inevitable.
There was a tremendous fight in the Channel. The
Devonshire (80 guns) took fire and blew up with
most of her 700 men. The Cumberland, the other
80, was taken, as were the two 50’s, the Chester and
Ruby, and with them were taken, according to a
reliable account, 30 sail of merchantmen.”
Later in the century the British fleet blockaded
the regular French fleets in Brest and Toulon, but
was almost powerless to do more than check the
depredations of the privateers which issued in swarms
from St. Malo, Dunkirk, and a score or so of other
ports. What if we find ourselves suffering from the
same sort of nuisance while the British Dreadnought
fleets are making demonstrations along the German
North Sea coast, and we have only our two cruisers,
and some destroyers, and Maoriland has nothing to
protect our trade against commerce destroyers? A
strong local navy is going to be Australia’s only
salvation in the future.
British Socialism and Australian
Security.
The following London cables appeared in
juxtaposition in the local dailies recently:—
The rank and file of the Liberals are much stirred
by the antagonism which has risen between Mr.
Winston Churchill, First Lord of the Admiralty,
and Mr. Lloyd George, Chancellor of the Exchequer,
on the question of naval construction. They fear
an open break between the two Ministers over the
navy estimates. The Liberal press supports Mr.
Lloyd George.
The British Socialist party has decided to run
candidates for a large number of Scottish constitu-
encies during the next elections.'
There is a sort of relation between the two
messages; and together they have a power-
ful bearing on the Australian defence ques-
tion. One of the features of British politics
to-day is the growing strength of the Social-
ist party. The social reform and land pro-
posals of Lloyd George are impelling dukes
and the like to sell, or try to sell,
estates that their ancestors got dirt
cheap in the dim, individualistic past.
Anti-Sosh leagues are formed; conserva-
tive leader-writers blither; butlers, city
clerks, parsons, elderly ladies, parlor maids,
bishops, superannuated members of both
Services, and the genteel classes, generally,
demand in a stupefied manner why Provi-
dence doesn’t interfere and check the in-
famy. Yet Socialism goes steadily ahead.
The well-to-do are taking to it, in many cases,
as warmly as the indigent. Admittedly one
Z >St Socialistic measures cf
lecent times was the British Insurance Act.
Speaking as an employer,” said Sir
K™1 LEVE £ ! he millionaire soap manu
tacturer, to a Sydney paper, last week, ‘‘l
can say that the Insurance Act is a beneficent
measure, and a success in every respect.”
In tact, the long overdue social reform move-
ment has definitely arrived in the United
Kingdom. In order to achieve the further
instalment of Socialistic legislation for which
most thinking Britons employers as well as
employees—consider the Time is now Bine it
will be necessary for the nation to return’to
I arliament more men of advanced ideas than
it has in the past. Now, the British Radical
is, almost invariably, a sort of anti-defencite.
Also he is a sort of anti-imperialist. That is
to say, he deprecates the blewing of an un-
necessarily large sum on the fleet, and he is
opposed to the profitless bloating of the Em-
pire, on the grounds that these things cost
money which could better be spent on the
amelioration of the lot of Britain’s half-
starved underworld. It is as certain as any-
thing can be that if the British Socialist, or
any other Party, runs candidates at the next
elections, in Scotland or elsewhere, on the
social reform ticket, a number of them will
win. Incidentally, every Parliamentary vote
for more Socialism must mean a vote for de-
creased naval estimates. Apart from the fact
that pacificism is the traditional policy of the
Radical wing in the United Kingdom, there is
the further circumstance that even if the in-
evitable advanced Radical, or Labor, or
Socialist Government of the future aspired
after a big navy, it couldn’t enjoy that luxury,
and any measure of social reform worth talk-
ing about, at the same time. The finances
simply wouldn’t run to it. There is another
excellent reason why a British Labor Ad-
ministration would turn down the all-power-
ful variety of fleet that successive Liberal
Administrations have stood for steadfastly,
despite the fulminations of the Lloyd
Geoeges in their midst, and of the bigger
section of the party press. The British Liberal
Party, as at present constituted, is really a
Whig Party. Its vested interests are as consid-
erable. as those of the Tories, and many of
those interests are situated in the Dominions
overseas. The Lever firm purchased a pro-
perty in M.L. only last week, and other big
Liberal firms and individuals are doing like-
wise. every day of the year. The first
British Labor Government will not be repre-
sentative of people who possess assets in
Australia or S’Africa or Canada. It will
hold office by virtue of the suffrages of folks
to whom it would not matter materially if
“the colonies” were wiped off the face of the
earth. Australia’s present position is com-
fortingly secure. An Asiatic or other inva-
sion is unlikely, if only from the fact that,
even assuming an army corps of Japs or
Chqws effected a landing here, it would
want something in the way of overseas sup-
plies ; and those supplies would never arrive
with the British fleet at its present strength,
and the directors of that fleet keenly alive to
the necessity of “preserving the Empire in-
tact” —or, to put it in plain English, alive to
the necessity of “preserving their national in-
terests in these regions.” Unless, of course, a
big European Power selected the moment of
attack for doing something that would keep
Bull’s ships busy in Bull waters, Australia
can drift along more or less safely with a
weak fleet for the present—say for the next
five or six years. But from the moment the in-
evitable occurs, and the Socialist or Labor
Government invades the Treasury benches at
Westminster, the Commonwealth will have
as much hope of living, in a national sense —
assuming it is still, as at present, virtually
undefended—as Daniel would have had in
the lions’ den if the lions hadn’t been squared
or induced to run stiff. Once in the long ago
an English Parliament decided that its for-
eign dominions were too expensive and also
gave it the tired feeling, and that sort of
event might happen again.
—
A Wrong Way with Trees, and a Rotten
Way with Convicts.
“Lex” holdeth forth :
Touching the question of re-afforestation by prison
labor, 1 see (B. 8/l/’l4) that one “Norbell” has
appeared voluntarily for the defence in the case of
Myself x. the Holman Government. Defendants
harve many grievous faults, but I question whether
they deserved the disastrous advocacy of “Norbell,”
who is all there in the matter of cheap sentimen-
tality, bellowdrama, misguided enthusiasm, and so
on, but who in the urgent matter of knowledge of
his subject is several miles over the fence. For
example, he berates me for my approval of the
“separate. treatment” system, and quotes, in support
of his views, an impressive piece from Charles
Dickens on the evils and horrors of “solitary con-
finement.” Every criminologist, every lawyer, in
fact every citizen who has been through a gaol, appre-
ciates the radical difference between the separate
treatment system and solitary confinement. The
latter may be said to be practically non-existent in
N. S. Wales to-day. Separate treatment signifies
merely an arrangement under which the fallen sheep
are kept apart as much as possible from the criminal
goats. Thus an entirely different class of offender is
sent to Bathurst from the sort that goes to Parra-
matta. Most of the latter are hopeless, or very
nearly so; many of the Bathurst eonfinees are con-
sidered to have a good chance of being reformed into
decent citizens. And in all the gaols the apparently
repentant are kept out of touch, as much as possible,
with the palpably incorrigible. But otherwise there
is very little solitariness about modern prison life.
The men work together, exercise ' together, drill toge-
ther and go to church together. Certainly they
sleep in separate cells ; but as these are well-iighted,
during those hours when healthy, hardworking men
desire to keep awake, and as library books are
allowed in them, the fact is not regarded by those
concerned as a hardship.
“Norbell” betrays his edifying lack of famili-
arity with the prisons of N. S. Wales by one of
those minor touches which signify so much. “Does
a prisoner,” he asks, “in the horror-haunted confines
of Darlinghurst ever learn a useful trade?” Cer-
tainly not. Neither does he in the horror-haunted
confines of the Black Maria. Darlinghurst is not,
and has not been for many years, a gaol in the
proper acceptation of the term. It is merely a
clearing-house for criminals—a place where people
wait to be tried, executed or released on payment of
costs or what not incurred in bankruptcy, and such-
like proceedings. In the by no means horror-haunted
confines of gaols such as Parramatta, Goulburn,
Bathurst and Maitland, prisoners certainly do learn
useful trades. Moreover, the official figures go to
show that the majority of the men take to those
trades on their release, and tyereafter maintain
themselves and their families by them; or, at any
rate, it is fairly to be assumed that they do, inas-
much as but a trifling percentage of prisoners return
to gaol after having once left it.
The chief and most obvious objection to re-affores-
tation by prison labor consists in the fact that, unless
the experiment is conducted on a large scale, it
involves the association, on familiar terms, of crimi-
nals of all grades. This may be pleasanter for some
of the criminals, but it is demonstrably bad for
them in the mass, and more particularly is it bad for
the better sort of prisoner. The inevitable effect is
for the stronger individualities—who arc usually the


most desperate offenders —to dominate the weaker
ones, and so cause crime to be glorified ; a tendency
that the prison reformer has to fight every day of
his life. You can separate the good from the bad,
and the weak from the strong more or less effectually
within the confines of a prison; it is utterly im-
possible to do it in a forest. A second objection to
the M.L. system lies in the fact that a criminal who
has served his time tree-planting emerges on the
world helpless. Society, as at present constituted,
has no place for the expert tree-planter. The result
in N. S. Wales must be—once the Hodman Govern-
ment’s disastrous scheme gets into good going order
—that year by year a body of law-breakers will be
unloosed on the community, with no remunerative
trades to fall back upon ; a development which will
be exceedingly unpleasant for the community, as the
experience of M.L. goes to show.
If “Norbell” is under the delusion that M.L.’s
afforestation system is a success because it has stood
for 15 years, he needs to forget that error, just as
he does his errors in re Darlinghurst, etc. Maoriland’s
notoriously uneconomical, and from the prisoner s
viewpoint ethically pernicious, scheme has been per-
sisted in for the reason that so many other foolish-
nesses are persisted in there and elsewhere —because,
namely, it is an institution which appeals to the
uninstructed and the ultra-sentimental, and which,
under the party government system, is accordingly a
perilous thing to shift. Every student of prison
questions in M.L. knows that the arrangement is
rotten; and that it had been turned down by the
rest of the world, after due examination of it, with
the utmost unanimity, till a N. S. Wales Labor
Government took it up. Re-afforestation, as Norway,
Sweden, Germany and a dozen other big countries
have shown, is as much a matter for State
enterprise as water conservation, defence, and
so forth. But once a country gets busy with
a scheme like convict afforestation, which, at
vast expense in the way ,of time and monev,
gives a surface appearance of paying its
way, while making things a little brighter for
wrongdoers, an army of “Norbells” immediately
comes into existence and barracks blindly for its
continuanae. There is a type of well-meaning idiot
who would cheerfully advocate the advisability of
continuing to employ convicts at the job of trout-
fishing on the Snowy River, assuming such a
demented system were ever inaugurated. It is this
dreary fact which makes it necessary for patriotic
Australians to assail vigorously the Holman Admini-
stration’s aspiration to give the criminal classes —
to their own ultimate detriment —a good time at
the exncnse of the section of the community that
obeys the laws.
As in the Days of Herod.
Dear Bulletin, —Tasmania’s population,
despite the fact that it can’t get away
without crossing the water, still continues
to take the risk of sea-sickness. For the
nine months ending September last (figures
for the last quarter are not yet available)
the natural increase was 2671, but the excess
of departures over arrivals by sea was 4268.
The dead loss for the first nine months of
the year was thus 1597. As there is always
a big influx of summer trippers about the
end of the year, it is estimated that the
fiaal figures to December 31 will show a
total gain of 532. If these estimates are
realised, the population will reach 201,000
—about one-third of Sydney’s total. But as
the summer visitors all depart from the
Speck during the first quarter of the year,
the end of March will see the figures slump
again to about 197,000. Luckily for the
Tas. Treasury the Federal 255. per
capita is paid on the population as at Decem-
ber 31, when the population is always 4000
or 5000 better than in the winter. The
local birth-rate is the highest in the Com-
monwealth; yet the average excess of emi-
gration over immigration during the past
15 years has just about nullified the baby
surplus, and seems likely to keep on nulli-
fying it, for these reasons: —
(1) The scale of wages is lower t-han in other
States.
(2) The Income Tax exemption is lower. (In-
comes of £lOO a year are subject to tax.)
(3) The cost of living is high.
Wages are low because of the unfortunate
tendency on the part of the Government and
private employers to regard low wages as
the basis of prosperity, and because. indus-
trial organisation for higher wages is only
in its infaney. Taxation is high because
the country has borrowed so largely for non-
revenue producing purposes that its interest
bill now amounts to nearly half its total
annual revenue, wherefore it cannot afford
to let even a £2 per week laborer escape
the drag-net of direct taxation. And the
cost of living is high because the country
doesn’t grow or manufacture sufficient to
feed and clothe itself, and has to pay high
freights to the steamship monopoly for beef
grown and goods manufactured elsewhere;
moreover, in the cities rents are ruinous be-
cause of the drift of the population from
the decayed villages of the interior. Immi-
grants fail to arrive from overseas in any
appreciable numbers, or to stay in the coun-
try if they do happen to arrive, for the same
reasons that propel the native-born into
exile. When a fertile country like Tas-
mania, with no manufactures worth men-
tioning, and whose biggest city isn’t 40,000
strong, finds itself with only one primary
producer to the square mile, it is surely time
for the application of heroic measures. But
the Solomon Government doesn’t even pre-
tend to view the almost stationary popula-
tion with anything approaching alarm. No
effort is being made to attract either labor
or capital to the island ; no effort is made
to stop the exodus of the working
population. The State’s 450 miles of de-
crepit railways meander through thousands
of acres of fertile land devoted to sheep and
rabbits and briar-bushes at a dead loss of
nearly £70,000 per annum, and hardly any
attempt is made to convert that freightless
territory into a paying asset by means of a
thumping land tax. The old policy of com-
nelling the genuine land-worker to settle in
inaccessible regions, far from rivers and rail-
ways and roads, is being continued ; and to
provide these settlers with indifferent roads
and worse schools the Government is spend-
ing nearly twice as much as it gets for the
land. The mongy representing the annual
loss is balanced by an annual loan, which
increases the public debt and the interest
bill without providing a means of paying
either. Labor suggested that the recent
windfall from the Commonwealth —£900,000
spread over ten years—should be earmarked
■for the purchase of hydro-electric works,
the establishing of State saw-mills, and the
proposed Ouse Valley irrigation, railway
and closer settlement scheme. But, instead
of doing anything so sensible, Solomon’s
Government is spending the money in mere
sand-shifting projects calculated to pay no
dividends with the utmost regularity for the
next quarter century, at least; and by that
time most of the sand will have drifted back
into the toy. canals, and the worm that dieth
not will have eaten the bridges and the crazy
little jetties, and the macadam of the high-
ways will have been pulverised by the traffic
which high rail\yay freight is even now
transferring from the railways to the roads.
Then the loan evil will break out in a fresh
place, for the joint and several ver-
tebrae of the districts affected will rise up
as one valley of dry backbones and demand
that the vanished assets shall be re-built by
forging the name of Posterity on an lOU.
And pretty soon after that the foreign in-
vestor will discover that the alarming
growth of the public debt and interest
charges has driven all possible parents out
of the State, and the lOU forged on Pos-
terity will be valueless, for there will be no
Posterity worth mentioning. Then, pre-
sumably, Tasmania will be seized for debt
and the Commonwealth will have to find the
money to get rid of the bailiff, and the
Speck’s name as a sovereign State will be
Mudj For persistently heading towards
this brand of perdition the Speck’s rulers
deserve to be classed as first-class lunatics ;
while for handing over to such rulers a free
gift of £900,000 without imposing stringent
conditions as to how the money should be
spent, the Commonwealth Parliament lias
shown itself an over-indulgent parent and
fully entitled to the usual reward thereof.
A wiser organisation would have put the
£900,000 in the hands of public trustees, to
accumulate at compound interest until it
overtook and obliterated the Tasmanian
debt, and thus forced the isle into doing a
clean-up. But alas ! there are so very few
wiser organisations.—Yours, etc., John D.
History as She Is Wrote.
“Locust” discusses Parkes and a
panegyrist:—
For an example of history garbled to bolster up
the posthumous reputation of one man, commend
me to B. R. Wise’s “Making of the Australian Com-
monwealth,” wherein the old, haphazard Parkes
has his Special pleader. For years we have had
Parkes accepted as the leader of the movement for
free, secular and compulsory education, although
he 'fought it in the unscrupulous bandit fashion
which was charaeteristic of him in Opposition, and
finally only accepted it under the pressure of James
Greenwood and George Dibbs. Yet the Bashi-
Bazouk fighting which Parkes put in against educa-
tion was tame and uneventful compared with the
bitter opposition he made to Federation. He opposed
every movement for the union of these States — every
movement—until the subject was forced on him, as
the education movement was, and ever so many other
movements.
Parkes had no constructive power. That was one
of his defects, and there were 99 others. He could
copy, but his disposition was to resist; and so he
was the most formidable and most unscrupulous
Opposition leader we have ever seen. There were two
reasons why he should have opposed every practical
movement for Federation. One was narrowness of mind.
The other was the knowledge that he couldn’t sup-
port Federation without being expected, unless he
was prepared to take a back seat, to draft a scheme,
and when a big question was on hand he could no
more draft a workable and statesmanlike scheme
than a gorilla could manufacture an umbrella. He
never felt very deeply about any great public ques-
tion ; in fact, his line was small questions, loud
noises and horribly bad poems.
The Federal Council existed. It had not much
power, certainly, but it was a beginning, and was
a huge step when one reflects upon the isolation and
jealousy of the States at that period, and remembers
that it was before the upheaval of Labor democracy
in 1891. The Council only needed the adhesion of
N. S. Wales to make it the jumping-off place for
something bigger. Gillies, on behalf of the other
States, offered Parkes (1) the leadership of the
bigger movement, (2) an alteration of the Constitu-
tion of the Federal Council to suit N.S.W., (3) a
free hand in proposals for defence, and the settle-
ment of the Westralian question, and (1) an exten-
sion of the functions of the Council “to consider
and formulate the proposals of the larger Federa-
tion.” Parkes, the critic, opponent, opportunist
and writer of bad poems, refused the offer, and
thereby stopped the clock for 10 years.
Gillies wrote (Wise, p. 16) : “Now, why should
you not join us to do this great worlr? What is
the difficulty? Surely it would be a worthy ambi-
tion for you to adopt the best mearns at your dis-
posal—in fact, at your hand—to unite Australia in
a Federation. .. .No one at present can do the work
but you. You can remove the Federation barrier
which has been created by the isolation of N. S.
Wales from all the other colonies in the Federal
movement. .. .What you refuse to-day someone else
will do to-morrow, and I should be pleased to see
you take the pride of place.” In face of this, Wise
talks of the “unwillingness of Gillies and the other
Premiers to co-operate with Parkes,” and says it
was due to “a suspicion of his motives in proposing
to supersede the Federal Council.” But Wise “gives
the show away” in this sentence; “The inconsist-
ency of his [Parkes’s] present attitude was attri-
buted to a vainglorious unwillingness to take part
in any federal movement of which he was not him-
self the leader.” This is a perfectly accurate sum-
ming-up of Parkes’s attitude in regard to every
great public question throughout his career in
politics, and most conspicuously in regard to three —
(1) one-man-one-vote (pioneered by W. H. Traill),
(2) Education (pioneered by the Rev. James Green-
wood), and (3) Federation. Wise says: “Echoes of
these charges may be heard even at the present
time.” And they will be heard as more than echoes
when history is written, not garbled.
There are so many other inaccuracies in Wise’s
book that it would take another volume to correct
them. For instance, there is his account of Parkes’s
retirement in 1891, when he left “hoffis” in order
to curry favor with the employers (newly combined
for the first time), and threw over his faithful fol-
lower, Sid Smith, and the eight-hour clause of the
Mines Bill, at the behest of McMillan. According
to Wise, this was a noble, positively-heroic, piece
of self-abnegation on Parkes’s part. He forgets
that those in the House knew that Parkes, after
opposing William Henry Traill’s one-man-one-
vote proposals, characteristically stole it and put
it in his programme to catch the Labor vote in the
new House of 1891, when " Labor came in for the
first time in numbers; and, after playing with the
Labor party for nine months, he went out of office
on a Labor measure introduced by his own Minister
of Mines (which he must have acquiesced in in
Cabinet) in order to “dish” the Labor party.
Wise’s piece of special pleading should not be
allowed ot go without protest, nor his garbling of
recent history to pass without correction. Neither
his nor the other orthodox writers’ attempts to
create a fictitious historical personage of Parkes
by crediting him with the merit of everything he
opposed till it was forced on him will in' the end
succeed. He was not a statesman; he was merely
a very cunning politician. He never pioneered a
great measure, but opposed everything of import-
ance till the pressure of the stream was too power-
ful, and then he turned round and went with the
stream, and claimed credit for his wonderful merit
in doing so.
The Language Test, and What It Is Used
For.
The good old days when Australia was re-
garded as a Heaven-sent dumping-ground for
alcoholic wrecks, consumptives and similar
rejects from other countries are departing
with great thoroughness. Thanks to the
efforts of the great Liberal dailies, there is an
extensive popular notion that the Immigration
Restriction Acts were designed for the specific
purpose of turning back valuable additions to
our national brain and brawn, by the simple
process of requiring, say, a Lithuanian, to
translate 50 words of Irish, or Swiss-Aus-
trian, or some other European language. The
Socialistic Tiger is supposed to utter glad
roars, and to wag his tail with delight, as the
rejected immigrant gathers up his vast and
sturdy family and retires from our Great
Waste Spaces. As a matter of actual fact,
so far as white people are concerned, the
language test is used only when there is no
other legal method of keeping out a morally
or physically undesirable newcomer. The
principal use of the Acts is for excluding the
physically unfit, who will either become a
charge on State or municipal funds, or will
introduce loathsome or contagious disease.
Almost every big batch of immigrants is
found to contain some undesirables of this
class, and although better arrangements for
medical examination before departure from
Europe will ultimately reduce the proportion
verv greatly, hundreds of persons suffering
from syphilis, advanced consumption and
other contagious diseases must have been
rejected at Australian ports during the last
few years. A considerable assortment of
lunatics, alcoholics and other degenerates
have also been passed out in the same way.
The examination is not confined to the steer-
age, and not a few undesirables have been
found amongst the class who wear boiled
shirts at the evening gorge when the sea is
not too rough. The beastly rudeness of the
“Colonials” was deeply resented at first by
the more brassbound variety of ship’s doctor;
but a thumping penalty for any attempt to
evade inquiry by the Federal Health officers
has cleared the marine atmosphere. Conse-
quently, the Hon. Coukcy Howard’s medi-
cal record for the voyage receives the same
attention as that of Bile Jones in the
steerage. A just and sensible arrangement
allows for a bond in doubtful or difficult
cases, under which the newcomer may land
conditionally on undergoing treatment and
reporting for an official medical examination
at a later date. The rejected partv also has
a second run for his money at the port to
which he is actually booked (the principal
examination is made at the ship’s first port
of entry), and if the second doctor passes
him he gets in. Elaborate arrangements
are being made for medically examining im-
migrants before their departure from the
Cold Country, in order that all reasonable
chance of hardship from medical rejection
may be avoided. But even then the Aus-
tralian system will provide for an open door
compared with the requirements for admis-
sion to the United States or Canada.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Reid again harped upon the tendency of
the people to concentrate in the large industrial
centres. “Australia,” he said, “was eclipsed by the
great cities.” There is no need to be alarmed about
that.... Certainly the growth of the cities does not
and cannot prejudice the development of the interior.
It is the other way about, because every man earning
a living in the city helps to employ another in the
country producing things for his support, and thus
makes the path of the pioneer so much easier.
Pioneering work can go on in the middle of the
town as well as in the bush.
—Slab of Wisdom from Sydney D.T.
Again the glad New Year and the buzzy
season of flies, c amerados.
Again the day of the Glad Hand and the
Glowing Eye, and of “the tendency to
concentrate in the large industrial
centres.”
Again the day, and the night, of the Long
Beer and the Short Cash and the
Medium Pleasure therewith.
Again the daily newspapers of the City of
Ma are desperately put to it for live
“copy,” and the wherewithal to clothe
Aunt ’Tildagrapfi and Grandma in
attractive garments before the world !
Crises in Uncle Samland and Germany,
prices in Loandon, vices in Poland and
Peru, ices in the Southern Ocean or the
Dago shops—all furnisli forth food for
the linotype and the rhinotype.
The possibility of sewage in the gigantic
freshwater lakes found at Canberra, or
in the imagination of the Canberra-
tionist papers, is thrown at an agonised
public which is praying for rain, and to
be kept far from the threatened drought,
loan-epidemic, beer-strike and general
Slump of Things !
Aunt ’Tilda asks foamingly each morn-
ing, “What do we Believe?” and regi-
ments of emaciated amateur controver-
sialists rise up and deploy in her
columns.
By-the-way, what DO we believe, came-
radosf
Do we believe that the men and women of
1814 were The Pioneers?
Or do we believe that the men and women
of 1914 are the Pioneers?
The men and women of 1814 discovered
things ; the men and women of 1914 are
to develop things.
George Reid declares that Australia is
eclipsed by the great cities.
I, Oliver Cromwell Cabot, declare that
Australia is eclipsed by the Great Bor-
rowing Policy of the great cities!
We cannot see Australia for the Bloated
Capitals; we cannot discern the Com-
monwealth for the flocks of Loans.
If a thing is not near Melbourne, it is no
good; if a thing is not near Sydney, it
is no d good ; if it has not been
built with Borrowed Money it is no
d good at all.
This is the Gospel of Hopeless Persons in
1914—the Persons who tell us that “the
growth of the great cities does not pre-
judice the development of the interior.”
What do we Believe, camerados? Do we
believe that?
If we cannot see the wood for the trees ; if
we cannot see Australia for the Big and
Bloated Capitals; what shall we do?
Let us get us an Axe—and a Big Axe,
camerados!
Let us hit the Hopeless Persons and the
Professional Bloaters in the neck; let
us chop the trees a bit, so as to let the
wood he seen!
Let us also get us also a Gun- —and a good
Gun, camerados!
Let us shoot some of the flocks of Loans, and
stick the Great Borrowing Policy up
against a wall!
The Pioneers of 1814 did not foresee the
Piowneers of 1914, believe me.
The arrival of that great plOUneer, Dago-
vich Toopopulos, who opened up the
vast Steakadeoyst Scrub in the City of
Ma was not contemplated by the Pio-
neers of 1814.
The heroic sacrifices of Patrick O’Noses,
the immortal plOUneer of Flemington,
were never dreamed of by the pioneers
of 1814.
No, no!
The founding of our glorious pawnitall in-
dustry, by the famous Brothers Isaac-
stein —possibly our grandest plOU-
neers—was a develonment of 1914 that
must have paralysed the pioneers of a
hundred years ago!
Yes, yes!
George Reid himself could never have anti-
cinated the intrepidity of our present
plOUneers — No-Yes-No !
Again, the pioi/neers of the great Agent-
General-and-Staff-Attached-Persons-Han-
gers-on-and-Do-little Industry in Loan-
don will claim their meed of recogni-
tion among out present-day plOUneers.
But what do we Believe?
Do we believe in it, or do we not, camerados?
Aunt ’Tilda clearly thinks we ought to,
because she says that “every man earn-
ing a living in the city... .makes the
path of the pioneer so much easier.”
It would, earner ados ; oh, it would!
Let us bloat, swell, distend, expand and—
hurst!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
Sentimentality and the Criminal.
Randolph Bedford’s reflections :
A recent Bulletin par. which says: “One of the
inscrutable mysteries of woman’s character came out
in the case of Mrs. Newman,” might justifiably go
further and include man in the description. Mrs.
Newman could kill babies for gain and yet be Flor-
ence Nightingale without Florence’s theatrical-
ism. Mrs. Ivnorr’s business was baby-killing for
fees, miscalled “bonuses,” of £lO and upwards; yet
Mrs. Knorr, outside cof her business, was as kindly
and sympathetic and motherly a murderess as ever
wept over the deaths of Little Willie and Madame
Vine. Similarly, Deeming, to people who judged
superficially the new acquaintance—and they are
greatly in the majority—was a very pleasant, kindly
and tactful companion. After all, our horror is not
for crime, but for its manner; and in the ratio of
the secrecy and treachery of its commission, so is
the horror of it. It is for that reason that the
cowardly and secret murder of children seems to ex-
clude all humaneness from the perpetrator, so that
the discovery that the murderess was addicted to
porridge or some such other commonplace strikes
us as being at the least improbable.
Such an egoist as the natural criminal must be
in order to be a criminal, excludes his own specialty
from the list of crimes. The old lecher after youth
squares his crime with the honor that pays 20s. in
the pound; the dealer in adulterated groceries can
point to a long and vegetably-moral life in extenua-
tion of salicylates in the tomato sauce; and every
commercial and speculative criminal secures absolu-
tion —not only from himself, but from the commun-
ity—with the sacrament of success, just as every
failure can prove by his failure his morality. One
proves his ‘‘honor” by the evidence of his money;
the other proves his “honor” by the testimony of
his poverty Yet outside of this specialisation and
this “squaring” of the one inherent vice, all these
people may be quite human. Kindliness, the nursing
instinct, the “ministering angel thou when pain and
sickness wring the brow” are as characteristic of any
but the most perverted and ferocious criminals as
they are of the prostitute, many of them, like Trilby,
“with all the virtues but one.”
Says the best of Macbeth's cut-throats: —
“I am one, my lord,
Whom the vile blows and buffets of the world
Have so incens'd that I am reckless what I do
To spite the world.”
These are not criminals in the real sense, because
they frankly know what they do. One true criminal
egoist, whom 1 knew, arrested red-handed for murder,
when asked in gaol, “What brought you here?”
answered, not “Murder!” but “Bad luck.” And who
will decry the honesty of perversion of the cashier
who had stolen regularly his £5OOO a year for five
years, and recoiled in horror from the spectacle of
the office boy in manipulating Is. 9d. of the petty
cash ?
Many years ago, as an entering clerk in a dry-
goods warehouse of the ripe age of 16, and at a
salary of £lO a month, I walked through the old
George-street Markets on a first of September, and
saw masses of native rose and flapnel-flower and an
advertisement of a Manly flower show. In that
moment the Spring raged in my blood and the bush
cried out to me to hate the city. At that busy time
—which is in the Rag Trade equivalent to the Sack
of Rome, the Discovery of America by Columbus,
and the Fall of Jerusalem —vacation by consent was
impossible. It had to be taken. In the entering
room I looked at a Mont Cenis of printed calico
miscalled “zephyr,” a great Dividing Range of
China matting, a Teneriffe of boxes of ruching, and
a Gibraltar of muslin and silk ; and I determined
on flight, not to escape work, which I have always
been fond of, but because the sun shone that day
and might not shine again. Bdt the war chest was
empty. I went to the cashier, accountant and
highly-respected treasurer; mentioned that a month’s
salary would be due me in three days, and asked for
a sovereign. After much demur, he gave me 10 per
cent, of my own money ; then he kept me changing
my weight from one leg to another in sheer nervous
fear of him while he told me I was heading in the
direction of the Pit, and that he that goeth not to
the Savings Bank o’ Saturdays and to the Tabernacle
o’ Sabbaths shall meet the magistrate o’ Tuesdays.
At last he dismissed me. and I cleared my file of
invoices in two hours and gave the Rag Palace leg-
bail and fled to Manly. It was an innocent day. I
had my own company, and dreamed dreams on the
big sandstone cliffs with the Pacific booming below,
and tried to make verses among the native roses out
by Narrabeen ; and I had three ice-creams and three
ginger-beers and two rides on a sleeping rotary pro-
pelled by a gloomy man, and called in joke a
“merry-go-round.” I went back to the calico pyra-
mids next day and worked double tides; but I was
carpeted by the trusty treasurer and talked at as
if I were guilty of simony, arson, piracy and murder
in the third degree, instead of a mere schoolboy
offence of “wagging it.” He told mq that now I
was at the critical position between the chancel and
the gallows; doomed now to be other one of the
Rogues’ Gallery or a saint in a stajitjed-glass window
—as if there were no middle course. But he gener-
ally impressed me with the belief that I was 90
per cent, original sin and the rest vacillation, while
he himself was of the only real and genuine com-
munion of saints. It affected me so deeply that
three months later I took the same sort of voluntary
and irresponsible holiday, and, being weary of rags,
anyhow, gave myself the sack. A few years later
the saint was arrested, in the sort of house saints
are supposed to avoid except when necessary to con-
vert the inmates. He had been robbing the firm for
years, and so ingeniously as to defy audit after
audit. Yet he was tremendously human ; so human
as to be able to extract much spiritual good from
the giving of advice he could not take himself. A
peculiarly criminal characteristic this—to be himself
old Mr. Bright’s disease while handing the kidney
medicine to Hercules. * . ...
And the conclusion of the whole matter is that
the detection of crime that starts out by putting the
criminal beyond the pale of humanness must nearly
always fail.
