like these hasty people to live up here behind the
bar.
In good times the bar is fairly straight (say as
straight as George-street at its curliest), fairly wide
(say as wide as Pitt-street at its narrowest, with
a stone breakwater on one side and a sand spit on
the other), iairly smooth and fairly deep (say Bft.
9in., with the vessel loaded down to 9ft.). Then,
with a good tide, a good head of steam, a good
skipper, a good loud curse, and a go'od slice of luck,
out she bumps, through the sand, thumping (say)
three times. The skipper then wipes his perspiring
brow, asks forgiveness for his language, cautiously
wriggles the wheel to see if his rudder is intact,
rings up the cussing engineer to ask if the pro-
peller is still there, and, all being well, starts the
long thrash against the southerly that always blows
when I’m a passenger for Sydney.
This is in good times. Just now, with no fresh
in the river and a long succession of north-easters,
the Nambucca bar has to be seen to be believed. For
you can easily see it. The crossing of the bar
becomes a succession of thrills. At the top of high
water, the coaster having wriggled over the “cross-
ing” or inner or sixpenny bar, takes the bit
between her teeth, so to speak, and takes the bar
proper. The skipper's plan is to plough through
the sand by main engine force before two things
can happen: (1) that the sand gets into the engines,
or (2) that the seas turn the vessel’s bow ever
so slightly and wash her up on to the hungry spit.
For weeks navigation on the Nambucca was like
this, but latterly this weird brand of navigation has
been impossible, so on January 8, the pilot and
30 volunteers pranced out on to the bar and dug a
channel through it. The scheme was for the pent-up
water inside to scour out a corridor wide enough to get
the dredge into. But this relief is only a make-shift,
as tons of sand are rolling in, with the weight of
the Pacific behind it, every tide. Meanwhile, our
fish, pigs and produce are missing the market, and
decaying before our eyes and under our noses, which
is worse. We are carting our very beer 20 miles,
and fresh meat and vegetables are not to be ob-
tained. These last-named don’t matter much, but
the beer problem is causing acute reflection.
I shouldn’t be surprised if somebody doesn’t hear
of Port Stephens some of these days. It is a real
port.
»
The Sydney “wharfies” lately started one of
their playful - strikes—at least it wasn’t
exactly a strike, but it was part of one. Some
time ago the existing agreement was thrown
overboard into the soup, and a new one was
arrived at which fixed the remuneration at
Is. 6d. per hour during ordinary working
time apd 2s. 3d. per hour overtime. The
other day it was suggested that this one
should also go into the soup—pretty nearly
all the agreements seem to go there —and
that the pay should be 2s. per hour ordinary
time and 3s. overtime. As the proposal
wasn’t immediately accepted, a curious sort
of semi-strike started. The wharf-lumping
business was to go on as usual during the
day, but no overtime at all to be worked. The
change came on Friday night when there is
a rush to load the numerous vessels which
quit on Saturday, and many a surprised
and haggard ship, which had a time-table
to live up to, fled out on Saturday partially
empty, leaving a heap of its cargo behind it
on the wharf. Ex-Attorne.v-General Hughes
is head of the Sydney Waterside Workers’
Association, and he rushed into the breach
on behalf of the existing agreement, but ap-
parently he was simply bidden to rush out
again. Because N.S.W. is allowed to use
only one port for all its foreign trade (apart
from coal) the Sydney wharfies can hang
up practically everything at short notice.
There is no waste of time negotiating for
the support of the men at big rival cities
on Jervis Bay or Twofold Bay or Port
Stephens. There is just one meeting, and
then a thud and a crash, and an anguished
Hughes remonstrating in the distance.
Vasco Loureiro, son of Melbourne’s well-
remembered brushman, is giving himself a
trip to Australia this summer. As a youth,
Loureiro was inclined to be unrobust, where-
fore he chose an open-air occupation—
sketching passengers on Port Phillip
steamers at a shilling a blob. The blobs
proved orofitable, and presently he took to
inter-State boats, where he charged a higher
fee. Successful beyond expectations, the
artist packed up his pencil and set forth to
conquer the world. He did it. At any rate,
he now has a pleasant bank-balance, apart
fripm the means to publish his book. “Around
tliq World on a Pencil,” due shortly.
Dunlop, the Victorian doubles champion
at tennis, who was just off to Europe to play
for the Davis Cup, lost his head badly after
the fire at “The Colosseum,” in which vast
fluffery he got a big interest through marry-
ing a daughter of the late Treadway, its
founder. There are few men who have mixed
more pleasure with their business, and no
doubt reckoned their business was all the
better for it, than Dunlop: and therein he
followed on the lines laid down by Tread-
way, who in his latter davs was in the habit
of handing round field-glasses to the men
chosen for Australian Elevens and such like.
Yet after the fire, Dunlop, exploding in the
Melbourne papers over the alleged effeteness
and general unsatisfaetoriness of the Fire
Brigade, made sarcastic remarks about the
prevalence of billiard tables at the fire-
stations. Whereupon Chief Officer Lee was
stirred up to say that if Dunlop’s firm hadn’t
been so infernally mean, and had employed a
temporary watchman while the regular hand
was off on leave, there most likely would
have been no fire at all for Dunlop to get
annoyed about.

George Dean, who used to be billed as
“the actor who can sing and the singer who
can act,” hasn’t been billed as anything for
some time past, owing to sickness. Dean
has appeared in most theatres in Australia,
under most managements, and friends who
think his has been hard mozzle are giving
him a “complimentary farewell” matinee at
the Little Theatre (Sydney) on Friday, the
oOth. The J.C.W., Willoughby, Fuller-
Brennan and other managements have per-
mitted their people to lend a hand, and it is
hoped that the cash-box will be full enough
to take the beneficiare to England, where
prospects for him are rosier.
** * *
“Monsieur Beaucaire,” revived at Sydney
Royal last Saturday, gives the departing
Lewis Waller the best chance he has had,
and he does so well that it is fortunate Julius
Knight, who was Beaucaire the First so far
as Australia is concerned, is now dealing in
Shaw and other dramatists. Not that Julius
wasn’t good, bu<t that Lewis is better. The
superiority of Waller is most noticeable in
the Djana-by-moonlight . love scene, where
the triumphing Beaucaire yearningly com-
mands the melting Mary to “Turn to me!”'
It is such a delicate passage that it becomes
painful _if r. stressed. Waller, with every
female in .the house hanging on his words
and ready 'to follow him through thick and
thin, mercifully glides over it. and thereby
earns the gratitude of every fidgeting male
in the audience. He is graceful throughout,
and acts with charm and sincerity, though
just a little careless with his dressing. Madge
Titheradge seems a trifle overweighted at
first. The uppish Lady Mary looms so large
in the text that she looks a hopeless job for
such a slight and willowy actress; but, as
the character softens (in the heart), the
clever Australienne takes hold. Frank
Woolfe, as the unscrupulous Duke of Winter-
set, is fairly raw. Almost every actor has
his mannerism,, and all villains (Woolfe’s
line of business is stage villainy) are more or

less alike ; but as the Duke he should have

ably. It’s a pity Waller can’t restore a few

Pinero’s “His House in Offler” has been
chosen by the Hugh Buckler Company for
its welcome reappearance at the Little
Theatre, Sydney, next Saturday.
There are no signs yet at the Sydney
Criterion of a fair deal with its audiences.
When in “Officer 666” Fred Niblo frankly
declares just before the curtain swoops down
that the whole thing has been a mere
theatrical fake—that such things couldn’t
have happened except on the stage—he does
not offer the audience its money back. And
some of the audience would rather like to
have it back. However, the fact that the
dramatist, admits that he has been writing
rot is a sign of grace. So many dramatists
don’t even know they are writing rot.

If it does nothing else, Gene Greene’s rag-
time competition at Sydney Tivoli shows
what asses some people are prepared to make
of themselves free of charge, and as far as
it has gone that’s about all it has done. The
Romano Bros., in a series of poses; the
Schmettans, equilibrists; and Reynolds and
Donegan, skaters, fill the hard-work section
of the bill; Gene Greene. Josephine Davis
and Gladys Gordon (whose singing would
“go” much better if the second voice, which
might belong to an unpaid landlady or a
revengeful _ mother-in-law were out of the
way) provide the more-or-less musical half.

Women’s proneness to pleasure and men’s
proneness to foot the bill are strikingly ex-
emplified at the White City (Sydney) these
nights. Any doubting philosopher is re-
quested to forward his name and address to
the manager of the pleasure-ground for
press-agency purposes.

The tango craze has wormed its way into
‘A Day at the Races” at the Palace (Syd-
ney). This week the co. has been illustrat-
ing how the dance, body-lock, or whatever it
is, originated, was developed and reached its
present stage. Thus another great problem
is solved, and the income-tax-payers’ union
can get back to work.
The seven people in Sydney who have not
yet seen the pantomime, “Aladdin,” at the
Adelphi, are urged to go along without delay
so that Statistician George Willoughby may
close the records.
The best at Sydney theatres this
week: —
Crystal Palace: “House of Discord.” This is
one of the A. B. Co.’s special domestic dramas with a
moral lesson. The moral lesson has been taught
before, but no moral lesson can be taught too often.
In time somebody is bound to be impressed. Even
the Ten Commandments have a chance.
Lyric : “The Convent Gate.” The story of a girl
who, being abandoned by her lover, flees for refuge
to a convent, but on explanation, flees back to her
lover, and is married. Moral: Some people are never
satisfied.
Colonial: “The Chinese Death Thorn.” This is a
5000 ft. film exposing the evils of the opium craze.
All opium fiends are offered a reduction at the doors
on presenting cards of membership to three accredited
poppy joints at Potts Point.
Empress: “The Test.” An up-to-date or bunny-
hug version of the Garden of Eden. Caste: Adam, a
doctor of medicine, too attentive to his job ; Eve, a
neglected wife; the co-re. with the cloven purpose,
a military officer. The story begins in the usual
way, and ends in the ordinary manner. A fair ner-
centage of the audience will, therefore, be able to
judge its accuracy for themselves.
Glaciarium and Olympia (West’s): “Out on the
Deep. Good story with a big shipwreck scene.
Lyceum (Spencers): “Between'Man and Beast"
being a series of hair-trigger adventures with the
wild parishioners of the jungle.
Imperial: A dozen of them.
The revue is still booming at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney). Some nights it booms
rather more uproariously than it does on
other nights, but the disturbance occasioned
by its triumph is invariably audible at least
aJblock or two away, which is all a reason-
able show can demand.
In Adelaide, the King’s has been enjoying
itself immensely, with both Royal and Tivoli
closed. Manager Muller has had three first-
in-Australia turns to show. Foster Lamont
and Foster are “iron-jawed.” In these days
of rickety artificial molars, it is refreshing
(or uncomfortable, according to individual
fancy) to see an athlete doing violent turns
on a rope suspended solely from her com-
panions’ teeth; and they bite just as well
when their owners are upside-down or cor-
ner-ways. The Nell Elsing Co. does a
“sketch” of American sentimentality, set
among wharf-loafers who call girl “goil,”
and say “sure t’ing” for Yes. There is one
pretty touch. On Thanksgiving Day they
take stock of what they have to be thankful
for. The sole asset is one large onion—
except that the boy has his goil. Then she
is snatched away (entirely for her own
good) to a Home. So the boy brings out the
onion, and unconventionally, as man to man,
thanks his Creator for that. (Curtain.)
So far, Miss Elsing has had fewer chances
than an unnamed male companion. As for
the Trovatore Trio, they do not show suffi-
cient cause why they were brought all the
way from the U.S.A. Still a change in
their turn after the first three nights was
an improvement. The Mclntosh has de-
scended upon Adelaide, and instigated
manager Crawford into transferring the
whole Tivoli show to the Oval. Brans-
combe’s Dandies, also in the open, continue
to be a gold-mine. With every Cricket
Association member having the right to ad-
mission for himself and two ladies, there
should be no lack of attendance at
Mclntosh’s show, anyway. Pictures—
West’s, Wondergrapli. Pav., Empire and
others—vary from “Moths” and “Butter-
fly,” which have no connection with each
other, to the true method of dancing a
Tango.
At Melbourne Royal the end of the first
week of “Diplomacy” was the beginning of
Last Nights. The highly theatrical comedy,
recently revived in London after a lapse of
three dozen years, is not showing cause for
its re-introduction to Australia. Miss Grace •
Noble has changed her outfit for the final
scene, and the pronunciations of foreign lan-
guages—French and Spanish—by certain
members of the co, are less suggestive of a
Cook’s Excursion than their previous ac-
cents, but the falseness of the plot and fool-
ishness of everybody engaged in diplomatic
service remains as glaring as before. The
young man who leaves his important des-
patch-box lying around whilst his bride has
charge of the key; the elder brother who
passes this carelessness without comment;
the scheming Baron who becomes suddenly
unsuspicious and amenable to “bounce,” and
finally the Countess, who will confess any-
thing in order to get possession of one par-
ticular letter, as though the holder of the
alleged letter (which turns out to be a blank
sheet of paper) couldn’t have obtained
plenty of duplicates thereof —all these
people are simpletons in diplomatic clothing.
Quite apart from the incidents of the stolen
paper and the purloined photo., the personal
appearance of the Countess (in this produc-
tion) invites contempt for the perspicacity
of all persons concerned. She strikes atti-
tudes of guilt wherever she goes, but all the
decent diplomatic people trust her implicitly,
except Henry Beauclerc, who appears to be
unfavorably impressed when they first meet,
but never thinks of suspecting her during
the next two acts. As for Baron Stein, he
regards the lurid Countess as the most
artful confidence woman on earth. She is
his belle ideal of a secret paper-chaser.
Whereas he ought to say to her: “My dear
Zicka. in your existing form you are im-
possible. Change your style, and try to
look innocent,” he kisses her scented hand
and calls her a great diplomatist. “Dip-
lomacy” is heartless, refined melodrama, ap-
pealing only to an intelligent audience’s
sense of improbability. No character in the
piece is worth the cheers, tears or groans of
an unsophisticated gallery. The heroine and
her mother are a pair of wandering im-
pecuniosities who cheat the jeweller and the '
laundry, whilst the young man who marries
into their disrespectable family is disposed
to cheat the barber.

The panto, at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne)
is the same money-making concern that
ever it was. Ali Baba Lupino and Edwin
Brett, wife of the above, are still keeping
up their end of the frivolity, which is the
thick end. Marie Eaton and the Forty
Thieves continue to store their stolen pro-
perty in a cave instead of getting rich on
it, and the vivacity of the many-legged
su PPorts Ganem in the refrain
of My Southern Maid” is beautiful to con-
template.
Recent additions to the second act of “The
Land of Nod” (Melbourne King’s) are an
improvement. Judging from the matinee
attendances, Anderson’s nondescript show
appears to be pretty much of a panto for
the young. Children are interested in April
I 001, the Man in the Moon and the King of
Hearts, and their views concerning jokes are
very liberal, as a rule. Also, they have been
so carefully instructed in the majesty of
their Gracious King that they feel almost
excited when a mild impersonator of George
V. pops up in a stage box to receive their
homage.

The Melbourne Opera House programme
was considerably enriched by two new items
on Saturday. Hawley & Co., who play “The
Bandit” at high pressure, are two men and
a lady, and their lightning playlet in half-
an-act is charged with cries of rage, horror
and despair. The plot suggests two old
Italian opera stories boiled down to 20 min-
utes. If either of the three performers—
one of whom figures as a dumb maniac or a
speechless nightmare—were to stop to sneeze
the spell would be broken and the house
would fall into a fit of laughter. But they
rush through to the gruesome end without a
pause. Hawley, in the name part, defeats
the game little lady by a long neck, with the
raging maniac two lengths away. Clemons
and Dean are dancers who can sing and
act. The male partner (Clemons presum-
ably) does a dance of inebriation which is
quite wonderful and wholly humorous. Noth-
ing so quaintly effective has “knocked” a
Melbourne audience since the late Johnny
Coleman introduced his “Scarecrow” nov-
elty. The lady also dances joyously, and
their rendering of ragtime coon songs—
especially “You Made Me Love You”—leaves
de niggahs beaten at their own game. Many
other white folk have been dealing with the
same colored ditty of late, but it remained
for Clemons and Dean to explain what the
other people were singing at.
** * *
At West’s., Melbourne, last Saturday,
“Salvation Sal” required a few hundred
feet of moving picture to reform a tough
called Red McGee. “Red” was ruddv in
language and worse in habits, but Sal ‘took
him in hand, and by hard preaching and
praying made him the envy of the local
Y.M.C.A. “A Cause for Thankfulness,” also
at West’s, demonstrated how Pride fell in a
railway accident, while “Between Man and
Beast” described how a Dago was pressed
to become a tiger’s breakfast.

t’ercy Grainger has had a remarkable experience in
Berlin His lively “Shepherds, Hey!” which has
achieved considerable popularity in England, was
played by an orchestra at a concert in the German
capital. One section of the audience (anti-Bull, per-
haps) hissed, while the other lot applauded. The
latter insisted on an encore, and the golden-headed

jSbe a f reed - , So wroth were we at not being

. Down to our own times, actors were in the
habit of wearing their hair long, but the custom is
now out of fashion. Henry Irving was one of the
last to stick to it religiously. At the annual dinner
of the Actors Benevolent Fund in London, Henry
Ainley, who recites Adam Lindsay Gordon better than
any other man in England, incidentally alluded to
, oust 0 ” 1 , and gave his theory as to its origin.
It dates, die said, from the Dark Ages, when actors
were legally classed with “rogues and vagabonds.” An
actor in those times when caught had his ears
pierced by an official of the local authority as a
present punishment and an abiding mark for future
identification. By wearing the hair long and well
over the ears, actors were able to conceal their con-
victions from inconvenient observers.
The Australasian Moving Picture Annual, edited
by Fred Samson and printed by Syd. Day in Mel-
bourne, is a better thing of its kind than most of
the English and American theatrical sixpennies that
come this way. Two or three stories are bright
reading, and the portraits of leading cinematograph
mummers are more interesting than the old, familiar
photos of the musical-comedy girls, who seem to
have only one smile among the lot of them. The
picture-actor nowadays has a wider circle of
adorers than the most beauteous of talking mum-
mers. Samson estimates the weekly attendance at
various picture shows in Sydney and Melbourne
alone at something more than 350,000 a week.
“0.K.”: The late Cuyler Hastings is hummed up
by “Bloggs” as “an indifferent actor, with a splen-
did stage personality and an attractive stage appear-
ance.” Well, Hastings lacked the suavity and the
mechanical tricks of technique which are necessary
to what is known as “a good actor” in the mum-
mer trade. But, from the general public’s point of
view and understanding, he was a capital actor,
because he held an audience’s attention in a master-
ful manner, and was gifted with a fine, musical
magnetic voice. His appearance, to my way of
thinking, was coldly pleasing, rather than attrac-
tive. But his voice ! It has fixed upon my memory
three or four of the scenes in which he figured-
notably the corpse scene in the first act of “Sunday,”
and the scene in “The Admirable Crichton,” where
the gunboat comes to the island and the butler real-
the fact that his reign has ended. I think the
voice of Cuyler Hastings was the finest I ever heard.
From London: Alec Hurley turns out to have
been only a little over 40, or younger than his
(legal) widow, Marie Lloyd. Margaret Cooper, at
the piano, has brought herself right up to date
with a satirical song, “In My Revue.” The sultry
“Saplio” has been filmed. The picture-version, hav-
ing passed the British censor, is innocuous and
rather dull. A whole week of Suffragette propa-
ganda drama has been given at the Coronet. Strange
to say, the propagandists relied on foreign work,
translations from Bjornson and Brieux. The latter
author was present in person. Horace Hodges, who
was in Australia with Wilson Barrett, has a fat
part as Chauvelin in Fred Terry’s revival of “The
Scarlet Pimpernel.” Edward Lauri is running the
tango Teas at the Pavilion—half a crown for tea,
sandwiches and various dances, and an undress
parade of_ milliner’s models in corsets and other
underclothing. And old Teddy Royce, who was in
Australia long ago, and was 72 last birthday, has dis-
tinguished himself by playing the burglar in “Sealed
Orders” (the big Drury Lane melodrama), with a
shoulder that had been dislocated just before the
curtain rose.

“Harrison O.”: What is “Private Boxer” giving us
when he alleges (B. 8/1/’l4) that Shaw “never was
a dramatist” ? The very first act of “Man and Super-
man”—probably all of Shaw that “Private Boxer”
knows aught about —presents Violet, whom all had
looked upon as a brazen hussy living in “sin,” as
a respectable married female. Isn’t this dramatic?
Furthermore, what about the triangular scene in
the last act of “Candida,” the second act of “Arms
and the Man,” much of “Captain Brassbound’s Con-
version,” and almost the whole of “The Devil’s
Disciple” ?, Anyone who asks for anything more
dramatic than the scene in the parson’s house, and
the subsequent trial and the scene on the scaffold in
“The Devil’s Disciple,” must be suffering from a
comatose artistic perception and a rush of ennui to
the head.
An interesting par. from Melbourne Herald: —
“When ‘Diplomacy’ was produced at the Theatre
Royal on Saturday night there was present in the
audience a resident of South Yarra, Mr. Ernest E.
Keep, who witnessed the first production of Sardou’s
play at the Prince of Wales Theatre, London, by
the Bancrofts in 1878. Mr. Keep was struck by
the marked resemblance between Sir Squire Ban-
croft’s and Mr. Julius Knight’s conceptions of the
part of Henry Beauclerc.”
This is interesting for the , reason that the South
Yarra citizen is presented as a typical old playgoer
who professes to have retained a vivid, not a vague,
recollection of theatrical performances which hap-
pened in the dim past, though it is questionable
whether the most educated memories can accurately
re-picture any passing form of entertainment after
an interval of 20 years. (Making due allowance
for the fact that impressions formed in the age of
enthusiasm are the most enduring.) The supposed
recollection in this case is a doubly humorous de-
lusion. Bancroft’s methods in any character were
essentially different from Julius Knight’s. Moreover,
Knight as Henry Beauclerc is not playing Bancroft’s
original part. The tall, slim “society” comedian
played Count Orloff, whilst John Clayton was the
Henry Beauclerc now “conceived” by Julius Knight.
“Diplomacy” had a notable cast for'its first produc-
tion in London. Mrs. and Mr. Kendal were the
young lovers, John Clayton the elder Beauclerc and
Bancroft the Count Orloff. Mrs. Bancroft was Count-
ess Zicka, and Arthur Cecil identified himself with
the part of Baron Stein. Apropos of Arthur Cecil’s
performance the Saturday Review said: “The ad-
mirable acting, on the whole, of the piece more than
atones for the faults of the adaptation.”
“C. Ockney”: A slip of the “W. V. Penn” surely
causes that Poverty Pointer to allude (B. 8/l/’l4)
to Arthur Roberts’s old-time production of “Gentle-
man Joe” as a “burlesque.” As a matter of fact it
was one of a series of bright little musical comedies
(“The French Maid” was the first) written by Basil
Hood and put into music by Walter Slaughter, and
was far more deserving of the title than the elab-
orate Daly and Gaiety pieces for which a string of
six or seven authors and composers were responsible.
Roberts was (and is) facile princeps as a burlesque
comedian; but he could give a genuine artistic
character performance whenever called upon, and his
“Gentleman Joe, the Hansom Cabby, M although
screamingly funny, was far removed from burlesque.'
Slaughter, the composer (of sugary “Dear Home-
land’’ fame), is dead long since; but the librettist.
Hood, an ex-military man, is still going as strong
as ever in London. He is the author of the “book”
of the latest Robert Courtneidge “hit,” “The Pearl
Girl,” at the Shaftesbury.
J. M. Synge’s “Playboy of the Western World,”
which has had tumultuous experiences in Ireland and
America, was lately at Liverpool (England), WhdFe
a number of the “bhoys” had to be thrown out of
the theatre for protesting both with voices and
missiles. The dramatic critic of the .Times and
many literary authorities consider the play a
dramatic masterpiece; but there are some I rish f
men who won’t see in it anything but an attempt to
smirch the fair fame of the women of their country.
After a couple of rowdy performances in Liverpool,
the police shut down on the show in the interests of
lawnorder; whereupon W. B. Yeats, poet, dramatist
and intimate friend of the late Synge, writes a letter
of protest in the Times, demanding of the police,
not that they suppress a play which a few noisy
rioters don’t understand, but that they protect its
performance until the proper authority declares it
unfit for presentation. ''
The position of the theatre orchestrians is hardly
as strong as that of the stage hand#.“ The time is
coming when all the music required for an ordinary
drama or comedy performance will be obtainable per
gramophone. Picture shows are almost certain to
utilise the funnel-graph in conjunction with the filmo-
graph. The performance will never be truly har-
monious until the audible, like the visible, part of it
is worked by machinery.
If any man has earned a rest for the re-
mainder of his life it is John Lomas, secre-
tary of the Maoriland Labor Department,
who has retired on the age limit. He started
to toil at seven years of age in the potteries
at Hanly (Eng.), and has been toiling and
even moiling ever since. In those good old
times he, with hundreds of other infants,
worked twelve hours a day for one shilling
a week. After a year of that the boy was
considered sufficiently grown to work as a
trucker in the mines, and, in return for the
sweat of his small body, was paid’sixpence a
day. As a young man he arrived in Maori-
land under contract to mine for the West-
port Coal Co., and was in one pit and an-
other until the ’9O strike. That threw him
out of employment, but just when he was on
the point of returning to Britain, W. P.
Reeves (then Minister for Labor) appointed
him to the newly-formed Labor Department,
under Edward Tregear. Lomas rose gradu-
ally by conscientious ability until, in 1911,
after 20 years’ service, he succeeded Tregear,
on that officer’s retirement, as chief.
The F.L.S. (Fellow of the Linnsean
Society) is something of a rare bird in Aus-
tralia. Edward Pescott, lately hatched into
one of them from the shell of plain citizen-
ship, is the principal of Burnley (Vic.)
School of Horticulture. For long years
this Government job was held by Bogue
Huffman, who _ wrote “A Vagabond in
Spain” after doing the vagabonding in per-
son. Pescott, originally a State-school
teacher, for a decade acted as inspector in
the employ of the Agricultural Department.
When Huffman was somewhat rudely lifted
out of his comfortable quarters at Burnley,
bitterly protesting, Pescott was regarded as
the. right person for the billet, and got it.
It is a pleasant job, in a land flowing with
milk, honey, poultry, pickled fruits and prize
vegetables. It seems probable that, even
prior to his new-found happiness in be-
coming an F.L.S., Pescott enjoyed life as
well as most special-billet Government: ser-
vants, and a lot better than those who heave
soil about without reference to the horti-
cultural possibilities thereof.
Pp e of the funniest items of the Victorian
Ministerial bv-elections was the violent in-
dignation of Minister Hagelthorn that his
private business should be made a matter of
public concern. Publicly Hagelthorn bar-
racks for the Watt immigration policy, but,
m his private business of land agent, he,
as Opposition-leader Elmslie put it, “flooded
the Wimmera with circulars setting forth
the advantages of N. S. Wales for land set-
tlement as against Victoria.” Hagelthorn
is only doing what he is legally entitled to
do; the fault lies with the people who em-
ploy him as Minister and M.L.C. No man
—even the broad-minded Hagelthorn him-
self would long employ a fire insurance
agent, for instance, after he had accepted a
scat on tlie board of a rival concern.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The theatre musicians’ strike is mainly for extra
pay, but another point in which there is a varia-
tion from the log relates to the playing of selec-
tions while audiences are leaving the building. The
musicians’ union is insisting that no music excepting
the National Anthem shall be played after the curtain
has been finally lowered.
The union of noise-merchants puts a new command-
ment out:
“Thou shalt not learn new music for to play the
audience out ;
“Thou shalt not send the people home to music glad
and gay,
Hut with the well-known dirge destroy the good
work of the play.

new refrain,
But make from Christmas to the Cup that awful
row again.
“They shall not suffer any new ‘Omph-ha’s’ from the
trombone, .
But with-, the old Anathema .shall undermine the
throne.
“They shall not play a march to make the going
audience glad,
But with the dreary German hymn drive stalls and
circle mad.

ring,

Savejfthe King.’

own

MORE ‘LIKE IT.
MABEL.: “ Did I see you coming out of an hotel

GEORGE: "No; but you might have seen me

The Tinline Scholarships, given by Judge
Murray to Adelaide University in memory
of his maternal grandfather, are designed to
encourage students to dig among the musty
old records of Australian early-days. He
(or she —the prize has been won four times
by girl-graduates and only twice by men)
gets £3O on selecting a subject, and another
£3O on weighing-in with a satisfactory essay
on it. Each of the scholars so far has been
recommended to take five or 10 years of the
early history of South Australia, and un-
earth everything that can possibly be dug
up about the brief period. The result is
going to be, eventually, a very complete his-
tory of the State, laden with a vast amount
of very dull detail that isn’t worth bothering
about.
The usual parson lias been making the
usual wholesale charges of iniquity -against
Port Adelaide this time. It is another Go-
morrah, according to the sermon ot Lionel
Fletcher, with rampant drunkenness and
immorality a-nd a two-up school, attended
by 500 people, where children are taught to
play. The usual howl of contradiction fol-
lowed, with the usual complete failure of
the attacker to give definite instances when
called on for them. At last Attorney-Gene-
ral llomburg took the unusual course of
patrolling the Port himself one hot night,
and found all things “quiet and orderly.”
The police report that it is “one of the best-
conducted seaports in the Commonwealth.
There are a small number of bookmakers,
but they receive no quarter from the police.
There are no brothels, and no common prosti-
tutes on the street. The last two that were
before the Court were from Adelaide.” I
am not aware of the gambling-school where
the 500 meet, and I would be very thankful
to the rev. gentleman if he would let me
know where it is situated,” says the boss
policeman. Of course there was no re-
sponse. Of Fletcher’s good intentions there
is no doubt; but the parson, in any matter
that needs proof, seems to suffer from his
professional training, which (1) Assures
him that he must believe, and must get
others to believe, things that are simply in-
capable of proof; (2) Ensures him an'un-
interrupted, uncontradicted hearing for any-
thing whatever he may say. Thus, when
he bumps up against a cold world that says
“Whaffor?” and “Prove it!” he at once gets
disqualified on a foul, and seems dishonest
when he was in reality only reckless.
“The Old Soldier”: The following aston-
ishing item was cabled to Australia a day or
so ago :
The Maharaja of Burdwan demanded a searching
inquiry into the S’African coolie scandal. LotU
Crewe’s speech, he said, had given some hope; but
till something was done the feeling in India must
remain bitter. He urged retaliation by repatriating
every Indian.
The person who made that
speech is not, as. might be
supposed from his title, a
great ruling chief. He is
simply one who has become
fabulously rich from the
profits made out of his
business as a “Zemindar,”
or agent for the collection
of land-tax from the culti-
vators of the old Subadari
of Bengal. When the
British first had that Pro-
vince handed over to them
to administer, they were in
a difficulty, and they were
only too glad to lease the
tax-collecting to any native
agents who had done the
work, or said they had, for former
murky rulers. When Governor-
General- Cornwallis came on the
scene, he thought it would make
things better to give the agents a
“Permanent Settlement” —that is,
to make the Zemindars’ emolument
a fixed annual amount payable for
ever, while they had the right to
make the cultivators pay anything
they chose for rent, which is really
what land-tax means in that part
of the world. The Maharaja of
Burdwan, then, is permanent lease-
holder of a large area of land which
he holds from the Government at a
fixed rent, and sublets to the cul-
tivators at much higher rents. It
is not easy to see what he is to
get out of this agitation. He has
no traditional standing in Bengal,
and if the British are ever driven
out of India one of the first things
that will happen will be his expul-
sion, and that of all the Permanent
Settlement Zemindars of Bengal.
It is astonishing that anyone in
his position should have ventured
to use such language, and the out-
break can only be accounted for
on the supposition that recent
legislation has greatly reduced the
value of the Permanent Settlement, and the
rack-renting powers of Zemindars under it
—a type of reform that has occurred often
in the past.
N.S.W. Navigation Department, waking
up suddenly, has found out that it can, if it
likes, regulate and supervise motor-boats
and motor-launches that ply for hire. For
14 years the Department has hugged the
view that the Legislature wasn’t thinking
of motor-driven vessels when it passed the
present Act; and as everybody has been too
tired to pass any more Acts about a matter
Tike this with no votes in it, the Department
left the public which patronises motor-boats
to Providence. Lately the papers have been
full of reports of accidents and prognostica-
tions of others; and, realising that Provi-
dence wasn’t taking on the job seriously,
the Department of Navigation has an-
nounced that it has the power to compel
motor-boats to take ordinary precautions,
and, what is more, is going to see that they
are taken. As nothing has been added to its old
Act, either the marine authorities have been
very obtuse all these years, or they are put-
ting up a bluff on the motor-boats now.

There is little chance for kecrecy in “wire-
less” messages when any schoolboy can erbet
a listening station in the back yard at a
cost of about 7s. 4id. One night recently
a small boy who had his station away down
at the southern end of the Great Loan
Land “tapped” a message sent from a M.L.
high-power station by one of Massey’s Cabi-
net to the skipper of a big immigrant ship
that was approaching the Maoriland coast.
Marconi’s magic had been called into use by
the harassed Minister to implore the skipper
of the immigrant boat to pick him a good-
looking cook !
Charity organisations again! A Laun-
ceston (Tas.) catmittee which runs a sort
of Home for Orphans recently declined the
usual Christmas oiler of a number of citizens
to take the youngsters on a holiday trip.
The charitable ladies gave as a reason for
rejecting the kindly offer that holiday picnics
were not good “for children who were being
brought up as servants.” Apparently the
Tasmanian servant handmaiden is expected
to have a dreary time, like Martha, who
was worn to a shadow with much serving
in a lean-to at Bethlehem.
The Hebrews of Victoria are seething like
the inmates of an over-stocked hornets’ nest
because Watt’s Immigration Department
cabled recently to its London agent not to
send out any more Jewish boys as assisted
immigrants. The State has openings only
for actual and prospective farmers and
farm laborers, and the Jew—at least the
Western European Jew —does not as a rule
make any violent rush to go on the land.
The Russian, Polish or Austrian Yid is a
sort of hereditary farmer, but his cousin of
Whitechapel and other crowded Cold Coun-
try centres prefers to till the soil where old
clo’ and jewellery grow, and plough the pad-
dock of the three brass balls, and put the
quadruped out to agistment.
These industries are already well represented
in Australia, but the exasperated descendant
of Moses won’t realise it. Hence the seeth-
ing condition above-mentioned.
SMALL.
BERLIN, January 13. —The Emperor’s physicians
have given approval to his trying manual labor as
a cure for stoutness. His Majesty is engaged in
sawing wood in the gardens at Potsdam, and chop-
ping it into suitable sizes for the stoves. The
gardener who is assisting gets the wood the Emperor
chops as wages.
THE KAISER (having missed the object three
times in succession): “It is the object,
Hans: it is small, is it not? ”
HANS THE GARDENER (thinking of his divi-
dend): “Yes, your Majesty, painfully so."

GIRLS OF SYDNEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Girl of blue skies and blue waters,
Summer is singing of you-
Singing of Sydney’s fair daughters,
Each with her lover or two!
Crowned with a garland of promise,
Roaming, with languorous speed,
Paths that are pageants of pleasure,
Tuned to voluptuous measure. .. .
The gods have been gracious indeed!
Pleasure’s rose-light is the one light
Lures them —in gossamer-gold,
Butterflies meshed in the sunlight,
Dazzling, enchantingly bold,
Flitting from blossom to blossom,
Sipping gold-honey like wine,
Youth their inebriate dower,
Conscious of passionate power,
Human, yet almost divine!
Drearn-gkl of red wine and roses,
Summer is singing of you !. .. .
Ere her glad carolling closes
Choose from your lover or two!
Vanity wins its own heartache —•
Many the maiden it dupes !
They that have lost are farseeing
Love is the triumph of Being;
Love neither bargains nor stoops!
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

“Croson”: The Doncaster (Vic.) Tower
is gone—slain by the ruthless hand of the
knacker. About 40 years ago Hans or
Harry Hummell, a Melbourne wood-carter,
came into money. He settled some of it on
his wife. With the rest lie built a wooden
tower on the south side of the Doncaster
main road, at the crown of the big bill, and
invited Melbourne generally to drive out and
view his work. Melbourne responded cordi-
ally—for about 12 months —when a gale
capsized the contraption. Hummell removed
the debris and put up another look-out a
few yards further west, and six months
later the wind duly upended it. The
ex-wood-carter had a trifle of cash left and
unlimited pluck, and this time he crossed
the road and erected the rig-out that has
stood the storms of 38 years. Had he used
native timber —there are still a few red-
gum giants standing a few miles away—
his monument might have lasted a few hun-
dred years; but he employed pine, stayed it
with wire guy ropes, and of late the '23oft.
of framework threatened to topple down
headlong. Hummell passed out a few years
ago in Collingwood.

“Lifebelt”: Curious what tbin hairs the
Law is called upon to split. The Americans
who want £2,600,000 compensation from the
Oceanic Steam Navigation Co. for relatives
drowned in the Titanic argue that, in the
eyes of the law, the big ship now at the
bottom of the sea was a floating island fly-
ing the British flag, and, therefore,
amenable to British law. Under the U.S.
Admiralty laws, the claims would be limited
to salvage and passage money—a mere
trifle of £lB,OOO. Of course, the desperate
co. wants to have it that way. Meanwhile,
the “island” is gone where even the blessed
eye of the law can’t see it. Wonder if it
occurred to any travelling lawyer, on that
icy, boatless morning, as he waited glumly
for the last terrific plunge, that (in the
eyes of the law) lie was standing on British
ground? And if he did, was it any comfort:
to him?
The officers of Victoria’s Board of Public
Health professed themselves amused lately,
when a lady rang them up, stated that she
and her husband proposed to make holiday
at a certain country town, and inquired as
to the sanitary conditions of its - leading
hotel. Being told that the Board knew
nothing about such matters, she curtly in-
quired what the Plank was for if not to
inform people whether holiday houses were
conducted according to the rules of hygiene,
and rang off. Jt was mean of her to snatch
the last word thus brusquely, yet she was
entitled to it. The thorough inspection and
regulation of hotels should be one of the
early duties of any body allegedly charged
with looking after public health. Probably
not one-tenth of the hotels, coffee palaces,
restaurants and boarding-houses in the State
fulfil proper sanitary conditions, and if the
vast ma-joritv of private houses were over-
hauled likewise, the health of the public
would be advantaged. Public health isn’t
merely a matter of segregating small-pox
patients or testing milk supplies. But what
results can be expected ■ from a body that
• hasn’t power to close a Domestic Economy
College whose kitchen windows look on to
a stable?

'The necessity for providing elderly vag-
rants with reasonable opportunities to keep
out of “quod” was suggested by the case of
■Julia Hansen, aged 67, who stated her griev-
ance to Melbourne City Court the other
.Monday. Julia’s last term of imprisonment
expired on the Saturday morning, and soon
after sunset a constable ran her in for
vagrancy. “The police seemed to be wait-
ing for me at the gate,” said Julia to the
Bench, whereupon the prosecuting inspector
admitted that it might be as well to give
the old girl another chance to remain at
large. Obviously she is entitled to as much
(CQuasideration as a stray dog. There’s a
Home for Stray Dogs in Melbourne, and a
feature of the establishment is a lethal
chamber where decrepit inmates are put to
;sleep for evermore. But the only home
jprovided for vagrant humans is a comfort-
less prison, with no lethal chamber on the
premises and no visiting staff of kind ladies
who would pat Julia’s head when she wagged
her doleful tale. Prison is no home for
Julia. It is equally inhospitable and non-
permanent. She can neither live there for
good nor die there for better.

The words of “Henry Horsecollar” :
So the blessed Corporation of Sydney—may its
:name be Mud—pnoposes to take the city garbage 20
miles out to sea and drop it there, in order that
it may not return to defile the beaches where Billo
takes his tart to swim in neck-to-knee costume. It
1S , i (Jea h* h s humble yet bumptious way,
and the alderman who devised it should have a
whole rubbish tip as his great reward, and the
mud punt should he called after him, and his
cognomen, which is probably Cornelius Hogsflesh
, ° ul “ b ? wnt large thereon, in letters of gold and
iett-off chop-bones. Australia is very flat and conse-
quently short of fain, and the seas round it are
father shallow in places. So man, especially aider-
man, whose name is often Smith, or else Briggs,
believes in building 4CO-ton punts, in order to take
■as much of Australia as possible out to sea, and
drop it there, that the ’ land may become flatter and
more rainless, and the isea shallower and less navi-
gable. Also, because the fish supply is scarce, he
drops evii things in the sea to poison the flounder
and dnve the flathead mad. Garbage is mostly a
fertiliser, and, if it were taken inland instead of to
;sea it might enrich the land instead of polluting the
waters. Also, it is more or less of a combustible.
.In some places that are unlike the city of Sydney
it is made to produce light and power and pay a
dividend—-the rather tattered suburb of Balmain
which is just across the water from Sydney, can do
things like that. But Sydney sticks hard and fast
to its costly policy of stripping the land and filling
up the adjacent waters. It is alleged in one of the
more obscure sections of the Scripture that at the
End of All Things the sea will be gathered up and
will cease to perform its old job. Whatever arch-
angel has the business of gathering up the bit of sea
adjacent to Sydney is likely to get a very low
opinion of the inhabitants. He will find a lot of
things there that aren’t sea, unless Sydney repairs
its habits between now and the event.
“Ten Guineas”: Sydney Coroner Haw-
kins, at an inquest on a woman unduly
dead, voiced the perennial grievance about
expert evidence. The woman was in a lift
in one of the' big department stores when
the thing stuck between two floors. After
a long wait for the concern to start, a step-
ladder was brought to help to get
the passengers out. While she was
getting out, the weapon started as
suddenly as it had stopped, and the
woman was killed. The store people,
anxious to allay public concern, produced a
pyramid of expert evidence to show that the
lift was in splendid order and that an acci-
dent was impossible. It was an heroic thing
to do with the dead body staring the experts
in the face, but evidence is a strange animal
and cannot change its spots. Hawkins
tersely remarked that “the expert evidence
is against the facts.” It usually is. I was
interested once in a Vice-Admiralty case, a
steamship having obviously and flamboy-
antly foundered in smooth water. A cloud
of experts—ship captains, marine and dock-
yard superintendents, marine surveyors and
civil engineers, naval johnnies and naval
architects —all swore that the ship was ab-
solutely incapable of foundering. I remem-
ber my astonishment when the Court, after
these witnesses had all finished, had the cool
cheek to bring in a verdict that the ship had
sunk. Still the experts earned their fees,
for the verdict meant that the vessel had
committed suicide, and my crowd didn’t get
a bean.
Perhaps, after aH, Bill Adams did win the
battle of Waterloo. Just across Alma-road
on the south side of the Fremantle Hospital,
is one of the oldest of Westralian cemeteries,
rail of the dust of ancient identities. Bush
fires have swept away all consumable struc-
tures ; but on a stone that lies prone and
broken, there is this inscription:—
Sacred
to the Memory of
WILLIAM ADAMS
(One of the Heroes of Waterloo),
Who died Deer. 12th, 1867
Aged 75 years.
AT THE PALAIS DE DANSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Listen! The sound of solemn feet;
The thud of Percy who collides
With someone’s slow-revolving “sweet”;
The dull, excruciating beat
Of timid footsteps that repeat
Grim, sluggish circlings, small and neat,
To dashing music which, besides
Fast floors, the management provides
For moving meat that glumly glides
It’s not the thing for one to prance
A\ ith joy at the Palaice dee Dance.
Who, throwing off his sadness, makes
A dash and chicken-scratches some,
Or does a hug, or even “cakes,”
Unwritten laws of dolor breaks;
Laughter no merry echo wakes,
As some wild dancer, whirling, shakes
The floor, and tries to make things hum.
He, too, will soon be grim and glum,
Ungay, and good, and circling dumb —
A dancer who no longer longs
To prance at the Palay dub Dongs.
VICTORIA.
F.H.

HOW TO NEUTRALISE DANGEROUS
STOMACH ACIDS.
Few people besides physicians realise the
importance of keeping the food contents of
the stomach free from acid fermentation.
Healthy normal digestion cannot take place
while the delicate lining of the stomach is
being inflamed and distended by acid and
wind —the results of fermenting food in the
stomach. To secure perfect digestion, fer-
mentation must be stopped or prevented,
and the acid neutralised. For this purpose
physicians usually recommend getting a
little bisurated magnesia from the chemist
and taking half a teaspoonful in a little hot
or cold water immediately after eating.
They recommend bisurated magnesia be-
cause it is pleasant to take, has no disagree-
able after-effects, and instantly stops fer-
mentation, neutralises the acid and makes
the sour, acid food bland, sweet and easily
di^stcdi
The regular use of bisurated magnesia—
-be sure you get the bisurated, as other kinds
of magnesia are of little value —-is an abso-
lute guarantee of healthy, normal digestion,
for it overcomes and prevents that acid con-
dition which alone is the cause of trouble.*

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Some months ago the word went around
Melbourne legal circles that George Moir
was as dead, for business purposes, as his
late partner, Sam Gillott. Also, his no'n-
legal friends and acquaintances—hundreds
of them —shook their heads and gave him
up for lost. George Moir, however, had an
unsuspected reserve fund of vitality to draw
upon, and he is now in a fair way to follow
many another old client to the cemetery.
The melancholy duty of a popular solicitor
is to be in frequent attendance at funerals,
which is about the only sort of attendance
he doesn’t charge for on the bill.
ROBERT
BEST,
M.H.R.
The Roman Catholic
community at Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) has
been celebrating the
75th anniversary of
the appearance of the
first sisters of charity
in Australia. They
arrived in Sydney
in the ship Francis
Spaight, Captain Say-
ers, on December 31,
IS3S. In the list of
passengers published
in the Sydney Gazette
, are found : Dr. Ulla-
thorne, Revs. P. B.
Geoghagan (after-
wards the first priest
sent to Melbourne),
Richard Marum and Thomas Butler; Mrs.
Cahill (superioress), Mrs. O’Brian, Mrs.
Callen, Mrs. de Lacy and Miss Williams,
Sisters of Charity. These sisters were
brought out specially to minister to the
members of the R.C. faith among the con-
vict women. The “female factory,” as it
was called, was at Parramatta; so a cot-
tage handy to the “factory” was secured
for the use of the sisters, and there they
were established for many years.
After a lapse of over a quarter of a cen-
tury, Dr. Strong, of the Australian Church
(Melb.), has had the pulpit of the Collins-
street Independent Church thrown open to
him by Leyton Richards, who succeeded
Bevan. Before he was ostracised, Strong,
who was then pastor of Scots Church, often
preached in the building opposite; but the
doors were closed against him for nearly 30
years. When Dr. Moorhouse was Bishop of
Melbourne the churchwardens of St. Paul’s
waited on Strong and invited him to ex-
change pulpits with Bromby, the incumbent
of St. Paul’s Pro-Cathedral; and Strong,
having obtained the consent of the Bishop,
preached there to a crowded congregation.
He was not allowed inside the altar-rail,
however, and before service a protest was
read by a clergyman. Moorhouse was after-
wards hauled over the coals for allowing a
“layman” to conduct the service, Strong’s
“orders” not being recognised by a different
denomination, and the Bishop was officially
instructed not to commit such a crime .again.

“H. Singemoine” on the attempted
canonisation of Tyson :
I am all with those horse-sense Bulletin readers
who heap the brick of disparagement on Randolph
Bedford’s effort to represent Jimmy Tyson as a
sort of backblocks Socrates, and a model, in general,
for the youth of the Commonwealth. I once spent a
week on a P. and O. ship with that incorrigible
old posturer (I mean, of course, Tyson, not
Randolph), and the imperishable impression which
he produced on most of the passengers, two days after
leaving W.A. (where he got on board), was that no
beauty actor possessed more childish self-consciousness
and vanity. Bedford appears to be afflicted with the
common delusion that vanity is a matter, entirely,
of taking pride in a handsome appearance, beautiful
clothes, fine eyes and such-like essentially feminine
attributes. To this superficial variety of psycholo-
gist, a palpably self-satisfied young A.D.C. in aggres-
sively Clifford-street clothes is abhorrent, whereas
the spectacle of a Tyson garbed, with equal care
and forethought, in a shirt that has not been
washed for months and a suit that is ripe for the
ash-barrel, and spitting recklessly to right and left,
represents the ultimate howl in the way of rugged
Australian virility. As a matter of fact, the one
is as much a poseur as the other, with the differ-
ence that the London youth’s manners and smell
are usually unobjectionable.
Everyone who knew Tyson realised, after a couple
of hours’ talk with him, that he took an inordinate
pride in his dirtiness, in his uncivilised clothes, in
his low-comedy boots, and his reputation as a stark,
dauntless, near-to-nature bushman. His mentality,
like that of so many other men with the capacity to
garner in and hang on to large sums of money, was
of an absurdly inferior order. He was illiterate as
the modern Australian schoolboy of 15 understands
the term. In the intervals of his business deals, life
was to him one long Jack Harkaway adventure. He
loved to be pointed at in the streets, on pub veran-
dahs and the like, as “bluff old Jimmy Tyson, the
multi-millionaire; the simple, unaffected bush bloke,
with no d—d nonsense about him.” A Bathurst
hotelkeeper swore to me that he once saw Tyson
retire to the back-yard of a shanty where he had put
in the night, and bestrew his hat and coat with
dust, a servant who was unused to his ways having
brushed the garments with an eye to profit. This
stunt corresponds exactly, if it is viewed aright, to
the nut’s anxiety to have his silk hat properly
ironed on Cup day, and to the nut’s best girl’s
determination to have a good go at the powder-puff
in the retiring-room at Government House during a
ball. Dust and disreputable-looking clothes were
an essential part of the Tyson make-up. If he had
been forcibly washed ; put into a plain, clean, blue-
serge suit; compelled to wear the unobtrusive boots
that normal men wear; and in other respects made
to resemble one of the Herd, he would have been
as miserable as an earl in bowyangs.
He never read a decent* book; he never gave utter-
ance to a thought of the least value on any social
or political or economic or literary question; and if
he ever did a generous deed, or thought a generous
thought, the world has yet to hear of it on oath—
no mere assurance will suffice. No one will ever
know what went on in Tyson’s mind during those
long, romantic night-rides through the bush that
Randolph mentions. However, if one may estimate
such things by their fruits, his thoughts chiefly centred
on (1) the amount of money he was going to make
out of the particular transaction he had in hand;
(2) his chances of cutting *down the salary of some
poor devil of an employee by a weekly shilling or
so, while continuing to retain the said employee in
his service; (3) the impression he would create at
Scott’s or the Australia or Daniell’s when he next
arrived there in costume—which is to say, unbathed
and .grimy, and theatrically under-dressed. A man
who, like Tyson, deliberately under-dresses for effect
is just as fatuously foppish, at heart, as he who
over-dresses in the same spirit. The dandyism of a
newly-arrived remittance-man only differs from that
of a Tyson inasmuch as the former’s is mostly of a
more sanitary nature. Tyson was a great money-
getter; a great sweater; the “closest” man I ever
knew; and what one may term a Brummel of the
gutter, or, alternatively, a D’Orsay of the old clo’
shop. No; his certainly wasn’t a character to put
into the Lives of our National Saints.
An ancient R.C. cleric passed out at
Hobart on Friday in bis 89th year. This
was Monsignor Hoyne, a Kilkenny man,
who, after his collegiate course in Ireland,
went to Calcutta to the R.C. College as pro-
fessor of mathematics. In 1851 he came to
Melbourne, and was assistant-priest at St.
Francis’s, Lonsdale-street, the first R.C.
church built in Melbourne. Two years later
he went to Heidelberg, then to Geelong,
then to Warrnambool, to Hamilton, and to
Ballarat. In 1899 he visited Tasmania and
remained there. He was chaplain to the
order of the Good Shepherd, and did duty
till three months ago. The oldest R.C. cleric
in N. S. Wales is Father P. Birch, who is
the last of that church to draw a State
stipend. He was enrolled for it in 1859.
“Chudah”: John Freeman-Mit-
ford, fourth son of Baron Redes-
dale, has captured the richest
heiress in Germany—Miss Fuld,
who will have five millions sterling
some day. Fuld is that rare bird,
a Chi-istian Yid. You do find
them now and then. Last year
Moderator Alexander, head of the
Victorian Presbyterian Assembly,
gave the right hand of fellowship
to one Ettmann, a converted Jew,
who labors to bring others in.
Rabbis Abrahams, Lenzer and
Danglow joined in a letter of pro-
test to Alexander. He, in re-
sponse. gave an overwhelming list
of well-known Jews who had been
converted, such families as the
Mendelssohns, Disraelis, etc. The
Rabbis then declared that none of
these were real conversions. They
pointed out that the Society for
the Conversion of the Jews ex-
pended about £BOO per convert,
and even then the outcome was
dubious. Moses Mendelssohn was
the greatest modern Jew to aban-
don the Hebrew faith. The con-
verted branch took the name of
Mendelssohn-Bartholdy. Most of
the converts are made among the
poorest class of Sheenies. They
take the money of the edified prose-
lytisers; use it: _to start pawnbrokeries,
money-lending joints, bookmaking busi-
nesses, etc., and, having connected with
affluence, hurry straight back to the Syna-
gogue. A Jew and a Catholic are two types
that can rarely be convinced of the error of
their ways.
An incident of interest occurred at a
river outing given on the jubilee of the
arrival of five Methody ministers who
arrived at Sydney on January 14, 1864.
(All five are still on top. but four only at-
tended the outing, the fifth, the Rev. Dr.
Manning, having joined the Church of Eng-
land soon after arrival.) The steamer, be-
fore heading for Correy’s Gardens, turned
into Lane Cove River, in order to give a
cheer to J. R. Houlding (“Boomerang”),
old-time author of “Christopher Cockle’s
Capers,” who resides on the waterside at
Woolwich. On the lawn in front of a pic-
turesque cottage stood the ancient tattler,
now 92 years of age, and he waved his re-
sponse to the greetings.
Biographical note re a strenuous Sydney
character: —
W. Scott Fell, who was entertained by some sup-
porters last week, contested a N. Sydney seat as an
Independent at the recent N. S. Wales elections, and
got bumped out. Previously to that he had fought
the Coal Vend, and encountered disaster ; likewise
a number of caitiffs who were avid to bankrupt
him: not to mention an army of other enemies.
W. Scott Fell is a resurgent sort of person. He
has now got the Broken Hill coal contract. The
Vend is still furiously hostile to him, and ic would
far sooner supply him with a dose of arsenic than
with the ships which he requires to carry out his
undertaking, so; he is chartering the necessary vessels
in England. With the help of these craft he and his
friends anticipate confidently that he will presently
garner in a cargo of bullion. Scott Fell also got the
Broken Hill contract some years ago. The Vend
was horrified at the news. It saw in his move an
attempt to destroy Private Enterprise by interfering
with its monopoly. In the end, and after much
embittered parleying, it bought him out —it is said
for the tidy sum of £lO,OOO. Also, he got the
contract on another occasion. But this time the
Vend refrained from buying him out, and he lost
money over the deal. Yet another time the Vend
thought that he was tendering, and to defeat him that
organisation tendered low. The monopolists got the
contract, and dropped good red gold over it, only to
find later that the exasperating Fell had not com-
peted at all. His bankruptcy experiences were char-
acteristic. Justice Street, of N. S. Wales, put it
across him right savagely. After Street had finished
his comments on Fell’s methods, the man in the
tram might have been excused for concluding that
the bankrupt was commercially outed for life. How-
ever, Fell went on to the Hisrh Court, and in that
august jurisdiction he was whitewashed purer than
the driven snow. Had the Hiarh Court failed him, he
would have appealed to the Privy Council, or Inter-
national Law. or to the arbitrament of War or Revolu-
tion. or to Heaven. He is that sort of man. Mean-
while his old foe, the Vend, appears to be wilting.
The famous judgment of Isaacs, of the High Court,
hit it hard, and, furthermore, some of the members
are understood to be distrustful of various other
members. By the present look of things, Scott Fell
will be an affluent pillar of society when the Vend
is, figuratively speaking, spreading its S. M. Herald
out preparatory to a nocturnal camp in the Domain.
Acting-Premier “ BISHOP ” BARNES,
of Brisbane.
Edmund Barton, of the High Court, was
65 years old on Sunday.
Colonel Yernon, who was N. S. Wales
Government Architect (or “Colonial” Archi-
tect, as the queer old title went) from 1890,
died on January 17 after a long struggle.
As a final expedient the doctors amputated
the old man’s leg, 10 days before his death;
but even that heroic treatment was un-
availing—as, indeed, the victim expressed a
hope that it might be, if the thing had to be
done. The late official retired from the
service some three years ago, when Griffith
reconstructed the Works Department from
top to bottom, demolishing the job of
Colonial Architect in the process. After
that he practised his profession in Sydney.
His title of Colonel was derived from his
connection with the old Sydney Lancers,
which he helped to raise. It was a high-
toned institution, though very unhandy with
its weapons, and it looked very well when
its horse didn’t shake it up too much.
B. J. Fink, a paper millionaire of the
Melbourne boom days, was gifted with a
saturnine humor. Here is the story of one
effort amongst the many which led up to the
deceased financier paying a half-penny in the
£on nearly £2,000,000. He called on a lean,
lank banker one morning, and asked him
whether he would take £50,000 of Mrs. Fink’s
money on fixed deposit at 5 per cent. This
was a shade over current rates, and the
banker demurred. “Besides,” he said, “I
might not be able to lend the money quickly
and safely, and the bank would be losing the
interest till I got a good security for it.”
But in the end Fink triumphed, and got out
with )<is wife’s fixed deposit receipt. Next
day, L.J. turned un smiling, and said to
the bank manager: “I thay, I’ve got a very
vood theecuritv for £50,000 at 51 per cent.
It’th a very nitlie thity propputy, and they
vant the money on mortgage for thix yearth
at five and a harf. Can you do it?” Finally,
the deed was done, and the bank was one of
13 institutions that received a ha’penny in
the £. The fixed deposit was subsequently
repaid in full.
THE MISSION MERMAID AND THE CURATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A remarkable change in the attitude of the
churches towards surf-bathing is being illustrated by
those now attending the Church Missionary Associa-
tion’s summer school at Austinmer. There are about
300 present, including eminent church dignitaries.
Surf-bathing is one of the chief attractions indulged
in twice daily, venerable clerics being seen shooting
the breakers with their daughters and prominent
lady workers.— Sydney Sun.
The canons shoot in ev’ry wave,
And lady workers splash;
It seems quite reckless to behave
In this way when we’ve souls to save—
Quite reckless, yes, and rash!
But there is. something in the air
That makes me think it’s right.
(Oh, see that lady worker there !. . . .
Tut, tut! I really mustn’t stare 1
That impulse I must fight!)
I never dreamed there were such curves
In my own Bible class!
It sends a tremor through my nerves,
As ev’ry shapely student swerves,
When to and fro they pass.
I try each night to keep my mind
Upon the work in hand,
But figures on the page black-lined
Keep romping till with dreams I’m blind
And wand’ring on the sand.
I cannot think that it is wrong
To gaze at milk-white knees,
And note that Someone’s hair is long!
She has a hand-clasp firm and strong,
When currents bump and tease.
It’s quite intoxicating—quite
To see her sunburnt nose
And red lips from the foam appear!
She is my own—my gospel dear!
To-night I shall propose!
N.S.W.

What is expected some day may be judged by the
fact that, though this bank pays no dividend, shares
are now quoted at well above par. And no dividend
can be paid for some time to come. The chairman
12 months ago mentioned seven years, though he
added that it might be a little less. But he
wouldn’t bind himself dr anybody else to anything.
The beast in the path is a certain amount of money
which the bank has to repay to old depositors before
the shareholders can get a bite. When the Govern-
ment of 25 to 30 years ago listened to the tempter
who said (just as was said to Denham lately) that
the State couldn’t borrow too much, there was, of
course, a beautiful boom, in which this and other
banks joined. Then the absurd people wanted their
money back ; and as a lot of their money was lent
on property at inflated values, and as the desire to
purchase properties at inflated or any other values
had fallen dead, the money wasn’t available to do
the repaying with. Hence the people who had lent
this bank their money were offered three-fourths in
inscribed 3§ p.c. stock ; the other one-fourth they
simply had to trust the bank to repay out of future
profits, and meantime it carried no interest at all.
The money which had been lent by the Government
to prop the institution up was, after variqus changes,
left in at £2 12s. 6d. p.c., the full principal being
repayable by instalments up to 1921, It didn’t look
such a very liberal settlement, from the, bank’s point
of view, at the time; but many things (including
the money market) have changed since Queensland
found itself sitting in the gutter outside the pub,
nursing a dreadful head and feeling quite sure that
the earth would never look green any more. Even
the sugar business, which was never to smile again
after Tommy Tanna left, has never laughed so joy-
ously as it has done during the past few years. Con-
sequently, it is quite possible that some of the old
assets, representing borrowed money on which the
bank itself is paying either nothing at all or only
£2 12s. fid. p c., have been earning very good
money for the bank. In other cases, no doubt, those
assets have been sold at good prices, and the money
put into active and profitable business.
At any rate, the bank has been so flush of
money that it has been pre-paying the debt to the
Government; but this, of course, is not out of pro-
fits. The whole position with regard to the ancient
debts of all kinds is shown in the next table: —•
Dec., June, Dec.,
1897. 1913. 1913.
Old debt to Govern-
ment .. .. £1,833,327 £218,293 £198,293
Stock (fixed debt
to old deposi-
tors) .. . . 3,116,621 3,091,669 3,091,669
Debt to old de-
positors payable out
of profits . . 1.038,974 629,283 591,121
It will be noticed that 9d. in the £ of the old
debt to private depositors, repayable out of profits,
called at the last distribution for £38,162. That
would mean an original debt of £1,017,650, or
about £21.000 less than the figures given in 1897.
From this it looks as if some of the old trouble has
been bought right up, no doubt at a stiff discount;
and such a transaction also suggests a hidden stock-
ing somewhere on the premises.
As for current business, take these figures:—

Watch the growth of the new deposits—pretty good
proof of what the men with money think of the
solidity of the concern nowadays. And this paper
certainly isn’t going to suggest to them that their
confidence is at all misplaced. A bank which is liable
to be called on for £6,915,625 to satisfy debts of
all sorts, and has £4,330,373 in hand to start doing it
with, isn’t likely to give depositors any anxiety.
If Queensland had the business to offer, and of a
quality of which the once-bitten-twice-shy manage-
ment could approve, the bank has resources sufficient
to justify it in taking another £1,000,000 worth of
advances at least.
The paid capital is £413,418. A 10 p.c. div. on
that amount would call for £20,671 per half-year.
On the figures in the first table the Q.N. should be
able to do that before breakfast. Very likely some
day, following the precedent lately set by the Bank
of New Zealand, the capital will be increased. The
business certainly calls for more. It is with such
things in their eye that people are offering beyond par
for shares in a co. which, on present appearances,
won’t give a dividend for six or seven years. What
can a man afford to pay for a share which will give
10 p.c. after seven years? In seven years 100 £3
shares then starting to give 10 p.c. will be worth,
say, £5OO, on a 6 p.c. return basis, and £2OO is
fine interest on £3OO for seven years, or on even
£325 —655. per share. (Much depends, of course, on
the state of the money market at the end of seven
years ; but except in very rare circumstances one cannot
expect to buy so as to get more than 6 p.c. on first-
class stock.) Put another way, if a man buys at
605., holds for seven years, and wants to show 7.
p.c. compound for that time, he would not be able
to sell for less than £4 16s. If for the shares for
which he will get £5OO after seven years, he pays
£355 now—that is, 7ls.—he will make 5 p.c. p.a.
compound. Whether, however, he will sell the Q.N.
£3 shares for £5 when they start to pay 10 p.c! is
a matter on which The Bulletin declines to offer
an opinion. It has run out of prophecies for the
moment.
