Of course, there was no obligation on the part of the
management to do it; but as soon as ever the direc-
tors had seen that they could pay an extra 1 p.c.
and had decided on that course, an official intima-
tion might reasonably have been given to share-
holders through the press. As it is, some-
body who evidently had a very good idea that
something was going to happen, started to buy up
the scrip of shareholders who didn’t know anything,
with the result that during the week before the
accounts were issued shares put on 15s. It may be
all right, no doubt, but it doesn’t look well—it
looks too much like the leaking-out of information
and the making use of inside knowledge for Stock
Exchange purposes. The bank always has been
secretive. For years and years nobody outside has
known just what sort of progress it was making out
of the mess it got into at the time of the big boom.
At that time it didn’t straighten things up at the
expense of capital, as most of the others did; it
relied upon future profits to fill up the big hole
(size unknown) caused by the shrinkage in the
value of its securities. All along it has professed
to have some reserves—that is to say, something in
excess of its capital. Yet even now, in spite of the
raising of the dividend to 6 p.c., the market declines
to value the shares at par, but writes the capital
down from £400,000 to about £300,000. If, as
some people say, the assets have been restored
and are worth 20s. in the £, a statement to that
effect from the responsible people ought to do the
bank a lot of good. A bank paying 6 p.c. ought
not to see its stock from 20 p.c. to 25 p.c. below
par. It may be very stupid of either depositors or
people who own, or want to own, overdrafts to take
any notice of the price of a bank’s shares ; but they
do. “There is something wrong here,” they say.
But how much there is wrong they don’t know ; and,
as usual, they are afraid of the unknown. If the
directors of the City Bank of Sydney can say that,
on a careful review of securities, they ( are con-
vinced that there is now no over-valuation, or no
over-valuation not covered by internal or contin-
gency funds, shares will probably recover to some-
where near par; and this paper believes that that
circumstance would do the business good.
The movements in the past year and the present
position are shown next: —-
Dec., 1912. Dec., 1913.
Deposits .. £1,902,886 £1,981,432 Inc. £78,546
Notes . . . . 10,386 7,765 Dec. 2,621
Coin, etc. . . 411,357 555,239 Inc. 143,882
Public securities 92,000 92,000
Advances . . 1,654,802 • 1,593,491 Dec. 61,311
Premises . . 192,729 194,617 Inc. 1,888
So the bank owes to depositors and others £1,989,197.
Assuming that the capital and reserves are intact
(and The Bulletin is not disposed to think that
there is now very much the matter with them), this
means that shareholders have £1 in the business
for every £4 12s. of the public’s. If there was any
assurance that the capital and reserves are intact,
the bank could safely take much more public money
than that. As at December 31 the bank had in-
creased its holding of liquid assets to £647,239.
Its liabilities being £1,989,197, it thus held nearly
33 p.c. of them in liquid shapes at December 31.
This is not a heavy holding of coin, etc. Still, the
proportion of liquid assets which a bank ought to
hold depends largely upon the current account deposits
in proportion to the total of deposits. The larger
the proportion of money that is liable to be called
for any day, the larger, of course, should the pro-
portion of liquid assets be. As the proportion of this
bank’s current account money is probably not very
large, the position with regard to liquid assets
may be better than it looks.
While the long job of straightening things up has
been in hand, the bank hasn’t been a very progressive
concern. A sort of settled gloom seemed to
over the premises. The declared profits are now,
however, equal to about 74 p.c. on the capital; and
if this and the raising of the dividend to 6 p.c. can
be taken to mean that all but perhaps the tail and
hoofs of the old dead horse have been shifted, a
little more vigor might be infused. Perhaps the
bank knows its own business best; still in these days
of fine exchange profits it does, for instance, seem
like throwing money away not to have a branch m
London.
The Commonwealth Brass and Metal
Foundries. Ltd. (Sydney).—Early last year the
first accounts of this “product of the recent company -
floating boom” were discussed like this: —•
“The co. has a capital of £ 26,950, of which
£23,021 is paid; and no less than £12,716 was
sucked up for certain patent rights. Add to that
paper asset £1348 for preliminary exes. ; total
froth, £14,064, plus probably something spent on
altering the co.’s business premises. To the end of
November last a loss of £998 was shown on the
working of the patents, which were alleged to be
worth £12,716. The shareholders didn’t like the
look of things, so they asked the directors to see
them again in three months’ time. As the accounts
for that period show a further loss of £434 on
trading, without any allowance for depreciation,
shareholders are invited to meet at the end of this
month to vote a bonus to the vendors or something.
The co. has already accumulated an overdraft of
£llO9, and owes £1434 on other accounts, and the
creditors are probably not as affable as could be
wished.”
Nobody is very affable now. Some time during
the year W. E. Downing, a director of John Allerdice
& Co. Pty., Ltd., a Victorian foundry firm, became
managing director, and in return for a certain allot-
ment of shares was to give his services for 12
months and put the resources of the Allerdice estab-
lishment at the disposal of the co. Apparently there
was trouble right away, and now, in a circular
accompanying the co.’s second balance-sheet, the
managing director issues statements which ought to
make some of his fellow-directors sit up and take
notice. One director (specified) is alleged to have
taken strong exception to the co. sending its traveller
into “the suburb which he seemingly considers his
close preserve,” this director being in “the whole-
sale plumbing trade”; another director, it was
reported, “was endeavoring to purchase shares at a
low price,” and strongly resented the issue of an
official circular recommending shareholders not to
part with their shares until they had certain forth-
coming information put before them; and an
opinion of counsel (J. A. Browne) is fur-
nished to the effect that there is “very
strong evidence that the vendors knew their
Australian patent was bad,” and that “the contract
between the vendors and the co. is voidable at the
option of the co., which may elect to ratify it on
any terms as to return of shares or otherwise as the
directors think best.” From this it appears that the
patent rights, which represent £12,720 of the cap-
ital of £29,607, are no rights at all, and are
apparently worth nil. The position according to the
published balance-sheet is as follows: —
Capital .. .. .. .. £29,607
Less Patent rights .. .. . .£12,720
Prelim, exes. . . .. . . 1,092
W. E. Downing’s services . . 3,100
Alterations to premises . . 298
P. & L. balance (less forfeited
capital) .. . . ■ . . 3,660
Liabilities .. . . . . 1,417
22,287
Tangible assets to represent a
capital of £29,607 . . . . £7,320
Apparently 598 shares of £5O each have been issued,
of which 240 seem to have gone to the vendors.
If counsel’s opinion is sound, and if the vendors
sold to the co. patent rights which did not exist,
and if, as he advises, the contract may be voided and
the vendors’ shares cancelled —that, at any rate,
seems to be the effect of his advising—there would
be 358 shares, and the assets would show about £2O
each for the £5O put in. But it appears that some
more shares are due to Downing, which would re-
duce the value of the tangible assets per share. And
whether the tangible assets are worth 20s. in the £
is exceedingly doubtful. Plant and machinery arc
down for £6030; and,' according to a report made
by one of Allerdice’s men, part at least of the outfit
is of little value. Add to all this that the directors
are quarrelling violently among themselves, and the
best The Bulletin can do for shareholders is to
oiler them its sympathy. It might also throw in
the advice never to buy a “patent right” until the
patent has been granted ; but probably it isn’t neces-
sary so far' as they are concerned. Yet there are
people who, evidently, are prepared to do it. Only
the other day a prospectus was issued in which the
public was invited to put up a large sum of money
to buy a “patent” which was ohly an application for
a patent.

As a matter of fact, all the shares are ordinaries
now. Jhere used to be £17,000 of 6 p.c. pref.
capital, represented by what were called “A” shares ;
but these were exchanged for “B’s,” or ordinaries, on
payment of the bonus mentioned a few lines from the
bottom of the table. Taking all things into account,
the co. has done remarkably well during the year.
The Metropole is a travellers’ house, and travelling to
Sydney had its disadvantages during those months
when the visitor had to be vaccinated before he could
get out of the State. Also, no doubt, the higher cost
of living has affected the price of hotel as well as
private provender and attendance. That the co.
should have made a profit greater than that of any
year prior to 1912 therefore shows careful manage-
ment. And you see the same prudence in the com-
paratively large amount put to reserves, notwith-
standing that, owing to the increase of ordinary
capital, the 10 p.c. div. has called for more money
than hitherto. Still, even now, the capital account
( £120,000) is not high. For many years it was
extraordinarily low, the result of the bargain price
at which the present co. took over the property and
other assets from the Melbourne co. which formerly
owned them. The property still stands in the books
at the £lOO,OOO which it cost the co. plus £4OOO
spent on additions; but furniture, extensions, etc.,
seem to be mostly paid for out of current revenue,
although £14,500 of the reserves stand to the
credit of various funds to provide for special replace-
ments or to pay for upkeep in lean years when the
trotter will be scarce. Meantime the co. maintains
a nice, solid amount of liquid assets to provide for
such contingencies ; liabilities of all kinds amount to
only £2921, while assets, apart from the freehold,
machinery and furniture, total £29,860 —mostly hard
cash. With the recent building of two or three
other fair-sized residential hotels the Metropole may
suffer more competition that it has been bothered
with in the past; but until Australia has one of its
slumps this aspect of the case doesn’t look as
though it would seriously bother the co. The fact
is that, despite the fairiy quick growth of popula-
tion, hotel licenses are gradually growing less,
and there isn’t enough sure profit in the residential
business nowadays to tempt people to launch out
into a large expenditure on premises that have the
sword of Local Option hanging over them.

This co. has had an unusual run of profits. In older
days, when it was a meat packing and freezing
concern and nothing else, the extreme variations in
the seasons of Queensland made all the difference
between profit and loss. When stock was scarce,
after a drought or other trouble with the cattle,
prices for live stock ruled so high that there was
no profit in the export trade. But then a few
things happened, to the great advantage of this co.
First, the American Meat Trust either squeezed or
frightened so many American meat-growers out of
the business that the U.S., instead of being big
exporters, can’t produce enough for their own stom-
achs. The operations of the Trust have in this and
other ways pushed up the average price of meat in
England; and an uninterrupted run of good seasons
and a comparative absence of redwater and other
troubles has enabled the co. to get the supplies
to sell at the good prices. Moreover, in the poor
times of 10 vears ago, the co. plunged in Northern
station properties to the extent of about £150,000.
Robert Philp, who is an incurable optimist, was, no
doubt, largely responsible for this move; and things
worked out this time very much as the optimist had
foreseen. True, the directors say this year that
the profits from the stations have “not been as satis-
factory as was anticipated”; but, then, we don’t
know how satisfactory it was anticipated they would
be. All that is certain is this: just as for some
time a few years ago there wasn’t any business in
Australia which showed such profits as lamb-raising,
so now there isn’t any with larger profits than
cattle-raising, and the outlook for at least a few
years ahead is particularly rosy. Anywav, the co.
has made slashing profits out of something. The
paid capital is £191,062, and the visible net profits
in the past four years have been £143,338, and you
can’t want much better than that, especially as the
co. is risking no capital but its own; its entire
liabilities amount to only £88,750, while its cash,
stores, book-debts and unsold products total £128,890.
And besides the visible profits, the co. has very
likely made a nice bit which has been written off or
set aside against contingencies. Factories, pastoral
properties, live stock and shares in other cos. are
lumped in the balance-sheet at £322,460 “after
providing for depreciation,” and in a case of this
kind the proviso is- elastic enough to stretch from
Dan to Bally Bally. If, however, the directors
haven’t seized their opportunity to do some hard
writing-down (which can be accomplished partly by
taking in the natural stock increase at a nominal
figure), they know less about how to make them-
selves snug than they ought to with their experi-
ence.

A remarkably solid concern is this pioneer among
“provincial” trustee cos. of Australia. Of its capital
of £20,000 and its reserves of £17,146, no less
than £33,695 is in cash and public securities, after
allowing for the dividend; the trifling balance is
represented by mortgages ( £1500), freehold pre-
mises (which cost £2612, and have been written
down to £1800) and etceteras. If the investments
bring in only 4 p.c. all round, they produce enough
to pay 7 p.c. on the capital, including the £5OOO
which a while ago was transferred from reserves.
And there is a very fair business, too, on which to
earn commissions; trust estates in hand are valued
at £1,789,808, as against £1,369,749 five years ago.
The man who wants to see a quick 25 p.c. rise in the
market value of his scrip can’t be recommended to
bother about inquiring into this or any other
trustee co. But there are very few concerns which
offer more solid security for a man’s pfincipal and
have better chances of steadily improving their busi-
ness and their profits.
Great Cobar, Ltd.: —
Dec., 1907 .. Profit £188,931 Div. lli p.c.

The P. and L. balance brought forward from the
previous year was £34,356 ; total to be dealt with,
£76,273. This amount has been appropriated as
follows: —•
Spent on concentrating plant.. .. •• £48,414
Written off (repurchased debentures) .. 27,859
Total ..£76,273
Nominally the co. writes off £57,500, debentures
that face value having been bought for £51,068.
This £57,500 is made up of £50,000 written off
underwriting commission and £7500 off trifles, in-
cluding £2500 spent on establishing a butchery
and bakery—two of general-manager Bellinger’s ideas
for keeping the cost of living within bounds at
Cobar, and thus protecting himself against demands
for further pay. Nothing has been written off plant
directly; but the £48,414 spent on the new con-
centrating plant, and not charged to capital account,
is an indirect writing-off. Once more, of course, the
shareholders get nothing. They have had nothing
since shortly after the co. was formed, in 1907,
and they ought not to have got anything then.
Now, why? The Great Cobar had been worked
for some years by a syndicate, consisting of the
Longworths, A. J. Gould, Dr. Read, and two or
three others, and it had made rich men of them all.
Moreover, it is quite well known that the mine was
still making good profits when it was sold to some
speculators who passed it on to a London-Paris co.
The capitalisation was absurd, partly because the
promoters’ cut and other exes, were so monstrous,
but that doesn’t explain the failure to pay any
dividend at all. A mine shouldn’t make any the
less profit because the co.’s capital is £1,000,000
when it ought to be only £500,000. The only differ-
ence ought to be that the dividend per cent, is
reduced by half. Certainly we have to remember the
debentures; the co., which began with £550,000,
had increased them at the end of 1909 to £757,000,
but has since reduced them to £667,300 by repur-
chases. The truth is that, in order to justify the
monstrous cut which the promoters were getting out
of the flotation, the most extravagant estimates were
made regarding the mine; and somehow or other
those estimates were supported by technical men who,
to say the least of it, should have known better.
Incidentally, since it was notorious that the mine
hadn’t been making anything like the estimated pro-
fits, it became necessary to discredit the then-existing
plant and the management. With new men and new
machines they were going to produce copper at a
profit if the metal fell to £45 per ton. Kendall
said so even at the end of the first year.
When the expected results didn’t turn up, Blake-
more, who had been general manager for the syndi-
cate, and remained on with the blQated co., was
sacked. That was what it meant, anyway. It
wasn’t the new plant's fault, of course —the time
hadn’t yet arrived for cursing the new plant. Ken-
dall struggled on awhile ; and then he —who had been
responsible for the plant—-was got rid of, too. That,
however, seemed only a fair thing. Then came Bel-
linger, from America, with a fine reputation as a
smelter. Now Bellinger is going. This paper has
always had a lot of sympathy for Bellinger. The big
mistake he made was in not telling the cold, hard
truth as soon after he arrived as possible. A more
crafty man would have taken the gloomiest view of
the position. “The case, is a very serious one,
gentlemen, but I think I may be able to pull you
through, as long as you don’t expect too much.
Almost any doctor or any lawyer would have told
him the thing to say when meeting a client or a
patient (they mean the same thing) in difficulties.
But Bellinger is a big, comfortable man who hates
to make anybody else uncomfortable. So he said, in
effect: “Oh, things are not so bad. Buck up —
we’ll soon be all right!” Perhaps he mightn’t have
been quite as cheerful if he had realised his diffi-
culties. But how could he,-a man just dropped into
Australia, with nothing much but misleading reports
to go on so far as the mine, etc., were concerned?
Even when he had suffered many disappointments,
and made the shareholders suffer with him, he didn’t
turn off the tap of his excessive optimism. Thus he
promised that the new concentrating mill would run
700 tons a day, whereas “the latest advices are that
Mr. Laird expects shortly to be able to treat 500
tons dailv.” When Pellew Harvey, a mining
man on the Board, was sent out last year to look
round, the conclusions he came to were, briefly,
these: —
(1) “Tlie general manager has failed to appreciate
the importance of selecting the best men possible
to supplement his efforts.”

(2) “The general manager should have more fully
appreciated the difficulties, and made them clearer
to the directors and shareholders.”
(3) “Administrative qualities of exceptional char-
acter, apart from those of a technical nature, are
needed in one responsible for the control of such an
important enterprise. In my opinion, without wish-
ing to reflect, I feel that reorganisation of the entire
staff and a change in the general management of the
co. is necessary, a particular qualification being an
intimate knowledge of Australian labor and condi-
tions in that country as a whole.”
The Bulletin has been putting it that way for a
couple of years past. And at last the Board agrees
and appoints 8., M. and Co. as managers, and
“under this arrangement, G. C. Klug, 8., M. and
Co.’s general manager, will take control at an early
date.” Few men know Australian conditions better.
The reconstruction of the co. is no doubt desirable.
Under the trust deed the co. must redeem £lOO,OOO
of debentures every year at 105 before paying a
dividend. This is far too onerous an undertaking.
The mine is capable of a long life, but not of a
dazzlingly profitable one. The redemption of the
debentures might, therefore, reasonably be spread
over a longer period. Also the capital not repre-
sented by tangible assets ought to be written off,
so that the big appropriations towards the cost of old
mistakes may be done with, once and for all. This
may help everybody to realise that the thing is a
plain bread-and-butter proposition, without too much
butter; and when nobody has anything to gain by
pretending that it is anything else, a heap of money
may be saved, and a heap more disappointment pre-
vented.

The long jump the profits have taken from the
figures for the previous half-year is due to (1) an
increase of about £3OO in the rents, etc., and (2)
the heavy outlay on repairs during the early part of
last year. For a long while nothing was written
off for depreciation of the, building wherein the
wool and coal and shipping people most do con-
gregate, the idea being apparently that the unearned
increment was racing ahead. Also, accountants have
struck a new excuse for not writing down pre-
mises. The cost of building has been mounting,
they say, at the rate of about 10 p.c. p.a. for the
past five years, so that a building which was worth
£20,000 in 1908 would cost £30,000 now; hence,
if the building stays in the balance-sheet at £20,000,
it has really been written down £lO,OOO in five
years. It is ingenious; but the trouble is that
some day the premises will have to be renewed, and
(if prices don’t fall again) they will have to be
renewed at the higher price. Hence the increase
in the cost of building doesn’t do away with the
necessity for a rebuilding fund. This view of the
matter evidently appeals to this co. ; wherefore,
though its principal tenant had lately to make large
additions to secure a renewal of a lease, the co. is
now writing down its premises. And there is plenty
of surplus profit to do it with, since only 44 p.c. of
the half-year’s earnings are divided among share-
holders. If things go on like this one wouldn't be
surprised if the gilt-edged old concern got back to
the 7 p.c. which was paid for many years prior to
1902.
“Vital Spark”: What has become of the
Meteor Igniter Co.? It was to supersede electric
sparking in explosion engines, and up to now I
cannot find that it has even been successfully demon-
strated. I took shares 18 months or so ago, under-
standing that the thing was a proved success, and
I’d like to know which is responsible for the long
delay—the thing itself or the management.
General-manager Bellinger, of Grea,t
Cobar, writes that the meeting of shareholders in
London in December had nothing to do with his
resignation, which was forwarded to the directors
last September. Though 8., M. and C. have formally
taken control, Bellinger will remain in charge till
February. The introduction of 8., M. and Co., and
the large powers conferred on them, have caused a
stir in the camp. G. E. Baker, a director, who
was sent out from London to look after the business
side of the undertaking, has resigned.
From Cobar: Occidental’s manager Norris
is reported to be. leaving shortly. This co. is up
against a tough proposition in its lower ore bodies,
notwithstanding the increased contents, regularly re-
ported to the press. Also, it is said that Great
Cobar’s recent importation, G. A. Laird, of Mexico,
has left, and will probably not return The Chesney
nlant, which cost over £50,000. has been hung up.
If, as stated, it is a success, why is this so? Surely
it wasn’t put there to look at!
On top of the announcement of the pro-
nosed hvdro-electric scheme for the Grafton Copper
Mine. (N.S.W.) comes news of further labor troubles
resulting in the closing down of the. property. For
the past 12 months the once-flourishing co. has had
troublous times, due mainly to shortage of payable
ore, a state of affairs largely brought about by
neglect of development work, especially when all was
prosperity. The outlook is none too bright, more
especially if the water-power scheme be persisted in.
A large supply of optimism is necessary to proceed
with such a proposal in view of the depleted ore
reserves, the somewhat uncertain and not too large
water supply for power purposes, and the cost of
plant and transmission of power over seven or eight
miles of rough country from Falls to mine.
* * * *
The Bourke’s Hill Sluicing Co. (Emma-
ville, N.S.W.)' reports, as usual, that both plants are
sluicing with average prospects. The recent rains,
have assured the water supply for some time ahead.
The time is, however, approaching when the question
of removing the plant to a fresh site will have to be
considered. Although a fairly substantial reserve
has been established to cover the cost of removal, it
is possible that dividends may be affected for the
time being. The unkind behavior of the tin market
and the possibility of a decrease in tin values of the
ground also point to the likelihood of diminished
profits. * * *
“Ballarat” : What are these incompetents
of the lodide (N.S.W.) talking about? Imagine a
pumping difficulty in the dry Western country! At
Daylesford (Vic.) there is water worth talking
about, yet by ' carrying a tank under the cage the
Ajax is hauling a million gallons a day; and by
bottling up the shaft above, it is down 200 ft. below
the working levels. And here is the lodide sinking
7ft or so in half as many months! lodide wants
development, but it seems to want management more
than anything else. # ,
Westralia produced 31,3860 z. more gold
in 1913 than in 1912, and thus broke the fall in
output that started nine years ago. Kalgoorlie, as
usual, furnished more than half of the yield, but
the percentage is slowly but surely being reduced.
Some mines there still have “millions’ lh sight,
but all are wasting and several have galloping con-
sumption. The fabulously rich Oroya shoot has
passed away ; the Lake View Consols, Associated,
Boulder Perseverance and South Kalgurli have been
depleted of their best; Boulder has one foot in the
grave; Ivanhoe is off color in depth, and as a big
producer Kalgurli’s day is past. The best Kalgoorlie
developments of the year were the intersection of some
high-grade values at a depth of 2000 ft. in the Lake
View lease, the opening-up of the south-western ex-
tension of the Horseshoe No. 1 lode at 1.600 ft. in
Chaffers, and the discovery of one or two good makes
of ore at depth in the Horseshoe. There is nothing
startling about any of these finds, but they may
lead to better things. To the end of 1913 the field
had produced £62,630,877 worth of bullion, of
which 10 mines (Horseshoe, Boulder, Ivanhoe, Per-
severance, Oroya-Brown Hill, Lake View, Kalgurli,
Associated, South Kalgurli, and Associated Northern)
were responsible for £51,729,647. Yilgarn did_ the
best among the other fields, while Murchison,
which occupied next place on the production list,
also showed up fairly well compared with the figures
for 1912. Meekatharra was again the main source
of the Murchison output. The principal additions
to the State’s producers in 1913 were Bullfinch Prop.,
Corinthian North and Edna May, all at Yilgarn.
These tluree shows are together crushing about 10.000'
tons & month for a return of, roughly, £20,000.
BullflapJdJ- may rediuce its yield at any time, but both
the Edna May and Corinthian intend to increase'
their outputs before long. Dividends for the year
totalled £909,387, made up mostly as follows: Great;
Boulder, £262,500; Ivanhoe, £170,000; Kalgurli.
£96.000; Golden Horseshoe, £60,000; Sons of
Gwalia, £64.000; Yuanmi, £43,750; Associated’
Northern, £35,000; Boulder Perseverance, £34,986;
Bullfinch Prop., £23,807; Sand Queen, £21,000 ;
Lake View and Star, £32,000 ; Mararoa, £15.000;
Associated, £12,384 ; Great Fingall, £12,500 7
Black Range, £7250; Edna May, £4125 ; Golden.
Ridge, £2318; Burbank’s Main Lode, £1767. Pro-
duction of other minerals was well in advance of the
previous year’s figures. Copper and silver-lead showed 1
substantial increases; tin just about held its own..
Collie’s coal industry went ahead, and if not ham-
pered by increased railway freights and local
harassing conditions, is likely to continue to do so.
Producer gas played a prominent part in the reduc-
tion of working costs on Westralian mines during the-
year, and many shows in the back country which
could not be previously worked on account of the
prohibitive cost of fuel are now being turned to pro-
fit. Sons of Gwalia has gone in for this class of'
power on a big scale, and but for the enormous
scrapning that would have to be done, several of the-
big Kalgoorlie mines would follow suit.
# * * *
Associated Northern (W.A.) is up against
trouble at the Victorious leases.. Ora Banda. . Pro-
fits have become attenuated owing to depletion of'
the rich shoot and exhaustion of oxidised reserves,,
and developments in the sulphide zone are on the-
lean side. A patch of good stuff has just been.
struck in the east end of the No. 6 level; but, as with
several others that have been exposed, its extent
is not likely to be very great. The co. seems to be
still in doubt whether the resources of the property
justify the erection of a sulphide plant, and from
all The Bulletin can hear the doubt is well-
founded.
Central and West Boulder (W.A.) has
issued a report stating that £14,196 was obtained';
by tributing on its Kalgoorlie leases during the 15
months ended September last, and that its cut out
of the proceeds amounted to £1505. The ore treated’
gave an average return of 7dwt., but boreholes put
below the 400 ft. level indicated the existence of
stuff worth about 12dwt., and steps are being taken
to prove the extent of these values. The. co. has a',
large interest in the Damo tin property in Nigeria.,
which has lately been puffed by its controllers, and’
declared to contain a considerable area of tin-bearing-
ground.
Kalgurli (W.A.) now has £60,000 in cash’
and investments and a bullion reserve of about:
c 13.000. . Neither general-manager Black nor Dr..
Maclaren, who made an inspection for the Board,,
hold out any hope of payable values being met with’,
in deeper sinking ; but in order to make doubly sure-
that there is nothing below, Black recommends that
money be spent in diamond-drilling. This will pro-
bably be done. Meantime some of the old levels and!
the ground between them are being prospected in-
all directions and payable sections opened up. Tt
seems likely that the mine will continue to be profit-
able for a year or two yet, but exactly how long- iff
is impossible to say.

Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) is conti nmrlTy
being credited with good developments, but somehow'
the market doesn’t respond as much as expected'.
The latest reports claim that a length of 9ffoft. of
payable ore has been exposed in the bottom level
(No. 19), and that hopes are entertained of the-
shoot extending for another 300 ft. or more. Winzing

good values, and with the earlv prospect of satisfac-
tory developments at the No. 20 level, where driving'

Apparently some person or persons operating on
’Change hold an opposite opinion, and are backing'

Fraser’s old mine at Southern Cross'.
GW A ) has lately been prospected to 1000 ft. by bore-
holes. -In all, '3569ft. of drilling was done, and’
results showed the reefs to persist at depth and to-
carrv good values in places. W. J. Loring recentlv
tried in London to get capital to work this show,,
but found there was nothing doing. Apparently-
London gamblers are afraid that if a start was once-
ma(le —and it would have to be on a big scale- to-
rpake it worth while—-they rnigh,t have to meet
demands for increased wages and higher water rates,.,
besides having to chance the possibility of the earlv
introduction of reforms, such as abolition of com-
tract, stoppage of night shift, limitation of heights
of stopes and other things which the State Labor
Ministry proposes to add to the laws of the country
as soon as it can bring the Upper House round to its
way of thinking.
Unable to find anything suitable in the
West, manager Hamilton, of the Boulder, has gone
to Stawell (Vic.) to have a look at the old Mag-
dala. At one time this was the deepest mine in Vic-
toria. Latterly very little has been done in its work-
ings.

Ballarat proper had the luck to get 900 oz.
more gold during the last quarter of the year than in
the previous three months, but the return of 52390 z.
irom 22,567 tons is less than the old field used once to
turn out weekly. Even out of an average of under
sdwt. stone, a few divs. were naid by the two Llan-
berris mines. Yet how sick things are is proved by
the fact that only 823 miners are left to punch out
quartz in the central division.
The people who have stuck to Long
Tunnel, Walhaila, are sure that the turn has come.
Not only is 2ft. of stone showing in the winze below
3225 ft., but it is butt up against a nice-looking
dyke. This is the best bit of right stuff got since the
new crowd dispensed with the stinking-fish party.
When “Geology” Herman started the hunt after
Cohen’s reef in the deep ground, his idea was to get
below the belt of crushed country where the reef
had shot away from the dyke and lost its richness.
If additional work proves the old contact of the
diorite with a sprinkling of antimonial pyrites and
with the quartz hugging it closely, stranger things
than a humming time might happen at Walhaila. It
would be like Dickenson’s luck to hit the double in
two has-beens like the Tunnel and the A.I. at
Gaffney’s. # #
The Langi Logan boom has been so well
worked that lew holders know that the boom has
.already happened, though they must know that the
cunning crowd is out. Here’s for a comparison.
Cathcart Central is in 50,000 £1 shares, once 605.,
now 355. The gutter was found to be 200 ft. wide,
and “proved ” worth over 3oz. a fathom by truck
■samples. But when blocked out it came down to
2oz. a fathom, and costs are loz. a fathom. The
■co. started paying 2s. divs., and then came down to
Is. every five weeks, and passed the Christmas div.
altogether. And the Cathcart Central is the richest
thing since Madame Berry. Now the Langi Logans
gutter is 1000 ft. wide, yet they expect it to beat the
Cathcart for value. The co. is in 100,000 shares,
now 365, or £lBO,OOO for a mine not yet operating,
the evidence of value being confined to the results
of bores. The action in bores drilled into wash
with much water above it is a concentrating action,
and the gold content of the bore is not reliable evi-
dence of the bulk. The rise is going up in the
richest ground yet touched on the mine, so results
there may give another lift to the increase in share
values which followed on the bore-sampling. But if
Langi Logans are worth 365., Cathcarts are worth
755. ; and you can get them for 355., which is pro-
bably their value. # *
Charters Towers crushed 6120 tons of
stone in December for a return of £25,159, of which
£13,956 was from the mills, £5613 from the cyanid-
ing of 5493 tons of tailings, and £5590 from the
smelting of concentrates. The average was £4 Is. 7d.
per ton. For the year 78,910 tons were crushed, and
the total return from all sources was £306,898. In
dividends £49,903 was paid—-a surplus of only
£l2 024 over calls collected. The Brilliant Extended
paid’ £18,750. or nearly 40 p.c. of the total. lt lias
iust opened No. 22 level, but the reef at 3000 ft. is
less reliable than it was at 2000 ft. Apart from these
deep levels the field’s most hopeful prospect at the
moment is in the shallow and long-abandoned Moon-
stone mines. The deepest workings here are only
164 ft vertical, but owing to the extremely flat dip
.already four levels have been opened. Last year two
mines,' the Papuan and New Queen Cross, crushed
between them 3839 tons for £26,887 ; but the proved
reserves worth £7 are very small. The Papuan will
crush again shortly. ,
* *
Hampden-Cloncurry (Q.) reports striking
the Trekelano lode at the 200 ft. level of that outside
property. The lode is described as being 12ft. wide,
worth 7 p.c., though the first sampling over 48in.
gave only 6 p.c. At the 80ft. the lode has been
driven on for some hundreds of feet. The co. is
smelting over 60,000 tons of 9 to 10 p.c. ° re
annum, and a lot of development will be required to
keep pace with that drain. Reserves are estimated
at 255,000 tons averaging over 9 p.c.. lhis isn t
much of a quantity to support 350,000 shares and a
smelting capacity of from 60,000 to 70,000 tons a
year. , * *
North Queensland’s coal fields are reach-
ing an interesting stage. Blair Athol, 250. miles
west from Rockhampton, produced 4o 438 tons in
six months to September 30, for a profit of £5094
It has declared a sixpenny div. < £2500), and some
of the optimists are sure the mine will give £lO,OOO
a vear in dividends. Other mines on the same field
are coming to the front. The Newcastle Colliery
put out 1725 tons for a month; and a co-operative
party has started work on one of the Bluff leases, and
is said to have a contract to supply 60,000 tons to
the railways. Further north, Mount Mulligan pro-
mises fairly well; the lower tunnel is showing a
seam 24ft.'thick of good quality. Numerous leases
: have been taken up at the Bowen coalfield. Geologist
Dunstan points out that the shaft section of the
1 Denison seam is apparently coked, and he advises
deeper sinking. The field is about 50 miles from
Bowen. *.
London mining speculators are looking at
-the Palmer (N.Q.). A 50-acre lease embracing the
Queen line of reef was recently applied for on
behalf of E. W. Caldwell, who had an early-day
experience of the old field. The Queen turned out
£125,000 worth of gold from very shallow workings.
' It is said, also, that 8.. M. and Co. are investigat-
ing The Louisa line, for the working of which a
co has just been formed and registered, and which
eets a £7500 subsidy from the Government, has
aDnointed a manager, and as soon as the wet season
trover operations will be started. Things look
better for the Palmer now than at any time since
it went to sleep. # *
The Laloki mine, which Great Fitzroy
• has decided to hitch on to, to see if it will help to
find the elusive dividend, has an area of 116 acres,
and is at the foot of the Astrolabe Range (Papua).
The original finders scratched a little, saw that there
was something in it, and sold out to the British
"New Guinea Development Co. This co. also scratched
a little and saw that the mine was worth while ; but
a proposition of the sort was out of the co.’s line.
Hence Great Fitzroy was offered a 15-months option.
That co inspected and tested ; and, partly in hope and
partly in desperation, took the thing over. Fitzroy s
testing showed the ore body to be of dense sulphide,
with little silica, and a suitable flux for Fitzroy s
own ores, besides which it should provide a tonnage
for local smelting.
Sungei Raia tin looks about as badly a
busted piece of Malay mining as any Melbourne crowd
has ever spent money on. A. S. Carroll, manager,
in a special report to shareholders, carves up things
generally, basing his criticism on a personal examina-
tion of the mine and of the accounts. His opening'
‘ bump was to find debts; his next, to alight on a
judgment got by angry creditors. By the time this
tangle had been unravelled he found that borings
•on the mine only gave up to o.Blb. of stream tin to
the cubic yard. This wouldn’t pay. So Carroll
plumps for letting the ground on tribute to Chinese.
By screwing a royalty out of them and selling the
plant, he ought to clean up the slate without further
calls on shareholders. To get them a bit of
their own back he proposes to pick up a Selangor
block of 49 acres and bore it, and, if the sampling
is 0.K., to equip it as an open-cast mine for £2500.
Carroll, by this time, ought to have learnt the
East,” and so will no doubt be trusted with the
cash to have a final fling.
* * * *
There is a more hopeful feeling among
mining men regarding the future of the Waihi
(M L.) field. The deep-level developments in the big
Waihi and Junction mines have caused the people
who control the Waihi Reefs and Gigantic properties
—about 800 acres—to get busy, and the shaft is to
be deepened from 800 ft. to 1250 ft., and a crosscut
thrown out to intersect the bullion-producing Waihi
reef system. The Waihi field outside the two big mines
has never been prospected much, because deposits of
recent origin have covered up the older rocks, and
the pumice, etc., has to be pierced several hundred
feet before the reef country is reached.

at Motorua (M.L.) at a comparatively shallow
depth. Almost simultaneously the Consolidated
Oilfields of Taranaki Co. got news that oil had been
struck in its Huiroa bore at a mere 1200 ft. lhe
locality of the latter strike is 10 miles south-east
from Inglewood, in the Huiroa survey district, lhe
Consolidated shares are mostly held in Canterbury
(M.L.).

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, January 19, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
Manly was tied up in bunting and Chinese
lanterns for several days, with a final car-
nival bang on Saturday night. Colored
lights gleamed round the fringe of the
watery basin on the harbor side in the
evening, and every species of craft sidled or
fussed across the bay. In the afternoon,
Manly visitors stamped off the crowded
ferry boats in that mouth-open state which
marks the deep-breather on the track of
ozone. Near the wharf the air was thick
with prawns, which turned their pale pink
corpses and starting eyes in a limp heap of
briny smell on the passers-by. The regatta,
which started at 2 p.m., was as thrilling as
most regattas—that is to sav, the sailing
craft meandered round a pleasant sea-breeze
and came back to their moorings without
smashing records. The man who seemed to
be working hardest on the ocean side was a
surfer who rushed at the Pacific Ocean with
an ironing board, got out as far as he could
with his timber, and then rode it into shore.
An English tripper who saw the flurried
tail of the Manly carnival on Saturday even-
mg, remarked that Australians get a lot
of fun without making much of a hole in
their cash. At Sea View (Isle of Wight)
I understand you pay to take a short cut
across a small beach-end that leads to the
big stretch of creamy sand. It’s only a
penny or so, but it’s a beginning. Having
gained the Right Place, you then enter into
negotiations to hire a small tent with a flap
that slaps you in the fact if the weather
is gusty. There is no separate charge for
the flap or the slap: you only pay for the
tent After retiring to this stronghold you
disrobe for a dabble in the sea. If you’re
careless about it, you find the tent too small,
and poke a leg or an arm into the fresh
?J. r - , V®, re i, s n ? cll ai'ge for the fresh air.
Most of the day is passed in a bathing-gown
elaborate enough to satisfy the critical
glances of any stray flotsam and jetsam of
Society which may be paddling at Sea
View. Tea is brought to the tent door in
the afternoon at a high figure, and if the
t. ® 1S in R comes high enough to mingle
with your cake and lap the milk-jug. I
orget whether you pay to walk back across
the sands or not. I think you do. In the
evening Sea View either attires itself in
the latest London fashions, or gets into a
semi-batliing-gown and wades after shrimps.
~ re free. There are other charges
which I disremember, but I believe the thing
costs a lot more than Manly.

Agnes Goodsir, an Australian artist who

ot giving a one-woman show here within the

Some wandering Australians now in
i ans were invited to a Tango Tea there
just before the last mail left. “The en-
trance fee was five francs each, and the tea
itself, which included most of the delicacies
out or season—your genuine French gourmet
doesn t care a sou for food in season—must
have been a costly spread. However, as
we . w m r u e guests, expense was no object to
us . The Tango itself, or what we had time
to see of it through a dazzling parade of
trocks, was no more exciting than the old-
tashioned galop, so I expect we saw a Sun-
day edition. It was merely an excuse for a
mannequin display.”
The International Medical Congress, the
first to meet in M.L., will open in Auckland
on lebruary 9. Dr. Valentine, the Do-
minion s chief Health Officer, is a large
cheerful, breezy individual who might easily
be mistaken for a heavy-weight champion
with a punch. The Massey Government is
going to spoon-feed the Pill and Plaster bri-
gade with sulphur fumes at Rotorua, and
will explain through its tourist guides, the
number of inhalations a minute a
really reliable geyser will make after it has
swallowed a few bars of soap.
John Harper, who is to take Tom John-
son s place as Chief Commissioner of Rail-
ways until other arrangements are made, is
a six-tooter who has grown white whiskers
in the service. Years and years ago there
were Harpers who had a timber-yard at
anamatta, and John, who was the boss’s
son, played with the chips and the sawdust
until he was old enough to put on a junior
porter s uniform. Then he more or less
specialised in traffic, and has been the official
mouthpiece of the Department before the
VVorks Committee since ever there was a
Works Committee to inquire into the
prospects of proposed railway lines. Unlike
Johnson, who is a bachelor and has lived
ins out-ot-office life in the quietest corner of
a club, Harper is a domesticated animal,
who drops out of his official quarters in
Macquane-street into a pair of gardening
trousers and grows cinerarias and other
choice but inedible vegetables. Like a for-
mer Commissioner, Oliver, he gets the rest
of his lecreation as an enthusiastic cricket
barracker.

The Jim Allisons, of Coomoo Coomoo sta-
tion, up Quirindi way, came to town last
week. They’re cooling down at the Clifton
Gardens Hotel, on the brink of what used
to be Chowder Bay—but you mustn’t call
it that any more. Though Coomoo Coomoo
homestead has a name that often gets it
into trouble with casual correspondents, it
is a most attractive sample of an Austra-
lian up-country bungalow, with verandahs
wide enough to billet a squad of cadets. An
unusual duet in the vegetation of those parts,
a huge lemon tree nodding to a giant orange
tree, greet the sundowner at the entrance.
Mr. Jack Bridges, of Kickabill station—
the big sheep-walk on Liverpool Plains that
used to belong to the Higgins family—is
sauntering by Manly’s towelled shore. His
irreproachable hair, always beautifully
parted in the middle, keeps the even tenor
of its place in any breeze that blows. This
is a cause of bitterness to that portion of
the male population in the surf-side village
which is young enough to feel annoyed at
the ruffling of its wind-blown locks. The
gone-to-bits appearance of Society on any
line, afternoon in the breaker-shooting ham-
let is most apparent in its seaweedy coiffure,
with its draggled skirts a good second.
Cricketer Noble was married without any
fuss to Miss Nellie. Ferguson of this crowded
centre of population the other afternoon.
Best-man Pope, supported the groom who
said ‘I will” in an undertone, while the
bndesmaidens, Dorothy Noble and Maud

Miss Susie O’Donnell, a daughter of the
late P J. O’D. of Mingay station, said she
would love, honor and obey (in a soft whis-
per ) Barrister Flannery on Saturday last.
She is a sister of little Aileen, who married
the long cricketer Warwick Armstrong a
while ago. The groom used to be associate
to JLamuna Barton, and wont off as socre-
tary or gentleman help to Australia’s noblest
s pn (this myth still lives in old newspaper
clippings) to England for some royal crown-
ing or burying, years ago. The Flannery
honeymoon is flitting past in Melbourne.
Dr. Adams, who used to be a naval
man, lias bought Vectis, Double Bay,
and will hang out his sign on the front door
or the garden rail. His wife is the eldest
daughter of E. Knox, of Bellevue Hill. The
Adams pair tripped back from England
about the time the vaccination plague was
brooding over us and our belongings. He
was offered a seat or lounge on the Board of
Health, and promptly closed with the offer.
Now he is getting into business for himself.
A daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Etienne de
Mestre, of Moss Yale, who was married to
one of the Burwood Yates family, may be a
granddaughter of the late Etty de Mestre of
sporting fame. De Mestre bred horses and
lost a pot of money in clean, honest racing
on Bje AnsLmli&n turf. He owned Archer,
the Melbourne Cup winner in 1861 and 1862.
Tim Whiffler, another of his Cup victors, got
away with the prize plate in 1867, and lie
an °B ier winner f° r the big event in
.v when Calamia left the field weeping
in the rear. In spite of many victories the
late sporting de Mestre didn’t leave much
behind him except a record for going
straight. s
Poiret, the Frenchman who is said to
design frocks as Bernard Shaw carves out
the framework of a play—with his tongue
in his . cheek—is threatening the return of
the crinoline. This trousered dressmaker
knows that women -who want to be first in
frenzied fashion will make any kind of
fools of themselves if a Paris clothes-man
leads the way. Therefore he talks of “sim-
plicity of line and breadth of mind,” and
puts his followers into a pair of silk panta-
loons. The crinoline tunic, according to the
gospel of Poiret, is the forerunner of the
early-Victorian bird-cage skirt.
The Victorian Trained Nurses’ Journal
Una remarks that the result of the ballot
(on December 30) re increased fees for
nurses was yet to come in. The proposal
was carried in Sydney, and ought to be
pushed through as a bare act of justice to
highly-specialised workers. As Miss Helen
Gould, of Sydney, an acknowledged autho-
rity on modern nursing, points out, the
working life in private nursing is short
After humping her suit-case for 10 years
from house to house, the average nurse is
an old woman in nerves and bodv. She
sleeps anywhere or nowhere—usually on a
make-shift couch in the patient’s room If
she. be worth her wage, she does all the in-
valid cooking , and the chores in the sick
room, in addition to her own .duties.
A nurse who loves her work is too ex-
hausted after a strenuous case to take
another forthwith. She has to snatch a few
nights regular sleep to brace her up for the
next uncertain series of half-waking naps

The Twilight Home for old ladies is in the
way of luck.. It is getting a handsome New
Year s gift in the shape of a nice plot of
F°und near Spit-road. Mosman. The donor
is Mr. H. Gordon, of that suburb of feverish-
looking roofs and many cool waters. The
committee will put on its spectacles and
peer into building plans immediately, if not
sooner.
. Have you ever noticed that when the
nights are warm and sleepless the morning
papers throb with brain-racking letters
e . e lv it0 T-rr on^ u ? h Quarrelsome topics as
v\hat Do We Believe?” This tiny planet
is peopled with millions of atoms who are
ready to bruise, beat or boil for the cook-
pot millions of other atoms who believe
that they don’t believe what the other specks
try to believe. The subject is an irritating
one that ought to be put on ice to cool till
Faster revives the theological warriors.
. Maud Telfer, a youthful Sydney soprano,
is preening her feathers for flight to the
Gold Land. She joins the Quinlan Co. in
August, when it begins rehearsing for its
1915 world tour.
Florence Smithson, the English soprano
who ekes out a living on £lOO or so a week
in her busy season on the other side, is
coming here in July. Just now she is
warbling at Drury Lane, where hubby Dan
Rolyat had a bad accident a while ago. He
is still laid up and feeling very sick about
the back. But the Smithson voice trills
long and loud enough to make bread and
marmalade for both. Incidentally, Dan is
in easy financial circumstances himself.

Leura is full of panting Sydney goffers,
who stamp round the links mopping their
dripping countenances, and hoping to catch
a cool change round the next corner. The
almost übiquitous Gidley Kings, the Kirk-
knds, Grahams, and the slender Mrs. Max-
well Allan (skimming after her ball like a
swallow darting at a gnat) are just now
habitues of the breezy mountain village.
. A writer in the Argonaut (San Fran-
cisco) heaves an unkind aspersion of ink at
Mrs. Spankworst, the English militant lady
who had a brief look in at N’York last year.
He says a N’York reporter had the bad luck
to describe the traveller’s costume as “a sort
ot dressing gown,” whereat Mrs. Spankworst
arched her back and bristled for days. But
the reporter, being a man, knew no better
and intended no insult. After this passage
of arms, the “Votes for Women” apostle
was to have taken part in a grand march
at a ball in Chicago, with a gentleman
named Samuel Gompers—one who has led
many strikes and got into many troubles of
his own. But at the last moment,” snorts
the Argonaut, “Fate, in the shape of Mrs.
Gompers, intervened with an injunction. She
said, If Sam dances with any woman it
must be with his wife.’ ” That settled it.
Samuel Grompers evidently knew that he
was hable to fall in the soup, and fled from
broth to come. He did his performance
with Mrs. Gompers, while Mrs. Spankworst
—deserted, abandoned, lonesome and be-
trayed looked on from the gallery with
window-smash in her eye.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 19, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Fuller dame, garbed in mourning, de-
parted last week by the mailboat. Some of
the. National Council women and represen-
tatives of some of the pet Fuller charities
hurried down to ugly Port Melbourne with
vegetable offerings. The outgoing lady
murmured tactful speeches on the steep
gangway, with one eye anxiously fixed on
the children. The other eye was sufficient
for the breathless visitors. Kirkpatrick, who
attends her, is an excellent courier but a
poor nursemaid. (Stewart Richardson, an
earlier Help, was ideal at the nursery job.)
Anyhow, he had a small levee of his own to
keep him busy. The farewelling lady said
to the upturned faces and hats, “It is not
altogether good-bye; I’ll be keeping in
touch.” Previously her “good-bye” message
to the people of Victoria had been reveren-
tially published by the dailies. Time was
when only the salaried male figurehead was
supposed to offer words of consolation to the
devoted pubilc through the newspapers; but
nowadays his wife takes a half-share in the
melancholy duty, and soon the governess and
chief maid will add a few sympathetic re-
marks, while the butler sadly drinks the
country’s ’ealth.
New State Governor Stanley is bringing
two fresh Helps for drawing-room inspec-
tion. One is Captain Gale, of the Bedford-
shire regiment, and the other is Lieutenant
Wilson, of the Tenth Hussars. The Hussar’s
haberdashery is bound to stun the Best
People, but I’m doubtful about the Bedford-
shire millinery. I’m afraid it’s rather un-
distinguished.
Commander Corneille gave a consul party
on his white ship L’Avenir, last week, when
representatives of all countries and their
ladies sampled the famous Belgian salads at
dinner. The cadets played soft, greasy music
after the feast proper, while the already well-
fed guests munched Belgian cake with their
coffee. The cake was made aboard and was
decorated with a sugar lion and other her-
aldic _ accessories. Belgium is very proud of
its lion, which walks gingerly across the
shield, like a performing animal on a tight-
rope.
From “Rosna,” in London:—
Dr. Alice Burn, a Maorilander who is school-medi-
' cal officer and assistant borough medical officer of
Cheltenham, has been in a fracas with the panjan-
drums of the education committee. The lady had
the effrontery to deliver lectures on vegetarianism in
her free time. The other medical officer, one of the
lordly sex, did not approve of vegetarianism, and it
was naturally considered extremely forward of a
woman—and a Maorilander—to have views. How-
ever, the lady gamely refused to resign, and the
education committee is now considering whether she
shall be executed or recommended for a title.
Australian sculptor Bertram Mackennal was called
in to have a chat with King George at Buckingham
Palace this week. He was also entertained by the
Authors’ Club. Now almost anything may happen.
Britain is slowly solving the problem, “What
shall we do with our widows?” The latest Blue Book
asserts that there are in England 77 plumbers, 70
paperhangers and whitewashers and four bricklayers—
all relicts. There are also 347 car-ladies and wag-
goneresses, 231 she-blacksmiths and strikers, 13
widow-masons and 56 lady-carpenters. Incidentally,
there are 126,435 (or so) widowers whose small
children live on bread-and-jam and go to school untidy
for want of a woman in the house. This world
badly wants re-arranging. Mr. Winston Churchill
will probably be driven to take it in hand some day.
The new owner of the Covent Garden property,
Mallaby-Deeley, will receive the tolls paid to the
Bedfords since the days of William IV.; thus “2d.
per round or head load” of strawberries, Id. a sieve
of onions, Is. 6d. per waggon of carrots, etc. One
old tenant of the market has been telling of some
record prices. For Lord Rosebery’s wedding he was
ordered to get 251 b. of strawberries and 251 b. of
grapes. The latter cost 255. a lb. and the strawberries
3s. 6d. an ounce.
A sigh of regret went around the very
best pastoral-property circles last week
when the death of Harry Ryan was an-
nounced. Harry, the elder brother of Dr.
Charley Ryan, was a stock and station
agent, and the squattocracy held him in
particular regard as a valuer of acres and
flocks and herds. Old Grammarians re-
membered him as a star oarsman or oarsboy
in the long ago. and clubmen called him
“one of the best.” Mr. Ryan was supposed
to be getting along nicely after a Christmas
Eve operation for appendicitis. Then he
had a relapse. People don’t die of opera-
tions. The relapse is what does it.
Allan Burgoyne, M.P., whose name spells
“Australian Burgundy” in London, is re-
turning thither this week, after a trip
around the vine-growing districts. In the
House of Commons the progressive Allan B.
is a recognised discusser of naval matters,
and a while ago he introduced a measure,
which was carried, against the White Slave
Traffic. When Be visits Australia he is
talking wine trade most of the time. He
and his brother hold several properties in
the Rutherglen (Yic.) district, and during
this last visit he has added the old Emu
Vineyard to their possessions. The Emu
Vineyard had been ever so long in the hands
of the Prentice family, whose name is at-
tached to the gold mines and township of
Prentice Freehold. The vineyard is a lovely
property of 500 acres, and the vines are all
flourishing from “resistant” stocks. A wise
grape-breeder follows the vine of most re-
sistance, it seems.
The massed population of Panton Hill, in
solemn conclave assembled, recently bailed
up one of the Gibson girls and handed her
an address of thanks and other embarrassing
things for her many neighborly acts to the
bush people. Mrs. Ernest Keep is the eld-
est of the Gibsons. They are now all
married save the philanthropic Alice, who
lives an ideal bachelor life in the hills. Like
her sisters, she is well supplied with cash ;
but she is, in addition, an artist with an
Edinburgh exhibition to her credit. Frank,
the brother, is well known in the art world
and helps to select the Felton pictures which
periodically disturb local art lovers.
Widower Deweit, who has been “in wool”
here for 30 years or so, has been entertaining
the Belgian master mariner at his Macedon
house —a place with the usual wonder gar-
den that surrounds every old summer man-
sion on the cool Mount. The Norman
Brookes’ place is next door, so early morning
tennis has been raging.
A young Scotchman who recently caused
heartburnings amongst our best-dressed
youths by the exquisite quality of his socks
and ties, happened to be in Maoriland during
the strike and nobly offered to do a little
stoking on a steamer. He got his chance;
but the experience seems to have scorched
all his dress ideas out of him. He hardly
troubles to look at other johnnies’ socks
now. and can remain quite calm in the
presence of a tie like a Chopin nocturne.
The youngest (and frankest) daughter of
a well-known family is wandering off
for a spell of foreign travel with mother
and her numerous sisters. The party is
dividing into two contingents and going by
different boats. The flapper explains
shrilly to all who will listen that “all of us
and the maids, y’ know, make too much of
an army.”
Some time in the future, Dorothy Bird is
going to flutter from the parental Fred Bird
nest to a flapper-crowded wedding. Her
engagement to Eric Brookes, of Queen’s-
road, Melbourne, is just announced.
The Burnham-Niall wedding is going to
be quiet, if not actually furtive or under-
hand. The dreadful news has been received
with yelps_ of annoyance from the unem-
ployed Society girls, who were looking for-
ward to the usual semi-religious pantomime.
From the gadfly point of view, the
quiet wedding (confound it!) will be a
wicked waste of opportunity.
The Grice clan is very excited this week.
Nancy, of the Mqondah branch, which waves
at Mornington, is marrying a Bananalander,
Alexander Howatson by name. Cousinly
Grices have been hurrying back from the
ends of the earth for the event, which is to
happen in the old-fashioned church on the
top of Mount Eliza. The hill is one of the
motorists’ favorite tests, and the Best
People will smoke to the top joyously.
The Clegg maiden is to become Mrs. Simon
Fraser on Tuesday, February 17. The
chief shepherd of Scots’ Ivirk, in Collins-
street, will work the transformation.
Audrey Chirnside, who is off with mother
to Robert Hitchens’s Nile, had a good-bye
party in the stalls at “Diplomacy.” A row
of lovely opera coats and a few men com-
prised the gang, which was led by a purple-
frocked chaperone. Audrey wore bunches of
baby roses on a very transparent chiffon
gown, and Beryl Fitzgerald was generous
with her handsome back. In another party
were the Frank Madden girls. Not to be
outdone by anyone in the matter of revela-
tions, one dimpled girl close by showed much
of a pink silk leg, preparatory to revealing
more on the kerb outside the Royal, at motor-
boarding time. The Chirnside outing fin-
ished with a rose-heaped supper at the
Savoy, with a good deal of champagne
amongst the roses.
Mrs. Donald McKinnon, the big, imposing
dame who dominates charity meetings, and
whose word is law —or jaw-—will be back in
Melbourne for the gyrations. She has been
having her daughter polished, finished and
wound up in the Old World, where you can
buy education with a head on it at cheap
rates.
The widow Landale’s two daughters are
coming out from London again. Daughter
Edyth and husband Cyril, who is also her
cousin, are due in Melbourne this month,
with return tickets in their pockets. (Or
maybe both tickets are in one of his pockets. )
The Ronald Murray pair will arrive in
February with their much-travelled infant,
to resume practice as Australian citizens.
The George Chirnside dame, who is taking
daughter Audrev back to the land where
dukes run about unleashed, hostessed a num-
ber of farewell house parties at Werribee. The
pathetic lack of lurable bachelors practically
reduced the gatherings to hen conventions.
Nephew Pat and a friend (Heaven help
them !) were the only males in the chatter-
ing congregation.
Rose Seaton, formerly a well-known
Shakspearian in Sydney, about two
years ago came south to Melbourne,
and has since been taking a lead
in McMahon’s Repertory shows. Lately
she launched out in recite! work, engaging
a few excellent but struggling artists to
assist her. Unfortunately she made the
new St. Peter’s Hall, Eastern Hill, her
place of entertainment, and as the general
public hadn’t struck St. Peter’s on Eastern
Hill before, it didn’t roll up in huge masses.
However, the recitals were an artistic suc-
cess. Now the lady is to reap some sort
of a reward; she is to take up a position as
high priestess of dramatic art at tlie Con-
servatorium of Music that lately was
Marshall Hall’s, and, in the future is to be
run by Pitzroy Hart, Edward Goll & Co.
Mrs. Freddie Shipman, who is helping
her husband to steer tenor Dufault through
Maoriland, writes shiveringly of the Inver-
cargill summer—and every shiver makes me
envious. “Fighting Freddie” expects to be
back in Melbourne in March, when the Nor-
dica action is programmed to start.
Irene Browne, as the dressy Comtesse in
“Diplomacy,” commits the unforgivable
(dressmaker’s) sin during the frock display;
a rough-edged seaxn in the train is clearly
visible as she trails to and fro. The usual
chiffon veil serves to uncover the leg that is
fashionable.
Mrs. Brough, who provides a clever old
woman study in “Diplomacy,” gives the play
a pleasant, old-time feeling to the older gen-
eration of Melbourne playgoers. Irene
Browne is mostly frocks —lovely Parisian
things made in Melbourne especially for stage
posing. One black dress, with a wisp of
white chiffon and a Paddy green sash for a
foundation, has the absurd new train that is
pushed in front instead of being dragged
behind. Irene tripped over this foolish dead
end at the end of a fine scene, and clawed
her way to the door, kicking all the way.
She wears race dresses and garden-party
gowns for nine o’clock morning calls, so the
Comtesse evidently didn’t own a walking
skirt.
Actor Frank Lincoln’s personable young
widow, who now runs the Fairlie House
flatterie, is at present enthusing over her
five-year-old son. He has been nicknamed
“Bundle,” because he was packed up in Eng-
land and sent out to Australia carriage paid.
Sculptor Cadorin, _ who is also a dress
builder, is off to his beloved Italy again.
Lovie Mueller Cadorin follows dutifully with
a damp handkerchief and tear-misty eyes.
She just hates leaving home and mother.
Incurable shoppers are thinking as they
rush towards the bargains of the sinister
speech of Fire-Chief Lee after our recent
ragtime blaze : “It is not necessary to be a
fireman to realise what would happen, if a
fire broke out at a busy time. The people
inside would never get out, and we would
never get in.” About the only thing left
standing after the sacrifice was a wall bear-
ing the painted yell—“ Great Record Summer
Sale Now On.” It moved many women to
tears.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
A sort of Sabbath calm broods over Adelaide. The
best tea-things are packed away. The croquet mallets
are at rest. The moth-brick and the gladder rags
of yester-year are lying down together. Nobody
is doing anything but work. Socially the place is
comatose, if not dead.
Mrs. John- Bagot has returned from England, only
to find the city more than ever like Pompeii or
Margate—that is, from the viewpoint of a confirmed
fashionable. Her son and his wife are quietly but
very firmly packing with a view to the invasion of
London. They will leave their infant behind. It
is of an age when food appeals to it considerably
more than social gaiety—and, thank Heaven, the
food of Australia is still all right. Dashes to the
Fog are also being hastily organised by the W.
Haywards and the A. E. Wiggs. :
Mrs. John Dunbar, of Melbourne, and her small
daughter have been at the Grand Central. Miss
Kate Gardner has also been leading the strenuous
life of the visiting Victorian after a lazy fortnight
on the Dalton yacht. The Stephen Rallis' are aboard
that well-known craft at the time of writing.
Dr. Constance Ellis, of Melbourne, has been staying
with her sister, Mrs. S. J. Jacobs, at Glenelg.
At the inter-State cricket match I noticed, in
various stages of excitement, ranging from that which
is commonly termed blithering down to the mild
variety that is observable when an Elder of the
Kirk throws a fit during a dull service on a hot
Sabbath afternoon —at this more or less classic
struggle I saw, I repeat, the Edwin Smith nobleman,
the Willie Popes, Mr. and Mrs. Walter Hawker, the
W. A. Magareys, Mrs. C. Irwin, Mr. and Mrs. Barritt,
Mrs. Roy Milne, Dr. and Mrs. A. A. Hamilton, Mrs.
John Corbin, and Miss Jessop. There were others,
but their names will not fit into this issue of Tin:
Bulletin on account of the advts.
And now for a seasonable li-r-I mean, anecdote.
A trades-union secretary was sleeping peace
fully in his camp on the sand, at Glenelg, the
other night, when he felt something crawl up his
Leg and go to sleep on his Heart! ! The place
being Glenelg, and he a trades-union secretary, he
naturally paid little attention to the happening—
no more, in fact, than an ordinary man does when
a scorpion dives down his bare back. In the morn-
ing the —yes, gentle reader, you have guessed aright
—the deadly snake, being thoroughly rested and
warmed, uncoiled its noisome and venomous length
and slid thoughtfully home. And now comes the
incredible part of the story. The trades-union secre-
tary was frightened. His teeth chattered. He trem-
bled. There is always some damning flaw in the
consistency of these reptile tales.
Aviator Jones accidentally swallowed some embro-
cation the other day. He absorbed it in mistake for
medicine, but after he had got a cpuple of flagons
down he came to the conclusion that some of the
1914 vintage of bush whiskv had got into the bottle,
and called a halt. Meanwhile, a total stranger was
passing bogus cheques in his name in another part
of the city. And only the other day he smashed his
aeroplane up. Still, as he philosophically puts it,
things might have been worse. The aero might have
killed him ; the cheques might have been cashed ; the
embrocation might really have been bush whiskv.
Life is full of such compensations. Talking of this
particular Jones, he has not been able to
get his flying machine into work again after its fall—
which wasn’t exactly a fall, but an over-swift descent
caused by failure of petrol. He is still pegging
away at repairs, and meanwhile a few friends in
Adelaide have handed him £25 with a bunch of com-
pliments. Jones, in responding, said that he is
not out so much to make money as to show Aus-
tralia the possibilities of the aeroplane in war.
Miss Dorothy Luscombe, as tall and fair and
lovely as ever, is on a visit to the Harry Duttons, at
Victor Harbor.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.
“Hamer”: Official figures show that 93
per cent, of Australian mothers have
claimed the liver since the maternity bonus
became an established fact. The allowance
has been paid to 130,000 women altogether,
it would be interesting to know the ladies’
opinions of Cook’s proposal to put the brand
ot pauperism on future applicants. But
Oook probably knows their views on the mat-
ter by now. which is the best of reasons
why he is likely to leave Fisher’s measure
alone.
From the Sydney Telegraph of Jan. 8:—
the coming octopus.
, Says the latest American Sheepbreeder (Chicago):
It is a safe bet that in 10 years the Chicago packers
will control the mutton and beef trade of South
America and Australia.”
But the Telegraph knows that this is all
nonsense. There are no Trusts in Aus-
tralia. Also there can’t be any Trusts in
Australia. The alleged “octopus” is really
a philanthropic animal which will give the
growers a few millions more than they get
now and sell the meat to the public for a
few millions less, and that will naturally
result in the animal having a few millions
left over for himself. The idea that the
octopus, having got control, will squeeze the
grower into selling for less and the public
into buying for more is. so utterly absurd
that the Telegraph wouldn’t even allow
Australia to protect itself against the possi-
bility.

“/Esculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water-only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
* Figaro . Read a par. here the other week about
'Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of "Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, "Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.
ABORINGI-
ALITIES
“Vakuta” : I’ll have ten bob’s worth that
the Papuan is the most superstition-ridden native in
the Pacific. I’ve had some of my boys working for
me for years, yet nothing I can do to shame them
out of their helplessness before witchcraft has any
effect. They look upon death in all cases as being the
result of hoodoo (pourri-pourri) ; there is no natural
death. If they hear the cry of a bird in the night
they know that witchery is abroad, and every waking
soul in the village lies as still as death itself lest
the evil spirit be attracted to them. The taboo is
another powerful factor in their lives. No native
would touch anything—house, garden, canoe or even
cocoanut palms—that bore the sign which says in
effect, “Beware of the Dog,” or “Keep off the
Grass.” To ask a boy to climb such a palm for
fruit would be equivalent to requesting him to
commit suicide. On the death of the native the
relatives smear themselves all over with grease and
charcoal, and, in some cases, retain the decoration
for months. Also, there are only certain kinds of
food they can eat. One man I knew, mourning for
his wife, was obliged to drink salt water so many
times a week. Towards the end of the performance
he looked like a skeleton on a hunger strike.
“E.S.S.”: Through the S.A. wheat areas
farmers watch the passing train with anxiety; not
only because it belches sparks, but because careless
travellers have a habit of unburdening themselves
of their superfluous glassware as they pass along.
Numbers of passengers carry bottles of milk or
ginger-beer or water on long journeys, and as these
are emptied they are pitched through the windows.
Many a bush fire has been started by a bottle, or
a piece of broken glass, lying among dry quick-
kindling stuff with a hot sun shining on it. For
this reason the wheat-growers gather up the ruins
and throw them into creeks, or stand them on
stumps. When a bottle-disgorging train passes
every day or so, this is no joke. In these parts the
wax-match is taboo, and anybody seen carrying a
firestick would be chased as a lunatic or arrested
by the nearest trooper for imperilling life and pro-
perty. Nearly everybody uses safety matches, and
no one is allowed to smoke an uncovered pipe in
the august presence of a wheatfield. When the
grass is long and the wheat is maturing the settlers
form themselves into fire-brigades, new handles are
wedged into tomahawks, the wood axe is ground in
readiness for chopping bushes, and a supply of bags,
tied on the ends of sticks, is kept on hand. In-
structions are given to station-masters to transmit
telegraphic and telephonic messages free of cost, so
that when Jones drops a match, or a spark from
his pipe, he can ring up Smith, Brown and Robin-
son to come and put their conjoint foot on it, and
prevent a national disaster. If I can induce the
bottle-hurling fiend to cut this paragraph out and
paste it on his dead marine I’ll have a great recep-
tion next time I carry the National Debt through
the wheatfields of South Oss.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”; I notice that recently one
Abo.-scribe exercised his pen in declaring that breed-
ing cows for milk production would increase tuber-
culosis. Wrong. You can’t breed cows for milk
unless you feed cows for milk also. In feeding
cows for milk you must give them those foods which
produce milk. In doing so, you down the cause
that nearly all diseases are traceable to, i.e., insuffi-
cient quantities of nutritive foodstuffs. Fill up the
animals with coarse, bulky grass, and milk as many
as you can cram on to a holding, and I guarantee
tuberculosis, colic, rickets, etc., will increase like
germs in a sewer-pipe. But cull out the weak, and
feed on rational lines, and you do away with the
very essence of disease. Tuberculosis could be
rubbed out of dairy herds that supply cities with
milk in a very few years, if all milkmen of this
branch were compelled to house and handfeed. In-
fection in this disease chiefly comes from dried
saliva left on the grs-ss by a sick cow. So if the
milkers were fed in stalls, continually under the eye
of intelligent men, tuberculosis could be held in
check, if not clean wiped out. Why blame the cow
for her diseases? It is the rotten, unscientific way
she is fed, or rather unfed. As for exterminating
an industry, as some scribes suggest, because it is
bad in spots, better exterminate creation at once,
and have done with it.
