“Tarago” : Every Abo.-ite with a claim to
the title has observed the howling gale which
follows in the wake of any bush-fire of importance,
even though the atmosphere off the track of the
blaze may be as stationary as a Liberal Govern-
ment’s policy. Now, I don’t want the whole tribe
to rush in with the information that it is caused
by the hot air rising and a cold current hurrying to
fill the vacuum. I know that. The thing I don’t
know is: What sets the course of the wind in the
first place. At the beginning of things the fire must
suck the cold draught from all sides equally; but
subsequently a steady breeze sets in definitely from
one quarter or another, and develops with the pro-
gress of the flames. I have then seen big gums bend-
ing to a gale on the track of a fire, while one could
cut chunks out of the heavy, motionless atmosphere
where I stood, not a quarter of a mile distant. Also
what is the cause of the frequent willy-willy that
whips up the debris over the burnt track when the
ashes are cold and grey?
THE FRISKY ELDER.
Train up a child when he is old, and when he feels young again he will not
depart therefrom.
“A.L.8.”: Evidently the discourse con-
cerning the color of crows’ eyes is endless. In vari-
ous outback Queensland papers I have seen its trail
during the past couple of years. Each writer has
definitely settled the subject; and every time it
has been settled it has been resurrected. “Machete”
(B. 30/10/13) says that the white-eyed bird is the
young of the brown-eyed bird. How does “Machete”
account for the facts: (1) in some districts there
are no brown-eyed crows; and (2) in some places
there are none white-eyed? “The Birds of Aus-
tralia,” by Lucas and Le Souef, says that Gould
thought the raven and the crow were the same
bird, and that he (Gould) was of “Machete’s”
opinion concerning the eye, or words to that effect.
Gould was wrong on the first item, and on the
second Lucas and Le Souef say: “We need not make
two species of crow because of the color of the
eyes, but the raven is certainly distinct from the
crow, and has never been found with a white eye.”
There are three kinds of these birds. The same
authoritative book opines that they are (1) Crow
or hazel-eyed crow ( Corvus coronoides ), Australia
especially north and west, plumage uniform purple-
black, all the body feathers snow-white at the base;
(2) small-billed crow ( Corvus bennetti), S.A., V.
and N.S.W., similar to C. coronoides, but smaller in
all measurements; (3) raven or white-eyed crow
(Corvus australis), Australia and Tasmania, plumage
uniform purple-black. Similar remarks are made
in Leach’s “Australian Bird Book.” But “Machete”
was partly right. I have concluded that the young
of all three have white eyes; that the black-eyed
chap (C. coronoides) is the only one of the three
that will learn to talk ; that he has a kindly nature,
which prompts him to avoid attacking sheep’s eyes,
etc., and that the bird chap mentioned ( Corvus
australis) is the brother of Mephistopheles. The
second bloque ( Corvus bennetti) is a kind of well-
developed cross-breed from these two, with many
of the characteristics of both, being kindly of
savage-natured, according to the condition of his
liver. All are carrion birds, though they also have
a great fondness for fruit.

“S. S. Severn” : Laria was a lordly person-
age with an assured yet deferential aspect. Six
feet, or so, he stood on feet like fenders. A blue
serge rami and a Wreath of frangipani flowers com-
pleted his nursing kit. When I went to the hospital
that morning Laria, being head orderly, was gently
feeding a dying dysenteric with milk from a spoon.
He rose, and stood at attention. “Sinabada,” he
said, “I bin want two weeks for walkabout.” “Too
many sick boys,” I told him. “What for you want
walkabout now?” “I bin want burn down one
village.” “What for?” “One man he bin throw
stone along my father’s house.” Word had come
to him from Maiva, his home, three days down the
coast, that someone had landed a stone on his old
man’s Papuan roof (which is a pourri-pourri, and
means that the head of the house will die unless the
magic is laid). Laria proposed to lay it by walking
into the bush and burning down the first village he
came to. Which would have been extremely in-
convenient for me and the sick boys in hospital.
“Ding Bat”: Anyone reading “Tiaki’s”
par. (B. 4/12/’l3) would imagine that there are
quite a number of white girls living with Maoris.
This is not so. I claim tc( know the Maoris as well
as most pakehas, and I, can recall but tw r o instances
of a white girl being an inmate of a pa. Anyway,
there is not such a lot to get excited about, even if
the girl tattoos herself. The Maori must not be
confounded with the “blackfellcr.” He is a moder-
ately intelligent and moral being as a rule. As for
tattooing. A few years ago it was quite the propah
thing in London. Maori men do not tattoo them-
selves these days, but the women still do, and, for
the life of me I can’t see why they should stop it.

ON THE PREVALENCE OF “BILL,” ETC., IN
AUSTRALIAN LITERATURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How we wish the clever writers *
Of our prose and of our verse
For their characters would take a wider range !
There are some which keep recurring
Like a decimal—we curse
Their recurrence, and we’re aching for a change.
Wo are weary of the legend
Where the sergeant of police
Loves the fascinating sister of a “crook,”
And condones a lot of felonies
And breaches of the peace,
And won’t prosecute when cattle have been “shook.”
People say, “It’s so Australian!”
And some similar event
May have happened long ago as in the tale,
But police are not romantic
Now —at least to that extent—
And the “crooks” they cop are handed to the gaol.
There’s the big, gum-booted digger—
Crimson-shirted, with the sash
Winch he wore when Ballarat first played the game.
And he’s nearly always doing
Something venturesome and rash
When he isn’t “slinging mullock” in his claim.
All the writers since the “ ’fifties”
Have delighted in this type,
Who is always big and masterful and flash.
And, whatever he is doin’—
Diggin’—dancin’—stewin’ tripe—•
Why, he always wears the shirt and boots and sash.
There’s the beauteous bush maiden—
Though her father keeps a pub,
In the local estimation she is IT !—■
And she rides unbroken “brumbies”
Through impenetrable scrub —-
An exasperating female, you’ll admit.
She is cultured and accomplished,
And with virtue she’s supplied
In accordance with a lavish kind of scale.
So, when tempted by the squatter,
She prefers to be the bride
Of a humble chap who runs the local mail.
Ah! these types are too familiar,
They disturb our peace of mind ;
But the one which makes us actually ill,
Is that weird, elusive bushman—
He’s in every tale, you’ll find—
And he bears the simple sobriquet of “Bill.”
This great prevalence of William
Makes our indignation boil —
Every reader of Australian fiction knows
How he prances through the poems
Which are “racy of the soil,”
While he positively permeates our prose.
He’s a shepherd, he’s a shearer,
He’s a breaker-in of nags,
And he always swims some river in a flood.
But he wrecks our nervous system,
And reduces it to rags,
’Till we really feel we want to have his blood i
He’s a stockman, he’s a drover —
He’s on any kind of “lay”
Which may chance to suit the man who slings the
ink —
But he always plays the hero
In an offhand kind of way—
That’s enough to make a reader take to drink.
There is game and there is glory
To be gathered by the bard,
Or the fiction-manufacturer who will
Write a stirring backbloclc story
(Oh! we know it will be hard!)
Or a poem that is innocent of Bill.
N.s.w.

BEALE PIANOS.
The fa& that to-day we are the largest piano firm doing business in the Commonwealth is due solely to the
quality of our instruments.
No effort has been spared in the manufacture of Beale Pianos to reach perfection of tone and ensure
durability. To-day our instruments stand absolutely unrivalled in those respects.
Beale Pianos are specially designed and made to meet all the exigencies of the Australian climate. They
embody every improvement known in piano construction, and are made under the direct supervision of an expert who
has made scientific piano manufacture a life study. In addition, they possess special features which yon cannot get in
other pianos ; such as our permanent steel tuning-system, hexagonal bass-strings, patent cast-in hitch-pins, and our
patent method of building up the casework.
The Beale Permanent Steel Tuning System renders what was hitherto the weakest part of a piano the strongest.
In every Beale Piano the, wrest-plank is made of solid iron. The wrest-plank is that part of the piano into which are
driven the tuning pins to which are attached the wires (or “strings”). In every imported piano the wrest-plank is
made of wood, which has to bear the strain (about 20 tons) of all the wires when the piano is in tune. So that in every
piano that has a wooden wrest-plank there is a probability of the wrest-plank splitting and the piano becoming untunable
in consequence. And in every piano but the Beale the wrest-plank is made of wood.
In all Beale Pianos the wrest-plank is solid iron, a part of the iron frame itself.
Write for our special booklets explaining this matter in detail.
We have recently put on the market the Beale Player-Piano. The Beale Player-Piano possesses the finest
mechanism it is possible to produce, fitted in a Beale high-grade piano of handsome appearance. The artistic possi-
bilities of the interpretation of a roll on the Beale Player-Piano are limited only by the artistic temperament of the
performer. The mechanism is so delicately regulated and every control so perfect that it is easier to play than any other
piano-player.
FREE EIFE INSURANCE: We supply, when requested, a Free Life Insurance Policy, without any charge
whatever, to buyers of Beale Pianos. This covers the life of the customer during the course of hire-purchase, and
insures a valuable and saleable asset to the family without further payment in case of bereavement. The Policy is a
purely free gift from Beale and Co., Ltd., so that no other person may claim under it than the person named by the
customer when accepted by us.
All Beale Pianos carry our written guarantee for Twenty-five Years.
A Beale Piano is lower in price, quality for quality, than any other piano you can buy, and in after cost it is
also cheaper, because it requires far less tuning and less frequent expert attention in every way.
PARTS : Should you by accident damage your Beale Piano we can always replace any injured or broken
parts at a very low cost to you, because we are the actual makers, with our factory facilities here in Australia. But if
you buy an imported piano, and it gets damaged, you will find it expensive to repair, because the agents have no factory
facilities here and they do not carry stocks of spare parts.

If you are at the seaside and wish to cool butter,
milk or the like, secure an earthenware vessel having
a cover. Then insert the lower portion of this in a
box filled with moist sand. Whilst the sand is damp
with sea or other water the foodstuffs in the vessel
are cooled in the same manner as is water in the
familiar waterbag when hung in a draught. Phillips
Ormonde & Co., patent attorneys, 17 Queen Street

WOMAN OF 70 HAS A FACE LIKE
THAT OF A YOUNG GIRL.
LONG-GUARDED FAMILY SECRET
LEARNED THROUGH A PROVIN-
CIAL LADY.
“From mother to daughter, generation
after generation,” writes a lady now 70
years old, “we have been noted throughout
the little provincial town where we live for
the beauty of our complexions, preserved
even to old age. Of late so many of our
friends seem to have been deceived and dis-
appointed by harmful skin remedies that we
have decided it is not right for us to keep to
ourselves any longer the formula that has
always been considered as our family secret,
and to which we owe our ‘eternal youth.’
Therefore we take this opportunity of com-
municating it to our sister readers. Simply
take 2oz. rosewater, 1 dram tincture of ben-
zoin and 2oz. Flowers of Oxzoin, and mix
them yourself at home. Any chemist can
supply you with what is necessary. Shake
the lotion before using and apply with a
clean piece of cotton wool or a sponge regu-
larly night and morning. In less than a
week you will surely be asked what has
caused such a wonderful improvement in
your appearance. The freshness of your
complexion will be dazzling. We know be-
cause we have often made the very same ex-
periment among our friends, and this is the
only thing we ourselves have ever used. It
is our family remedy for all complexion
troubles, and has never failed to give the
very highest degree of satisfaction; in fact,
we consider it an ideal means for preserving
the natural health of the skin. The formula
is old-fashioned, simple and inexpensive, but
always efficacious. We would be very happy
to give you others, but this is the only one
we possess of which we know the benefits
from personal experience.”
Important: Inquiries recently made of a
noted dermatologist proved conclusively that
the above lotion is unequalled for the pur-
poses for which it is recommended, and it
may therefore be regarded as being
thoroughly reliable.*
“Snell”: Foster sleeps out. He had a
cunning little verandah attached to one corner of
the front of his new residence for the purpose, and
settled down to the custom with fond expectations.
He was convinced sleeping out would steady his
nerves, improve his appetite, increase his bulk, and
knock 10 years off his age. But he finds there are
disadvantages. He is a little too near the highway,
and acquaintances of a frivolous turn, When passing,
can throw items on him as he sleeps—to wit, clods,
drowned kittens, old boots, frogs, cat’s-meat, ling-
fish, brambles, lobsters no longer fit for home con-
sumption, oyster shells, meatpies, saveloys and just
dirt. Ail these things have arrived to date, singly
and in bulk. But Foster hopes to live down this
disgusting familiarity. There are further drawbacks,
however. One of these is Craig. Foster does not
know Craig, but Craig is a familiar feature at the
Corner pub, where he is at home to friends between
the hours of seven and midnight. It was two o’clock
in the morning—a hot morning, too—when Foster
awoke, feeling devilishly oppressed. He had a vague
idea that he Was being Overlain, and that his hours
Were numbered. His impressions Were not Wholly
inaccurate. There was a man in the bed, Foster fought
his way from under, relieved himself of the heat and
burden, and then ejaculated, expostulated and threat-
ened vengeance. The man in the bed muttered and
moaned, and turned over twice. He was much bigger
than Foster, and he had his boots on. In an argu-
ment in bed the combatant in boots has a marked
advantage. The stranger had removed several articles
of wearing apparel, and they, with sundry empty
bottles, were scattered over Foster’s tender new
lawn; but he retained his coat, his boots and his
hat. The householder was furious, but he was,small.
When he made an effort to dislodge the intruder he
felt like a woolly caterpillar trying to get away
With a rooted gravestone. When he argued the inter-
loper merely mumbled in his sleep. Foster switched
on the light, and took Craig by the leg and endeav-
ored to haul him off the cot; but Craig straight-
ened his hind extremity suddenly, and shot Foster
into a corner, battering his head viciously against a
brick. Holding his head on with one hand, and
his pyjamas up with the other, the householder went
forth into the night, seeking police protection. He
returned with his feet full of thistle splinters and
old glass, and resumed the argument. Craig refused
to admit that anything was to be gained by discus-
sion. His only remark was wholly irrelevant. He said :
V orgorsakegosleepM’riar !” Then Foster went indoors,
and bedded himself down on the drawing-room carpet.
Hut he could not sleep, and at daylight, full of bit-
terness and other unchristian feelings, he tackled his
Unwelcome bedmate again. All to no purpose. Then
Foster removed what bedding he could from under
and about his unbidden guest, and turned the garden
hose on him. A few people, out early, or homing
late, gathered at the front fence to watch the
small man astride his verandah-rail liberally hosing
a large fat victim who wallowed about in the brim-
ming bed evidently having the time of his life.
Foster expended many gallons of water before Craig

Australoria” : They say that when a man
can’t spit the limits of his dryness have been
reached, but I know different since yesterday. The
evidence was a piece of wreckage tossed high and
dry after a booze storm on the front beach of the
pub. Never have I seen such a distressful eye in
a man. When I asked him to have a drink, his
thanks crackled like parchment. “Pint,” he said
to the beer slavey, then, reaching for a water jug,
he drained it. “Don’t you want the beer?” I
asked. “Rather,” he whispered, “but I want to
soak me mouth so I kin taste it.”

“Pete”: As “Pick” (P. 11/12/T3) desires
the information, let me tell him that crows trans-
port goods as perhaps “Pick” does his pint, viz.,
with their claws. When a youngster on the Upper
Murray I have repeatedly seen and chased the black
imps after they had robbed a nest, and they always
used their claws when lifting the proceeds, whether
of hen, turkey or goose variety. When flying, they
carried the egg closely tucked up to the body.
“McNorth”: If the Society for the Pre-
vention of Cruelty to Animals has a spare moment
it might do worse than turn its attention to the
atrocities of Binghi. The North Queensland binghi’s
plain and ornamental cold-blooded cruelty to heip-
less creatures surpasses
anything I have ever
heard of. Such out-
rages as the plucking
of a live scrub-hen or
turkey, or the cutting
up of a squirming fish
-for bait, are common.
But Binghi excels him-
self in cruelty when
he has turtle for
lunch. He just turns
the unfortunate shell-
back over on the beach
and casually carves a
piece out of its stom-
ach with a blunt
knife. He doesn’t
bother about killing it
first. The carving
process is very grad-
ual, and while it is
going on Mrs. Binghi
and the kids and dogs
wallow in the gaping
aperture, quite indif-
ferent to the groans
and struggles of the
turtle. Frequently the
autopsy is stayed for
an hour or so while
the herd of blood-
stained dogs and nig-
gers devour the first
cut of quivering meat,
so that the partially
disembowelled turtle
has its agony still fur-
ther prolonged. No
one interferes. The
ways of the black are
sacred. I know of one instance when a, Government
officer (ex officio, an assistant-Protector of Abori-
gines) stood by and watcheyl the whole horrible
business described above, and uttered no word of
protest.
“Marere” : Henare was sent from Gisborne
by his Maori brethren to interview the Minister for
Works in Wellington about a bridge. He returned
a fortnight later and reported: “By Cripes!” he said,
“te first man I meet in Wellington is Timi Kara.
He very pleased to see me. We go to Parliament
House. Timi introduce me to Mr. Bellamy, who keep
te Parliament Pub. We have te drink. Irish
whisky. Timi shout. He te fine feller, Timi. He te
best man I ever know. Mr. Bellamy shout. Timi
shout again. Mr,. Bellamy shout again. He te fine
feller, Mr. Bellamy. My word! Irish whisky te
good whisky. Tom McKenzie come in. Timi intro-
duce me. Tom the fine feller, too. Tom shout te
Scotch whisky. By cripes! that te good whisky.
We have te Irish whisky, te Scotch whisky, and
Mr. Bellamy’s whisky. By cripes! Wellington te
fine place. I have te good time.” Just here Wiremu
inquired: “Wot about te pridge, Henare?” “Aw, te
pridge! I dunno. I never see tat cow at all.”
A.G.M.”: Bullocky Bill had signed a
contract to deliver a 22-ton boiler at a certain place
on a certain date; if the boiler didn’t arrive on
time, no cheque for William. The journey was
begun on good roads, but soon the team struck a
track which was soft from recent rain. The boiler
had been loaded too far forward and was driving
the little wheels down and making the hauling twice
as heavy and the going half as fast, so that the
chances of delivering on time looked about as blue
as our great Liberal party. There was nothing for
it but to • shift the load. You might think that
was difficult for one man, but then you don’t know
the kind of man Bill is. He just dived into the
scrub, axe in hand, and presently emerged with four
long stout saplings, one at a time. These he leaned
across the load in two pairs, tent-wise, one pair
for’ard and one aft, so that the tops of each pair
intersected, and he passed chains round the boiler
and up to the intersections and braced up good and
hard so that there was no slack. Then William
got to work with the thing he is alleged to call a
“blanky shovel” and dug away the earth from under
the four wheels till he had lowered the waggon
about three inches, and the boiler swung clear in
the air. The rest was easy. His two polers drew
the waggon a couple of feet forward, and the boiler
was then lowered away on to its proper position, good
and snug. Ten minutes later an optimistic bullocky
hit out merrily on the trail after his cheque, with
his 22-ton parcel riding easy. Which shows that
if you can’t lift a thing off the earth you can pro-
bably lift the earth away from under it.
“Killie”: The silken Union Jack which
had been swapped by the public school in our town
with the similarly-named town in the old Mother-
land had arrived, and was about to be unfurled in
the presence of the local inhabitants and adjacent
cocky families. The shire councillor was in the
chair, and I enclose his speech in order that it mav
be entered on the records: —
Ladies and gentlemen, we have all mustered here
this evenin’ to show our great respects to the great
nag that for thousands of years has braved the
battle, and the breeze (cheers). I ain’t much at
talkm politics. I would sooner talk ter yer about
poddies and the state of our roads, which is scan-
dalous, and the takin’ away of the shire’s subsidy,
not speakin’ about the Rooral Workers (violent
nudge). Right! As I was sayin', this glorious dag
which I am going to ask my wife to unfurl is one of
the most glorious ties that ever tied flesh and blood
together. (Cheers and squealing of infants.) It's
all right, Mrs. —- —, never mind the baby. He ain’t
armin’ me. I like it. I got 10 of ’em meself and
proud of it. Well, as time is now gettin’ very short
I will now ask Mrs. to unfurl the dear old flag
and before she does it would like ter tell you all,
young an’ old, that the only way to keep the dear
old flag wavin’ ’igh above yer ’eads is by work—’ard
work ! Go to bed when the chickens go and get up
in time to' wake the cows up, and yer won’t go far
wrong. I don’t know much about foreign
languages, but I remember something what has
always stuck ter me, an’ that is something that was
stuck up c>n our wall at school. It was “Labor vincit
omnibus ,” which means “ ’ard work beats every-
thing.” (Cheers.)
I don’t know whether he finished there or not. I
did a Gaby Glide.

“Knocker-line” : The Australian cocky who
breeds a foal or two each year is hopelessly biassed
towards the “imp.” fetich. He glances over a stal-
lion’s pedigree, and unless “imp.” occurs every other
line he looks upon the sire as an “Australian mon-
grel,” and doesn’t engage its services. Veterinary
Lecturer F. E. Place, B.V. Sc., M.R.C.V.S., in a
recent article in the S.A. Journal op Agriculture,
wrote: “The heavy loams of Lincolnshire and the
shires were the occasion for the heavy-muscled,
strong-limbed Shire horse; while the hills and dales
along the Clyde influenced the evolution of the
strong-framed but more active Clydesdale. .. .Neither
of these conditions”— i.e., “heavy loams” and “hills
and dales”—“exist in S.A., yet the Clydesdale and
the Shire are the types that are favored for a country
in which the paddocks are level, or, if on the rise,
are very light to work.” To evolve a type of horse
most suitable for conditions in Australia the cocky
should therefore get a bit more of the “local en-
vironment” into his stock. But it is hard work to
drive this into his head; for, as the same lecturer
IT'S AN ILL WIND THAT, etc., etc.

THE VERSATILE FIEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ihe motor-car is responsible for an enormous ex-
tensaon of the week-end habit. The clergy complain
that the week-end habit is loosening the ties that
bound the well-to-do classes to the church.... Motor-
ists, flitting from place to place at the week-ends, do
not attend Divine service. In the spring and the
summer, when the weather is so favorable for motor-
mg, there is a considerable falling off in the church
attendance of people of means .—Melbourne Age.
The dear old Devil I have known
For forty years in many guises.
The red, sardonic lord of woe
Whom Mr. Goethe advertises
Is not the Devil I have seen—
He is a man of modern mien,
Who lures the maids with kindly glances
At small and cheap suburban dances.
The devils that the parsons make
Are those with whom I am acquainted—
An actor once, with natty shape,
Well-corseted and richly painted,
W r ho turns from holiness away
The ewe-lambs with his wretched play,
Or leads the old to Tophet’s valley
With some dull, limping, bran-stuffed ballet.
He turns to musical device,
And ruins us with airs from “Carmen";
Or tempts us Hellward in a trice
As courtliest and best of barmen ;
As some wight, commonplace and fat,
In fresh boiled shirt and Paris hat,
With vaudeville he shocks the Grundies, ,
Or runs a picture shown on Sundays.
He lectured as a grey old gent.
To deadbeat or to Yarra loafer.
But now, the saints to circumvent,
The Devil has become a chauffeur.
Behind him, on a cushioned perch.
He whirls the rich away from church.
The Church keeps one old-fashioned level—
He’s always up-to-date—the Devil!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.


By the authorities
on Modern Science

V
ford
The fact that some of your teeth are
decayed although you have always cleaned them is proot
that the preparations you have used —probably tooth powders
or pastes —do not preserve the teeth.
Tooth powders or pastes can never under any circum-
stances, preserve the teeth from destruction. That follows
from the simple fact that the parts most exposed to injury—
the backs of the molars, the interstices between the teeth, and
the cavities in them—are exactly those which remain untouched
by either powders or pastes. And therefore, mischief once
commenced in these places, advances undisturbed.
But Odol, being liquid, can penetrate the minutest
crevices, and, as it has a real antiseptic effect lasting for
hours, arrests all bacterial and fermentation processes which
destroy the teeth.

Business, Robbery, etc.
This year’s Sydney Show will open on April 17
and go the usual eight days. The prize list, run-
ning to £7OOO, shows few changes of consequence.
In the cattle section more encouragement is given
to pure breeds, which is a doubtful improvement.
“Pure breed” means the breed which some other
country has evolved to suit its own conditions; and
it is quite possible that Australia has yet to produce
its own best “pure breed” by the same process of
crossing and selection —just as it is doing with
wheats. As to wheats, by the way, the Agricultural
Society makes a standing offer to pay £1 for every
three-bushel bag which has won a first prize at a
country show and is entered for competition in
Sydney.
* * * *
“General Average”: “O.K.’s” statement (B. 8/l/’l4)
that “millions of tenants pay fire insurance pre-
mium all their lives and get never a sixpence from
the companies” sounds very much like the wail of
the man who let his accident policy lapse because
while he paid premiums he had never been lucky
enough to break his leg. The assured pays for cover;
and who wants better odds than a hundred pounds
to half a crown? Naturally, if “a miraculous epi-
demic of fires in brick dwelling houses” should
occur, rates would rise. And why shouldn’t they,
if the risk becomes greater? Oil and color stores
are naturally bad risks, and there have been fires in
too many of them lately in most of the Australian
capitals ; and if this puts rates up, the circum-
stances will warrant it. There are two considera-
tions for the assurer; the physical risk and the
moral risk, and in the face of recent experiences
the companies are justified in looking at both con-
siderations with a cold and calculating eye. To
put “O.K.’s” insurance theory on another basis —
think o' nil the people who have paid for the
support of a police force, and have never been
rescued half-dead from an infuriated homicide by
John Hop!
* * * *
The Oldest Inhabitant; A draper’s jubilee is
being celebrated this year. In 1864 Mrs. Way
started a millinery shop in Pitt-street where some
thirty-odd years previously a Mrs. William Nash
also flourished. In those days, in such businesses,
the wife’s name was generally used —the “man mil-
liner” had not become fashionable. When Mrs. Way
hung out her modest sign 50 years ago there were
17 shops, variously occupied, -between it and the
Victoria Theatre. To-day the same space is filled
by three drapery firms —another instance of the
growth of the big business and the crowding-out of
the little tradesman.
“N. or M.”: J. Hudson, first mate of the Rachel
Cohen —the barquentine that got so mixed up with
gales and blizzards and icebergs last winter when
trying to get stores down to Mawson’s party on
Macquarie Island—has patented an invention for
increasing the speed of vessels by decreasing the
water-resistance. Experiments with the oil launch
Mona, at Wellington, seem to show that the thing
works in practice as well as theory, the increase
of speed in this case being, so it is alleged, from
seven to nine knots. Two open tubes traverse the
hull below the waterline, from the bow about two-
thirds of the way to the stern, and slope gently
away from the bow. The inventor’s claim is that
the water which rushes through these tubes lessens
resistance and steadies the boat, at the same time
increasing its buoyancy by upward pressure on the
inner surface of the tubes. It sounds all right. But,
then, did you ever hear an inventor’s story that
sounded all wrong?

“Wonga”: What is the betting that an aerial ferry
service isn’t established before the bridge is built
across Sydney Harbor ? Articles of incorporation
have been filed in ’Frisco by a company which says
it is going to carry passengers in flying boats between
the city and Oakland, the other side of the bay.
The trip will take six minutes.
* * * *
“Hamer”: There is going to be trouble in the
Sydney building trades early in the year unless the
wildly unexpected occurs. For over 12 months
the Building Trades Federation has been figuring out
the best way to get uniform hours and conditions in
its line of business. After January 1 the real cam-
paign will start, and from then _ onwards, wherever,
on a jerry-building or other job, it is discovered that
bricklayer Bill Jones is not a member of the Union,
forthwith not only the bricklayers but also the
unionised hodcarriers, crane-men, laborers, car-
penters, etc., will down tools. The beauty of the
scheme from the toiler’s viewpoint lies in the fact
that even if there is not a single union carpenter or
hodcarrier on the job, there will be sufficient union-
ists in the other callings to hold things up in the
interests of carpenters and hodcarriers who are
unionists.
* * * *
“Far North”: I want to put that very good sport,
Agar Wynne, Commonwealth P.M.G., on to two rot-
ten legacies left to his department by the States.
One is the bad old way of doing business in outback
post offices, where Jim the postmaster tells Bill the
commission agent all the news of the mail and tele-
graph. In a place or two in the Northern Territory
the leakage is over the odds. Often some man in the
street knows the contents of a private wire before
the addressee has taken delivery. Another legacy is
the outback parcels post. Up near here the law of
Queensland still obtains. For the benefit of fat and
influential city merchants the mailman’s heart _ is
broken in carrying merchandise to compete with
local storekeepers who have to bring their goods up
by waggon at great cost. Afghans and other hawkers
make their stock up into small packages and ship it
as mail. Even the horse-mailman carries a hundred-
weight of catalogues and samples to addressees who
have never ordered them, and often refuse to accept
them after they have been dragged anything over
100 miles of rough country on a packhorse. There
should be discrimination in the carriage of commer-
cial papers. Where these uninteresting and mostly
lying catalogues have to be carried by coacli mail,
the postage should be Gd. an ounce. Where the per-
jured literature has to be delivered by horse mail,
postage should be Is. a carat or 9d. per lie.
* * * *
“Hamer”: After next July the boats of the Messa-
geries Maritime Co. will cease to travel to and from
Australia —as passenger vessels. During the ’7os
the M.M. boats were the crack steamships east of
Suez. But they stood still, and the N.D.L. liners
grew in tonnage and luxury and speed, and the P.
and O. did likewise, and so did the Orient ships ; and
now the M.M. vessels have become aged, slow and
evil-smelling back numbers. For years the only good
thing aboard them has been the food. The M.M.
liners were unique in the number of “classes” they
used to carry. There were six; and as, while the
first-salooners could travel all over the deck and the
second-salooners on all the decks except the holy re-
serve of the first class, the sixth class was limited to
its own few feet of deck space, the divisions, on such
a boat as the old Tanais, for instance, were pretty
cramped. Still, the system allowed certain sorts of
mentally afflicted people to travel very cheaply and
yet retain a feeling that they were several classes
superior to someone else.
* * * *
The N. S. Wales cow cocky is busy cutting his
own throat—or slitting up his own pocket, which
he probably regards as a much more serious opera-
tion. He has had the good sense to establish co-
operative creameries and butter factories; but he is
such a poor co-operator, as a rule, that when the
Private Enterprise serpent tempts him with a tem-
porary fraction of a farthing more per lb., he either
sends the business to the serpent or turns the cream
sour in his own factory with his acid remarks. The
result is that harassed co-operative factory managers,
who ought to be in a position to aim at the best,
give the grumbling cocky the benefit of loose grading,
and pass in, as prime, cream which is only second-
class ; and the result of that is that the butter which
ought to be prime is only second-class too. At the
last big London Butter Show various N.S.W. North
Coast factories, which formerly did well, did very
badly ; and the fact that it was the flavor that wasn’t
right takes us back to the unprofitable greed of the
blind cocky who, to squeeze an extra sixteenth of a
penny out of a factory, establishes there a system
of loose grading. When margarine gets further on
to the market, and pushes his second-class stuff right
over the edge, the greedy cocky will probably
recognise that he has made a fool of himself and
done the country a heap of damage incidentally.
No, on second thoughts, he won’t realise the latter
proposition. He knows there is a cow, but he
doesn’t know there is a country—not even incident-
ally.
» * * *
“Bullinbah”: Riley was dozing in the shade of
certain bushes fringing the beach at Cairns (N.Q.),
when two Jap. fishermen came ashore, buried three
bulky bags in the sand, and departed as they arrived
per fishing boat. Riley investigated, and found many
tins of opium, for which he promptly provided an-
other hiding-place. His balance at the bank being
as low as the shores of the Dead Sea, he took into
his confidence a local barber and publican, who put
up the exes, for a first-class trip to Townsville, and
gave him the address of a sure Chow buyer. Riley
landed the stuff 0.K., but the sure and positive
Chow was up-country. That, however, didn’t deter
Riley. He walked into the first Chinese store he
saw (to Riley all Chows were opium slaves) and
made a deal; he was to bring the goods round at
9 p.m. and get £3 10s. per tin. A meek Pong with
a £3 10s. expression in his eye opened the door at
9 p.m., and Riley walked into the arms of three
Customs officers. Riley’s Chow was the president of
the local Anti-Opium League! The fine was £lOO,
and the stuff was confiscated. The barber-publican
at Cairns paid up in order to keep his name out
of the business, but now Riley can doze on the
beach anywhere between Cape York and Melbourne,
and find opium, gold, jewels and wrecked ships, and
not one capitalist will finance him. The word has
gone round that he can’t be trusted with large
financial operations, he being an ass.
* * * *
“Snell”: Let me hear no more of the precision,
energy and acumen of the business man! I have
had more to do with splendid men of business
during the last three months than in all my life
before, and in not one of about 30 dealings was any
amount of precision, energy or acumen displayed by
the man with the goods. He was in every case any-
thing from three days to a month behind with every-
thing, and in six cases out of 10, when the commodi-
ties, apparatus and matters were dumped, said wares,
liquids and things were either the wrong items, or
they were articles suffering from careless handling
and mental defects, or the tooth of time. When I
appealed to the stupendous intellect that was super-
vising a warehouse with the pomposity of a Napoleon
the Greater, it fell into a sort of helpless fatuity. It
didn’t know. It couldn’t possibly imagine—in fact,
something must have happened. Then the tun of
brains got to work to do it all oyer again, and
mostlv jt did it wrong seven days behind the date
specified. Give me no more of it! I wonH have
the great, accurate business intellect as anything but
a dull chimera. Those shockingly unbusiness-like
people, the poets, authors, painters and musicians,
almost invariably know their own game, and get after
it with absolute directness. The poets who don’t
know poetry, the painters who do not know paint, go
out on their necks. And these imposing commercial
humbugs use the term “business-like” as if business
were next to godliness, going up.
“Leo Lear”: The time is long over-ripe for violent
official remarks to the frequent jury that trifles with
its oath in stock-stealing cases. A cattle duffer
commands more sympathy in country districts than
even a sly-grog dispenser in a “dry” area. He is
usually a first-class horseman and bushman, and a
free hand at dealing out joints or steak among
neighbors, after a periodical raid on some station
beef; likewise, he seldom, except in the case of a
feud, touches a poor man’s beast. Once, after a
quarrel, I heard a hardened “lifter” say to a neigh-
bor : “You’ll find me the biggest blanky tick on the
Dawson.” So he was, and still is, for that matter,
never having been convicted, though “had up ’ half a
dozen times. Take up almost any daily paper and
you’ll see loss of stock by duffing. Then comes an
arrest and trial, followed by an acquittal by the
jury, after damaging evidence. In Bananaland I’ve
known men select five acres of land for a homestead
in the vicinity of cattle stations, and a few years
after own 200 breeding cows, not one of which was
purchased. These were run rent free on any lease
that happened to be handy; though I’ve no doubt any
ordinary jury would swallow the pseudo-owner’s state-
ment that his five acres were capable of carrying
the lot all the year round, droughts included. There
are some States —Victoria, for instance—that encour-
age stock-lifting by the absence of any proper Brands
'Act. In Watt’s domain a person can brand with any
old smudge he takes a fancy to, and there are
often 12 marks similar to his own within a radius
of 30 miles. So, as far as recognition _of _ wan-
dering stock by their brand goes, the thing is similar
to identifying a runaway tramcar by the shape of
its wheels. On the other hand, in Queensland, if a
beast from Dalby happened to be spirited to
Oloncurry, and bore no other brand to show a change
of boss than the owner’s registered one, the animal
is at once located in the Brands Book, and the
Dalby resident has a chance of regaining his prop-
erty. A uniform scheme of branding won’t do away
with duffing, but it helps; and where the jury system
is so smellful a lot of help is necessary.
A FINANCIAL CHANTEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It may be asked how the increase in the expendi-
ture has been kept down relatively. In the corres-
ponding half of 1912, £211,890 was appropriated for
sinking fund on the debt, and that liar now been
knocked off. Then, £140,887 less has contri-
buted towards local government, and, apart from
these reductions, the expenditure has increased
£183,115.
That the rate of expenditure on Loan Account has
been fully kept up is evident from the next state-
ment. Nothing has been charged to the Raikvay
Duplications Account in the half-year, but those
works have been paid for out of the General Loan
Account. The loan outgoings have been at the rate
of close on £9,000,000 per annum.
— N.S.W. Financial Expert on the situation.
In Cohen Harbor our ships did ride—
(And a good job, too!)
When the Old Man, he fell over the side—
(And a good job, too!)
We flung out a buoy, and we lowered a boat,
And someone threw a Promissory Note —•
But «we didn’t believe the skipper would float,
So we’ll raise a loan to-morrow!
The Mate he steps to the forepeaks stay—
(An’ it’s cash we want!)
Says he: “We’ll borrow, and H — can pay!”
( Oh, it’s Cash we want!)
We were anchored fast, an’ the tide ran strong,
An’ everything ’boardship was wrong—
For the pay was short an’ the voyage long—
So we’ll raise a loan to-morrow.
Oh, the ’ands must graft, an’ the girls must
grieve—•
(For the Ship’s hard up !)
It ain’t all joy, as you may believe
(When the Ship’s hard up!)
There ain’t no chart of the course we’ve runned,
An’ it’s overboard with the Sinkin’ Fund !
An’ our credit’s broke, an’ we’re bein’ dunned,
But we’ll raise a loan to-morrow.
There’s rocks ahead, an’ there’s fog behind —
(All hands to the pumps!)
An’ Loandon Town’s in a damn queer mind—
(All hands to the pumps!)
The Old Man’s drowned —fish gnaw ’is bones —-
And the Sinkin’ Fund rests with Davy Jones —•
We’re ’ere in Port for a load of Loans —
(Gord send a Loan To-morrow!)
N.s.w.

Thorne’s Whisky is made in the

Glenlivet, the traditional home of

Its smooth, mellow flavor appeals to
the discriminating palate with grati-

never varies because of the care with

blending, patient maturing and
ageing give it every attribute
perfect whisky should possess.
So Thorne’s Old Highland Malt
in the “Black Square” bottle has
been considered
Incomparable

POLITICAL
POINTS
THE TRUSTS
INTRODUCE
THEMSELVES.
America is on the verge of a general open revolt,
as the populace is disgusted at the throttling opera-
tions of the various trusts.— Sydney Telegraph.
THE TRUSTS: “I'm the gentleman you so
kindly barracked for last year."
An industrial upheaval which was just
large enough to deserve half a dozen lines of
print was the proposal made by a small
meeting of large men in Sydney to strike
against payment' of income-tax. The pro-
posal was born dead, amid shrieks of mirth
from the large men who didn’t join in. The
fat person has an absurdly high opinion of
himself in many respects, but about his
capacity for playing the game of insurrec-
tion, all off his own stomach so to speak, he
has few delusions.
A dauntless politician is Hutchinson,
M.L.A., who had to get re-elected for Borung
(Vic.) when Watt included him in the new
Ministry. He told a meeting last week that
“the proper time to have opposed him was
at next general election, hut lie would not
run aioay from the fight." But for Hut-
chinson’s plucky determination to fight for
his job, the other fellow would have scored
a bloodless victory.
Joe Cook attended the first sitting of the
Inter-State Commission, and was vastly
edified thereat. Said he :
The contrast between the way these matters used
to be dealt with in Parliament and the course taken
by the Commission was very marked indeed. The
marvel is that the tariff has so long been made the
football of politicians.
Which truism applies equally to other ques-
tions hitherto controlled by Parliament
cursed with the party system. Also
the comment will be worth recalling
when the Commission’s recommendations
come before Parliament.
Being an M.P. isn’t all beer and cheese.
For their sins, which are many, the members
of the Vic. State Assembly had lately to
listen while one of their number threw this
at Premier Watt:—
Strange you should make those points so vital,
Quick-change artist might well be your title.
Poems of love and friendship at Christmas all revere,
But you will be asking for mercy from electors when
you appear.
The pack behind you looking meek as Sunday-school
kids,
Grabbing at shadows for honors and quids;
Changing in looks, policy never the same;
Joining with any if it looks good game.
It probably helps to explain why the House
hurried into recess. It was afraid of
another poem.
Seeing that almost everybody in the
N.S.W. Labor party is a candidate for elec-
tion to what Henry Parkes used to call
“lioffis,” it ought now to be a simple matter
to abolish the caucus and let the whole
Assembly elect the Ministry. Then the
entire Legislature would be available for
“hoffis.” The idea of Elective Ministries _is
good and logical, but on the democratic prin-
ciple of one-man-one-vote every M.L.A.
should have a voice in the election. It is a
hopelessly conservative idea to disfranchise
a large section of the electors. With one-
man-one-vote in the Assembly the Ministry
might reasonably be fixed for three years,
just as the Assembly, elected on the same
democratic vote by the adult population, is
fixed for three years. But the' disfranchised
section of the Assembly naturally claims the
right of insurrection, just as does a dis-
franchised section of the population. Con-
sequently there is confusion, upheaval, in-
coherence, uncertainty, blither, procrastina-
tion, interruption and all manner of trouble.
When the eloquent, plausible, shuffling, side-
stepping Premier Holman, of N.S.W., is quite
finished talking vague platitudes
about decentralisation, will he
kindly expound his views about a
short railway to connect Twofold
Bay with Monaro and Riverina,
and a still shorter line to connect
the northern part of the State with
Port Stephens, also about a scheme
for establishing State-owned cities
at these places? Holman is a
pleasant person to listen to. He
is as eloquent as a silver trumpet.
He is as affable as a money-lender
or a billiard-shark. He slides as
easily as a pound of butter at
Bourke in the middle of summer.
He .gets round the corner as sud-
denly as the shadow of a lizard.
He waves his arms with all the
agility of a semaphore. But when
one tries to pin Holman down to
any definite information about some
of the allegedly most important
planks of the Labor platform, it is
like trying to put salt on the tail
of a rainbow.
A side issue of the South
African strike was the same mis-:
erable old side issue which hap-
pened some time ago at Brisbane;’
There was the union paper which
supported Labor and the non-union
one which didn’t. (Somehow there
seems to be always the non-union
paper, and it is often the richest
one in the place.) When the general strike
was declared the men on the union or
friendly paper. were called out, and the men
on the non-union or hostile one weren’t, be-
cause they wouldn’t come.
“Discrediting White Australia” was the
title of a leading roar in Sydney Telegraph
(14/1/T4)the theme of the tirade being
that a certain Chinese woman wasn’t allowed
to go ashore for a. few hours at Brisbane.
It was a hearty _ roar, with all the Tele-
soul in it, and it came from the
bottom of the Telegraph’s boots, and the
wind of it blew off the Telegraph’s hat.
Yet, when soap-man Lever discredited White
Australia, by declaring it an all-round failure,
the ’Graph wasn’t moved to the bitterness
of great anger. Which shows how much more
important in the Tory mind is a colored per-
son who is here to-day and dead to-morrow
than a whole continent which has come to
stay.
A perplexing circumstance in the great
South African strike has been the nigger.
There are several or more of him to every
white man. A certain “advanced” section
of Labor has an idea that all workers are
brothers, and the nigger has applauded this
view with energy. In his capacity as a
striker he felt like a man and an ally;
also he felt that he was helping one section
of the white community to biff the other sec-
tion, and in his capacity as a biffer he was
more or less coming into his own. A large
section of the strikers certainly regarded him
as a dangerous nuisance, who was always
liable to break loose and discredit the cause,
but he was there and couldn’t be got rid of.
When a man is followed home by a strange
dog eight times his own size it is always
difficult to get rid of the animal. One way
and another the numerous and unmanage-
able nigger is likely to be a little more
troublesome now than he used to be, and
that is saying a good deal.
THE MULTITUDE OF COUNSELLORS.
When we go across to the military side of the overloaded Defence Department’s central administra-
tion we find a collection of futilities and incompatibles even worse than exists in the naval branch.
Positions appear to have been made merely for the sake of finding billets. Several officers hold
commands the whole of which could be more effectively and economically filled by a single capable
administrator. The most trivial excuse is sufficient for the erection of a directorship or a chief of staff
or an inspector.— Melbourne Age.
THE KID: “I wonder whether all these blokes are here to watch me or watch each other."
The system of providing ready-made
farms for new settlers, as introduced into
Canada some years ago, has found favor in
the sight of Minister Tolmie, of Maoriland.
He proposes that his Government shall
go in for it next session. The supply of
immigrants, imported and home-grown, isn’t
bad, but there are not enough attractiohs
about the present system of dumping down
a wife and family on a 320-acre block and
leaving them to belt, amateur-fashion, into
THE ALL-BUT OPEN DOOR.
“How does Japan look upon Australia?” was asked,
and the new Japanese Consul-General replied: “If
you open the door there will be many glad to come
in.”
NIPPON: “Now I come to think of it, I might
open it myself. ”
the big timber. By cutting up a block of a
few square miles into farms, clearing and
ploughing a bit of each, and building houses
it is hoped to produce townships in record
time, and —amongst other things—to give
mum and the kids some company while the
crops are growing. Tolmie’s Government
will doubtless borrow the money for this
brilliant device.
The Trust does not do badly in Freetrade
Britain when it gets its seven-league boots
on and steps out. Coats, the concern which
just about monopolises the thread industry
in Bull’s Country, made a trifle over
£3,000,000 profit last year, and the dividend
on ordinary shares whooped mildly at the
elevation of 35 per cent. Half a million
was carried to reserves, making them nearly
£9,000,000. Capital is £10,000,000, and all
things in the little garden or flower-pot are
lovely.
If he doesn’t change his mind,
George Meudell will be a candi-
date for Melbourne City at the
October State elections. Meu-
dell is made of wire and whip-
cord. The very abundance of
his energy has often made him
diffuse in wrong directions, but
he is nearly grown-up now, and
acquiring that solidity generally
associated with duller citizens.
A few years ago some doctor
told him that his lungs were
about to kill him; so he went
up to Bendigo to die in pure air.
Before he died he thought he’d
leave new life behind by plant-
ing a lucerne patch and rearing
chickens; and he got so inter-
ested in these things that he for-
got all about the pulmonary
trouble and one day discovered
that the trouble was dead and
had attended its own funeral.
Meudell has a shrewd brain,
much first-hand information, ex-
perience of maDv people and governments, a
merry heart and a big sense of humor, and
the sooner he gets into the Victorian Par-
liament the better for that very disappoint-
ing institution.
The fool-farmers of Victoria have decided
to disband the Rural Producers’ Ass. in
order to “defeat the attempt of the
A.W.U. to cite it in the Arbitration Court.”
The funds are to be vested in trustees, and
future fees are to take the form of dona-
tions, to be used in defending producers’
interests. The Arbitration Court will doubt-
less take note of this little dodge and will
make any award apply to producers gener-
ally, so the sole result will be that the
farmer will rob himself of the chance of
putting his case before the Court effectively.
THE WEIGHT OF WATT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Willie Watt always loses weight at election times,
lie says, and he was already a stone lighter for his
latest efforts. One meeting at Essendon had served
to get away with two or three pounds.
Our Willie Watt is gaunt and thin ;
The jaw is slack that once was grim.
The weary work of getting in
Grows harder every time for him.
But paradoxical is fate—
He’s gaining Fat while losing weight.
The flesh is weak and it may fall,
But he defies the Labor groans,
Ilis back against the Tory wall —
A legislative bag of bones.
Wherefore the democratic lot
Can see through Mr. Willie Watt.
And, not content with losing mere
Material, he barters too
The spirit which from year to year
Has served to pull our Willie through,
And voters save, for his inspection,
The skin lie sloughs at each election.

ECZEMA ON THE FACE.
DRIVEN AWAY BY DR. MORSE’S
INDIAN ROOT PILLS, SAYS NOWRA
LADY.
“I have found Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills an excellent remedy for the blood,”
writes Mrs. Jane Kelly, of Bridge Road.
Nowra, N.S.W. “Some years ago I suffered
considerably with eczema on the face, and
found nothing to drive this malignant com-
plaint away but Indian Root Pills. They
cleanse the blood beyond all doubt and keep
the liver up to the mark, and move the
bowels in a mild manner. They are our
family medicine, and I recommend them to
my friends and acquaintances as an invalu-
able remedy. You can use this testimony.”
This torturing and disfiguring disease has
its cause in an impure condition of the
blood, which often arises from a diseased
condition of the stomach and allied organs
of digestion and nutrition. When digestion
is imperfect the nutrition of the body is
inadequate to its needs. The blood becomes
thin, poisons accumulate in it, and these
poisons often manifest themselves in some
eruptive disease. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills correct diseases of the stomach and
other organs of digestion and nutrition. They
eliminate poisonous substances from the
blood, purifying it and increasing its quality
and richness. They get at the cause and
foundation, and correct diseases of the
blood and other diseases which originate in
a disordered stomach.*
SPORTING
NOTIONS
The joyful news that Sydney is at last to
have a real scientific track suitable for motor and motor-
bike racing and also for aviation is another indica-
tion that the Ma State’s capital doesn’t intend to be
wholly left behind and trodden on in the rush of
progress. At present there is no track in or around
the city on which motor-cars can race, and even
motor-bike contests are highly dangerous. The man
who opens out takes his life in both hands, and
there have been some narrow escapes in the past—
so many, in fact, that the motor cyclists have defi-
nitely given up the idea of racing in Sydney, while
the owners of motor-cars have never even considered
it. The new track is to be called “the' Sydney
Brooklands” —which is another example of the silly
habit of stealing an English name and tying a rag
to its tail to prevent confusion. It will be situated
some nine miles from the G.P.O. It is served by
two lines of railway, and is likely to be also made
available by a tram service which will land people
at the gates. The ground itself is a natural amphi-
theatre, and the ideal shape for a motor track. By
a slight manipulation of the natural features of the
ground anil the provision of a few “hairpin” corners
so beloved bj- motorists, it can be made nearly five
miles long. The whole track will be in full view
of the spectators and sufficiently close to enable the
numbers of the cars to be seen quite distinctly. When
completed it will be the largest track in the world,
nearly twice the size of the English Brooklands, and
capable of carrying 10 cars abreast at full speed,
with plenty of room between. It is a fine bold
scheme, and the promoting spirit is Mr. A Smith, of
Double Bay, a son of the well-known old-time Sydney
sportsman, Mr. Geo. Smith, owner of Bessie Bedlam,
and who was identified with the aboriginal cricket
team which visited England in 1869. The thing was
bound to come, and it is a pleasing circumstance
that it is going to be brought by a Sydney native, and
not an all-fired Yankee.
Henry Hawker, the Australian aviator
who nearly flew round Britain, arrived in Melbourne
last Saturday, and proved to be anything but the
fair maiden’s idea of a hero. And yet he certainly
is one. Just consider the things he has done. He
holds the English duration record of Bhr, 23min.
Alone he soared 11,450 ft. (record) ; with one pas-
senger he flew 12,900 ft. (record) upwards; and, later
on, with two passengers, reeled 10,600 ft. towards the
stars (record again). Then he took three passengers
8400 ft. into the upper air, and brought them back
again (record some more). In addition, he holds
the world’s hydro-aeroplane flight record, and estab-
lished yet another premiership when he bounded sky-
ward 1200 ft. in one minute. He is coming to Syd-
ney shortly, per biplane, and reckons to cover the
550 miles in five hours. After that he will show us
how to loop the loon-. He will probably meet his end
by falling down a front doorstep.
Roger de Paginat has dug out some inter-
esting figures regarding aviation. Just consider this
little list:—
