* * * *
The first aeroplane flight of any import-
ance to take place in Maoriland is booked shortly at
Auckland.. Ex-farmer Hammond has agreed to give
an exhibition on the Defence Department’s mono-
plane, Britannia. As Bill Massey and eo. don’t
know what to do 4 with this white elephant of the
skies, they’re lending it to Hammond in the hope,
perhaps, that he’ll break it. Later on, Hammond
contemplates flying- from Wellington to Christchurch.
With luck the journey, he says, will call for about
two hours. Without luck it will probably call for
a hearse. But that’s not being considered" just yet.
Still it might be worth while for undertakers at each
end of the route to put in tenders for the ex-farmer’s
burial.
Assisted by a gay nor’-easter, Sydney Fly-
ing Squadron blew merrily through two heats and a
final. The whole squadron was out, and half the
Harbor seemed to be floored over with boats. The
excitement in the first heat was the disqualification
of Mona, which crossed the starting line too soon, and
was promptly biffed out. On the other hand, Blanche
and Quibree crossed it too late, and thereby biffed
themselves out. The winner turned up in Livonia,
with Arline and Swastika second and third. In the
second heat, Lilian nearly capsized, but good sea-
manship and bad language kept the craft afloat and
enabled the skipper to secure second place, with Gold-
ing just in front, 23sec. away. But for the snapping
of a spar Lilian would probably have landed the
bacon. In the final, Golding and Acme fought a
duel. Golding was decisively defeated, and Acme
rushed on, to be challenged later by Kismet. But
even fate itself could not stop the triumphant rush of
Acme, and that craft won easily, with Kismet second
and Lilian third. Golding and Livonia were fourth
and fifth respectively.
* * * *
The chief event at the annual Manly
Regatta on Saturday last was the All Yachts Race,
and the chief feature of the struggle was the general
all-round rumpus. Eight yachts competed, and as
the handicaps were given at the beginning of the
contest the spectators knew exactly how the struggle
was going. Petrel romped over the winning line
with a huge lead on Rawhiti and Culwulla 111. But
Oulwulla flew the protest flag, and was understood to
have lodged objections to both the leaders. Aoma
was fourth, and also helped to put a joyous froth on
the beer of life by hoisting a flag of protest. Manly
regattas are often lively, but this was the most
stormy held for a long time. The people who patron-
ised the flagship complained they could see nothing,
and that they were not informed of the results. The
competitors complained on general principles, and it
was a day of general heartburning.
The N.S.W. swimming team sent to Bris-
bane for the championships on the 24th and 25th
is a particularly strong one. First is the mighty
Longworth himself, and with him his great opponent
of a fortnight ago, Cecil Healy. The third man is
the redoubtable A. W. Barry, who recently won the
100yds. State Championship, and the fourth E. C.
Fox, the 220yds. Breast Stroke State Champion. It
is a crowd that will take some beating, and there
will be an awful splash before it is walloped.
* * * *
Alick Wickham, the well-known Sydney
swimmer, turned professional a few weeks ago, and
on Saturday last made his first great splash at the
Waverley League Club’s third annual carnival. The
event was the 100 Yards State Championship, and, as
the event had to be decided in the Bronte bath,
which is just a trifle larger than the ordinary board-
ing-house washing apartment, it had to be split into
three heats and a final. J. Pryer, of North Sydney,
won tne first in lmin. -slsec., with two yards to
spare; S. Kauffman, of Randwick and Coogee, the
second by a yard in lmin. 9sec. ; and Wickham the
third in lmin. 3 l-ssec., with five yards between him
and the second man. In the final Wickham spread-
eagled his field, and won from Pryer by four yards, in
lmin. 0 4-ssec. Which was mighty fast travelling.
The Summer Baseball Competition at
Sydney saw one startling boil-over on Saturday. The
unlucky club is Petersham, which, although it had
worked hard and played well, always found the
fortune of war against it. This week it was up
against Waverley Red Sox, which came, like a lion
full of freshly eaten Daniels, to do the devouring
act. and started the game with two to nil. Then
Petersham startled itself and the onlookers by scor-
ing two each in two sessions while Red Sox froze.
Back again Petersham failed to score, and Red Sox
with perfect assurance put on two. Hereabouts
came the shock of the afternoon and a spellbound
audience saw Petersham make NINE. The flabber-
gasted Red Sox responded with three, but the soul
had oozed out of them, and the team was finally
overthrown by 13 to eight. It was Waverley’s evil
day, for when the Blue Sox went up against
Leichhardt the latter blew it out by seven to six,
which, though close, was quite decisive enough.
The double disaster threw quite a gloom over the
suburb where the burials are.
The Maoriland strike having simmered
out or blown off, the N.S.W. Bowling team will, it is
definitely stated, start for the Isles at the end of this
week. Of course, we have heard that yarn before,
but this time it does look, at the hour of writing, as
though the blessed team will really go. If it does,
its troubles will commence at Auckland on January
26, and end at Wellington on February 20.
* * * ' *
Metropolitan versus Country was the
chief item on the Tennis menu last week at Sydney.
The City overthrew the Gumleaves brigade by 15
rubbers to six—nine to three in the doubles and six
to three in the singles.
Maoriland Lawn Tennis Championships,
lately decided at Auckland, slew all the alleged cham-
pions. Every player of note was wiped out in the
early rounds, and the final was fought out by A. J.
Wallace and C. J. Dickie, from the same little, back-
blocks club in the cow-spanking interior of Taran-
aki. Wallace downed his mate; then they com-
bined and walked right through the doubles, eating
up all the cracks and finishing off Abbott and
McCutcheon in the final.
. Clarence and Lindsay Todd had a joyful
time at the Country Tennis Tournament at Sydney
last week. They gathered the Singles and Doubles
championships, and their team (that of Trundle)
pocketed the country teams championship. The
splendid quality of their play is evidenced by the
fact that during the whole tournament they were
defeated only once, and then in the last set of a long
series of exhausting games.
Three sons of Belial in the form of police-
men put up a special and particularly horrible per-
formance at Bulli (N.S.W.) on Sunday last. A
“two-up” chapel was conducting service, and was
surprised, surrounded and lumbered. Not one of
the whole congregation, some 30. escaped. Why
so much trouble should be taken over “two-uppers”
when so many scoundrels are allowed to run loose
is one of the mysteries of our crack-brained civilisa-
tion, but if there is any leather-medal in existence
for police dexterity these three John Hoppers should
certainly get it.
“J.P.”: If you ask the Yankee Track
Team what’s. the hottest athletic proposition it has
encountered in Maoriland, it will answer promptly:
“Mister Handicap!” The Yanks were absolutely stag-
gered when they stepped lightly off the steamer and
found that they had to run against handicap, such a
thing being quite unknown in the Jewnited States.
Everyone runs off the mark over there, the result
being that the nresent consignment of tourists are all
at sea in handicap events, and try to make up the
leeway too quickly. The whole system, in fact, is
strange to them, and they haven’t struck the correct
method of dealing with it yet.
In the match against South Australia the
Maoriland XI built up 362 without the aid of a
single century, the top score being the 96 of Reese.
Sneddon made 88, Sandman hit 56, Ilemus 46 and
good old Sundries put up the phenomenal score of
26—which was very remarkable for the Adelaide
team. When S.A. got to the sticks it also refrained
from century making, but it recorded 433. Pellew
narrowly escaped the century with 94, and so did
Hill with 92. Steele biffed 73, Wilsmore 57, and
Crawford carried his bat for 48. After that, Maori-
land went back to the old place of business, and lost
two wickets for 23. The bowling averages on either
side were deplorable. With 287 for six wickets,
M.L. closed and tried to rush S.A. through, but
failed, taking three wickets only for 161 runs.
Result a draw.
The eighth round of Sydney First-Grade
Cricket commenced on Saturday last, and was re-
markable only for the superiority of the ball over
the bat under circumstances that favored the imple-
ment of war generallv known as “the willow.” As
the Telegraph remarked, it was “a day made to
order.” But the order did not rise to the occasion.
Not one single century graced the afternoon, and
the highest score amidst perfect surroundings was the
98 of Macartney for Gordon against North Sydney.
11. Davis, of Waverley, filled second place of honor
on the batting list with 92, and J. S. Taylor, of
Western Suburbs, extolled himself by making 83
(no.). There were other creditable scores, but they
were too numerous to mention. Nevertheless it was
the ball’s day out. Petersham made a mighty bad
beginning against Western Suburbs, being dismissed
for 192 and slogged for 138 for the loss of four
Western wickets. Petersham’s agony was due to
the bowling of Owen, who took four for 55, and the
three of Dupain cost 44. Glebe rushed up 141 against
Redfern, and that Syrian suburb replied with 109
for the loss of seven wickets. Glebe would have done
better but for bowler Grummett, who flattened five
wickets for 31, to say nothing of Goddard, who got
three for 25. On behalf of Glebe, Cotter replied
with four for 48. Waverley bashed 271 off the
Sydney bowling, the best of which was that of Rowe,
who got five sticks for 36. When Sydney got to the
wickets it made 10 for the loss of one wicket. Thanks
to Macartney’s 98, Gordon hit up 231, in spite of
the bowling of North Sydney, for whom Hopkins took
three wickets for 20 and Minnett four for 77. When
North Sydney got to the sticks. Macartney again got
busy, and took four wickets for four runs, leaving
North’s score 17 for four. Balmain battered the
University bowling doggedly, and by energy, civility,
punctuality, early rising and strict attention to busi-
ness, hoisted 242 on the board. Stack smashed five
of the wickets for 80 runs, and Barbour three for 12.
By sunset, University had accumulated 25 without
the loss of a wicket. At Manly by the sea, Middle
Harbor fell upon the bowling of the invading Central
Cumberland, and smote it for 127. Coogan, of
C.C., got six wickets for 53, and Best four for 26.
After which, Central Cumberland fled into the tall
timber and was put through for 115, Randell taking-
five sticks for 47, and Rough three for 53. This
was rough indeed.
* * * *
The sixth round of Vic. Pennant Cricket,
concluded on Saturday, was sufficiently long-drawn-
out to suit the most enthusiastic stonewaller, the
commencement thereof having happened back on
December 20 of last year. And as most of the
games were foregone conclusions they evoked little
interest. North, having erected the steep tally of
379 for two wickets (Carlton 122 and H, Stuckey
117 n. 0.), closed; arid told P’ran to bog in. It dis-
covered that the said bogging in was superior to its
own bowling, and could only get the shopkeepers out
once for 295—a two-point victory. ’Varsity, gather-
ing in a feeble tally of 137, had its head knocked
against the wall by the Saints, who scored 297 for
seven wickets, then closed, and sent the Students in
again. The latter managed to save their faces, but
not the two-point licking. Northcote found its ridi-
culous first-innings score of 103 answered by 218 from
South. Going in again, it fared almost as badly,
scoring but 141. whereupon South rattled up 27 for
the loss of a wicket, and so won outright. ’Roy had
put up 202 to Essendon’s 83, and essayed to rush it
through again, but failed dismally, the ’Dons making
253 for nine wickets at the second attempt. Carl-
ton’s 136 was no good against East’s 212, though
sufficient to stall off the straight-out defeat. ’Wood
had the usual practice match with M.C.0., and had
the satisfaction of beating it on the first innings by
185 to 143, though neither score was anything to
brag about.
* * # *
The inability of South Australia to rake
up a representative team to cross the Bight has smit-
ten Westralian cricket heavily. Somehow the East-
erners to a man dislike the Bight, and, also, more’s
the pity, there seems to be a desire not to give Wes-
tralian cricket a lift. Recently an effort was made
to give the State a seat on the Board of Control with
a share of all the financial joys and sorrows attached
thereunto. But while the seat was conceded, the
trimmings were carefully shorn off. It was like ask-
ing a man to dinner and refusing him a drink.
Queensland cricketer 11. Ironmonger reaps
a just and righteous reward for his splendid efforts in
inter-State cricket this season, in the shape of a posi-
tion under the wing of the Melbourne Cricket Club.
Ironmonger is a left-handed trundler of promise, and
his new engagement should bring him the opportunity
to distinguisn himself in the best company. This
scribe is loth to take on the r6le of prophet, but all
the same he ventures to wager a drink that in the
fulness of time Ironmonger will be one of the orna-
ments of the Australian XI.
Sydney jockey B. Cain is an unlucky
wight. A fortnight ago his mount at Canterbury
Park (N.S.W.) capsized, and on Saturday last an-
other brute turned turtle in the Juvenile Handicap
Warwick Farm. On second thoughts Cain is a
mighty lucky wight. In the last spill he was one of
a field of 23, and not one of the 22 trod on him.
Jeff Smith is now in Australia, and, to-
gether with A 1 Lippe, Nat Williams and Jules Du-
bourg, were welcomed to Sydney by the usual dinner
at Hill’s Cafe. Jeff Smith is reminiscent of Clabby,
and at the same time has a suggestion of McGoorty
about him, and should prove a very hefty fighter.
His first match is with Dave Smith, and the prospect
does not seem to charm him overmuch. Smith, by
the way, is said to have recovered from his dog-
walloping and to be eager for the fray. But this
writer has grave doubts whether Smith will ever
fight as well again as he did before his last battle.
At the Melbourne Stadium Boyo Driscoll,
who had such a bad time owing to having swallowed
some false teeth, met Charlie Simpson, of Victoria.
The Briton had the advantage of a pound in the
weight, and piled up points very gaily for a time.
Then he began to wilt. Simpson came with a fine
rush, and during the last two rounds seemed to have
Driscoll done to a turn. But the visitor managed to
evade the knock-out, and saw the full term, and
having the balance of points in his favor, was
awarded the verdict.
* » * »
The much-talked-of battle between the
English Jew, Matt Wells, and Herbert McCoy, the
Australian, drew a crowded house to the Stadium on
Saturday night. The huge place seemed fairly
packed. At the midday weighing, Wells proved to
be four ounces over the requisite 9st. 101 b., and that
extra quarter pound, according to the articles, was
to cost him £2OO in the shape of forfeit. It would
almost have paid him to have the amount cut off.
Like most new arrivals from the Cold Country, Wells
makes weight very rapidly here, and finds it easier
to put on than to take off. He had worked strenu-
ously to get it off, and failed, and the prospect of
parting with £2OO broke his heart. It was an awful
example of the high price of meat. For a time it
looked as though there would be no fight, except
with the mouth. Then the Stadium took a hand,
and intimated firmly that it had some forfeits to
collect in the event of the fight going up the spout—-
£5OO from Wells and £lOO from McCoy. This
brutal remark brought both battlers to their senses,
and the disputed £2OO stayed in the hands of the
Stadium authorities till such times as the principals
could agree to its destination or refer the settlement
to the courts.
In the ring Wells looked easily a stone heavier
than the pallid McCoy. It was obvious that he
would never again fight as a lightweight. Matt Wells
has passed into the welter ranks. So much heavier
and bigger did he look that, at the announcement of
the weights, the rude section of the crowd called the
announcer “Liar!” Wells opened the dance with
quite a different style of fighting from any he-has
exhibited before. He went right in to kill, and com-
menced proceedings with a very dainty exposition of
the McGoorty left hook. He was doing quite nicely
with it when McCoy landed a right on his left eye,
which staggered the Jew, and made him totter all
over Palestine. It was a beauty. But in the second,
Wells returned it in the form of a wallop to the
side of the head that made McCoy’s teeth rattle.
Wells had evidently sacrificed speed for the sake of
heavy hUting, and some of his rights were deadly.
He had the longer reach and he had the weight, and
he made skilful use of both. McCoy had a more or
less shocking time for about TO, rounds. Twice he
was all but outed, and was saved from the final
stroke by the gong. And then, quite suddenly, Wells,
who had exhausted himself trying to make the
weight, went to fragments. He cracked up, his head
began to smoke, and steam rose from his body as he
lumbered round the ring. McCoy was so hard pressed
in the earlier part of the fight that he resorted to all
his bad old tricks —tricks that, with a sterner ref-
eree, will one day get him disqualified. Wells, bit-
terly resenting these tactics, showed a few of his own,
but immediately a partisan house rose to its
feet and went quite mad. Both were warned,
but McCoy seemed to this scribe to be the
greater offender. The break-up of Wells produced
terror in the McCoy camp, which to a man regarded
the obvious distress of the brilliant Semite as mere sub-
terfuge—a trick to lure McCoy into leaving an opening
for a knock-out. But it was soon noticeable that when
Wells did land there was no sting in his blows, which
had also become strangely slow. And yet, exhausted
and smoking as he was, McCoy could not reallv
damage him. There was no possibility of a knock-
out. When the end came, the chivalrous Wells anti-
cipated referee Scott by putting his own hand on
McCov’s head. The Australian had won, and demon-
strated by his manner of doing it that he is not in
the same grade as Wells as a fighter. The man of
Judah was quite exhausted but never in danger of
being knocked out, while McCoy was twice on the
ver T verge of it. The versatility of Wells was re-
markable, and the first welter-weight he crashes into
when well and strong is due for a very rough time.
Wells has developed an unexpected punch, and his new
style of fighting is vastly more satisfying to the ring-
sider than that which he showed on his arrival in
this country.
About a famous N. S. Wales family:—*
Nigel Cox, son of Dr. Arthur Cox, of Sydney,
passed into Osborne Naval College last month, en
route to the British or, it may be, the Australian
Navy. His great-great-grandfather was a sailor —
Lieutenant William Cox —and a notable Australian
pioneer. William begat Edward Cox, of Fernhill,
near Penrith, and he had issue old Dr. James Cox,
who died only the other day. The first member of
this family to attain to any historical prominence
was Robert Cox, tutor to Charles 11. He labored
conscientiously with the Merry Monarch, but the odds
were against him. Charles’s two most salient charac-
teristics —an incorrigible penchant for the frailer sex
and a domainiacal hatred of washing—evidenced them-
selves at an early age and drove Cox to despair, and
ultimately to the chucking of his billet. Bereft of
Robert Cox’s guiding hand Charles grew worse than
ever, with the lamentable result that is to be found
in the histories of several of England’s ducal and
other families. A chronicler of the period relates
that after Cox withdrew from the Royal household,
Charles was so peeved that he vowed never to bath
again, and adhered with splendid courage to his
pledge for some seven or eight years, when a doctor
or a sanitary inspector or some such busy-body
stepped in and insisted on the kingly troth being
fractured.
A wealthy Unionist member of Britain’s
House of Commons was lately sued for the
recovery of £l5OO due to his former tutor,
whom he had engaged at a salary of £OOO to
exercise a wise restraint on his natural fool-
ishness. The M.P.’s defence (which failed
utterly) was that he had been unduly influ-
enced in the matter, therefore his counsel had
to try to make it clear that the Unionist mem-
ber for High Peak was incapable of taking
care of himself. His wife said she “thinks”
he is now able to look after his own affairs.
And of such is the Propputy Defence party
in the House of Corns.
“Billy Blue”: The Sydney City Council
threatens to apply the day-labor principle
to its legal branch, and some stir has been
caused thereby. The first city solicitor, ap-
pointed away back in March. 1843, was
George Robert Nichols. He held on to the
office till his death in September, 1857, and
was succeeded by Richard Driver, who had
been articled to him. Driver did the work
till his death on July 7, 1880, and the follow-
ing month his former articled clerk, George
Merriman, got the job and continued in it
till October 12, 1893, when he went out
under somewhat gloomy, circumstances.
George Charles Waldron was then ap-
pointed ; and on his death his partner, the
present City Solicitor, Percy S. Dawson,
was elected to the vacancy after a stiff battle.
The Council has always paid the profession
by the piece and not by the day or week or
month, and the threatened change is regarded
with an unfriendly eye.
“Mallee Hen”: A Victorian politician
whose wakefulness has passed into a proverb
was recently up in the Mallee doing a little
electioneering. He had spoken over-night,
and having some hours to wait for the train
to Melbourne in the morning, turned into
the barber’s for a shave. The shave fin-
ished, the energetic politician didn’t move,
and then the tradesman discovered that his
victim was fast asleep. It is not every day
that a Mallee barber has a distinguished poli-
tician asleep in his chair, and he proceeded
to make the most of it. On tiptoe, half the
population was brought in to see innocence
asleep in the chair with its mouth very
wide open.
“The Religious Editor”: I saw a mild-
faced, red-whiskered man in a tram the
other day, and the thought struck me that
he belonged to a dying race. When I was
a small boy there were lots of these gentle
red men about. There were some amongst
my own relations, and they often patted me
on the head and predicted that I would be
a great man some day. It wasn’t their fault
that I made liars of them. They had the
reputation of being good husbands and
fathers, and they generally came on Sunday
in black clothes, and were very regular at
church. At home they always sat on horse-
hair chairs in front of fires and twiddled
their thumbs, meanwhile giving off encourag-
ing remarks like “Well, well! Is that so?”
They suffered from a lack of ideas and never
made much money; but they had a pleasant
flow of gossip, never forgot or lost interest
in old friends, and attended funerals in a
pleasant spirit. I think their fireside and
funeral manners were their most remarkable
points. Of course, they didn’t really belong
to this sunlit country ; they imported them-
selves in “the early days,” and, being red,
pious fire-lovers, they wondered exceedingly
(in a placid sort of way) at the black-
haired Australian sun-worshippers they
fathered.

Beauty Collection.
CUTTINGS FROM BEAUTY ARTICLES.
THE CREAM OF MODERN BEAUTY
ADVICE.
“ Wavy ” Shampoos.
“Homely Hints.”
Few people know that stallax o'.n be used as a
shampoo and is far better than anything- else for
the purpose. It seems to have a natural affinity for
the hair, leaving it very glossy, fluffy and with a
pronounced natural “wave.” A teaspoonful of
stallax granules, dissolved in a cup of hot water,
is more than sufficient. Genuine stallax comes to
the chemist only in sealed tins, a quantity sufficient
to make up twenty-five or thirty separate shampoos.
The indescribable lustre it imparts to the hair is
quite inimitable. ** * For an actual hair grower
nothing equals pure boranium. It is quite harmless,
and sets the hair roots tingling with new life.
* * * The use of rouge is almost always obvious, but
powdered colliandum gives a perfectly natural color
and defies detection.
Oxygen to Remove
Bad Complexions.
“Useful Hints.”
Oxygen has the peculiar faculty of destroying waste
matter in the body without affecting healthy tissues
m the slightest degree. Sallow, blotchy and lifeless
complexions are caused by the accumulation of waste
matter which adheres stubbornly to the surface of
the skin. The most practical way to apply oxygen
to this waste matter is to use mercolized wax, such
as may be obtained at the chemist shops. It should
be rubbed well into the skin for several nights and
washed off in the mornings, like cold cream. In
contact with the skin, it releases oxygen, and thus
clears the skin of the disfiguring waste matter. It
is perfectly harmless, pleasant to use and indeed
very beneficial as a skin food. * * * To bring a nat-
ural red color to the lips, rub them with a soft
stick of prolaetum. * * * p'or tired, hot and perspir-
ing feet use a teaspoonful of powdered onalite in a
foot bath.
Effectually Killing
Superfluous Hair.
“Health and Beauty.”
Many women know how to remove ugly growths of
superfluous hair temporarily, but few know how to
remove it permanently. For this purpose pure pow-
dered pheminol may be used. Get about an ounce
from your chemist and apply a little directly to the
objectionable hair. The purpose of the recommended
treatment is not merely to remove the superfluous
hair instantly, but also to kill the hair roots com-
pletely in a comparatively short time. * * * Women
who are annoyed by body or perspiration odors will
appreciate the hint that a light dusting with pow-
dered (white) pergol occasionally is an instantane-
ous corrective. ** * For oilv complexion smart
women are now using the natural allacite of
orange blossoms as a greaseless cream. It holds
powder perfectly, gives the face a cool, fresh ap-
pearance which lasts, and it does not encourage
growth of hair on the face.
Home=Made Beauty.
“Cosy-Corner Chats.”
The very simplest things are often the most effec-
tive in making a woman beautiful. For instance,
ifew people know that a dull or greasy complexion
■can be instantly brought to a pretty, youthful,
natural bloom merely by the application of ordinary
■cleminite dissolved in water. Moreover, the method
cannot be detected even under the closest scrutiny.
The result will last all day long and requires no
powdering even under the most trying conditions
Indoors or out. If you have no cieminite in the
house get about an ounce from the chemist’s and add
only enough water to dissolve it. Apply a little to
the face. It is good for the skin and ‘ immediately
produces that cool, velvety, natural “bloom” so
much desired. * * * To make the eyelashes grow long,
dark and curly, apply a little mennaline with the
finger tips occasionally. It is absolutely harmless
and beautifies the eyebrows as well. * * * Pilenta
soap is the most satisfactory for all complexions. It
even works well in cold or hard water.

The Holman Government Reforms—
After a Fashion.
Dear Bulletin, —Even tlie Holman Gov-
ernment of N. S. Wales lias moments of
what may be vaguely defined as thingum-
bob, for lack of a better word; that is to
say, it has moments when its finances could
hardly be worse, but are so presented that
if you don’t look at them they look a shade
better. For instance, during the last half-
year Holman and Co. enjoyed the magnifi-
cent revenue of £8,300,884, and spent no less
than £8,9(55,09 <, so the apparent and obvi-
ous deficit was £664,213, or at the rate of
more than £1,300,000 per annum. This,
when added to the previous shortage, is
awful enough in all conscience. Again, it
appears that the revenue was £825,555 more
than that for the corresponding half of the
previous year, so the income is expanding
at the rate of over £1,650,000 a year. The
tact that even that very handsome increase
hasn t the least effect in keeping the present
gtiiig of public lunatics out of insolvency is
likewise awful enough. But there is at least
one redeeming feature of a sort. The short-
en U ', e sec ? nd half of 1912 was
2 ’ a ! ld as that for the second half
or lJlo, as shown above, was only £604,213,
so it seems on the surface of things that
though the Holman combine is still the
worst profligate the State has ever possessed
yet it isnt quite so bad as it used to be.
lo put the matter another way, it might
seem that, by comparison with itself, it was
becoming almost sane, though by comparison
with anybody else it is still as mad as a
hatter. And then the details begin to
obtrude themselves. It appears that £247 149
was saved by reducing the contribution paid
into the 1 üblic Works l\ind out of revenue
~~Y lat is to say, by charging so much more
ot the \\ orks expenditure to loans, or using
so much more loan money as revenue, and
hacking another large slab off the pretence
that the State’s land revenue is set aside for
Works purposes. And £211.890 was saved
by stopping altogether the paltry contribu-
r ol li. WIIC ! ~u ,sed to be made in redemption
or the public debt —a curiously mistimed
proceeding, when it is remembered that the
debt is growing faster than it ever did
before, and that the present Government is
pledged more definitely to a policy of economv
and debt redemption than any of its prede-
cessors. The result is that the half-year’s
deficit for July-December, 1913, is really
roo on a ! the growth of the receipts, some
£30,000 larger than the gap in the accounts
for July-December, 1912; but the fact is cov-
ered up in a fashion by an enthusiastic juggle
With the ever-useful loan. It is no marvel
that the Holman combination “chucked”
the empty formality of telling the country in
the early part of the financial year how it
pioposed to dodge the spectre of insolvency,
and went to the electors “on the nod.” in a
manner of speaking. It is a great deal too
late now to put the accounts straight this
year, even if the Government had the faint-
est idea how to perform that feat; but by
judicious reticence and the wonderful agility
shown at the second ballot, the really great
evil the loss of the jobs and the salaries—
has been averted. And now there are three
etc r H if* 106 ahead ~ bar accidents.—Yours,

of Kriischen Salts !
Kriischen Salts, taken regularly for a few mornings
make all the difference between the gloomy pessimist and
a healthy optimist.
Kriischen Salts is not a “fizzy drink.” It is not effer-
vescent at all and therefore has no tendency to produce
flatulence or discomfort after it is taken.
By stimulating your liver and kidneys to activity and
cleansing your system, Kriischen Salts help Nature to cast
off sluggishness. Hot weather ailments disappear, and you
are conscious of an exuberant vitality, a desire to be up
and doing.
Apart from the medicinal qualities of these salts, they
contain many valuable tonic properties—ask your doctor to
glance at the analysis (on every bottle) and tell you if it
is not so.

A Dominion that Prefers to be Dirty.
The crying need for prompt legislative
action to check the spread of syphilis could
have no more convincing proof than was
provided by a case heard a week ago in the
Christchurch (M.L.) Police Court. A mid-
dle-aged woman who has been plying the
oldest profession in the world for some years,
was charged with being an idle and dis-
orderly person, having insufficient lawful
visible means of support. Chief-Detective
Bishop explained that the woman was living
in- her own house in the city, and that she
used the premises for the purposes of Raiiab
of Jericho. She had purchased the place
two years ago for £5OO, and had paid off
£200; She also had money in the bank. The
reason for the prosecution was that she was
, suffering from syphilis., and the police were
“doing their best to prevent what was a
crime against the young men of the city.”
Evidence was given by detectives that the
house was frequently visited by men, and
that when they had spoken to the woman
as to her means of living she had openly
defied them. She said she had taken legal
advice, and had been assured that so long
as she lived alone, and had no other woman
in the house, she could gain her daily crust
as she liked. And unfortunately that is the
Maoriland law. .It was decided by the Court
of Appeal some years ago, in the case of
Cassidy v. Cassells, that the fact that a
woman living alone uses her residence as a
place of prostitution does not constitute that
house a brothel within the meaning and
snuffle of the Act, and the woman does- not
come within reach of the short arm of the
law. It is further laid down that if a
woman possesses property, in cash or kind,
no matter how she came by it, she cannot
be held to be without means. Counsel for
the accused called no evidence to controvert
the police statements as to his client’s
health, but proceeded to show that, accord-
ing to what passes for law in Maoriland,
her condition didn’t matter. The police, he
said, should use their influence to have the
, • u - re P laced on the Statute Book, from
whlch /t had been wiped., through sentiment
and the action of certain “well-meaning”
people—otherwise Wowsers. The magistrate
pertinently asked where the Department of
lublicHealth came in, and the reply of the
Chief-Detective was that it didn’t come in
anywhere. It stayed out. The District
Health Officer —who is, of course, a medical
man—had been applied to, and had explained
that he had no power to deal with such a
case. His department can insist on the noti-
hcation of every case of chicken-pox and of
all other more-or-less harmless infectious
and contagious disorders, but it has no
authority to notice the disease which is to
be the physical and mental ruin of hundreds
ot Maorilanders yet unborn. The matter
was of course, dismissed, but it was re-
gamed a s so grave that the leader-writers
of godly Christchurch were driven to rail
about the inability of the law to deal
with strange women” and “a certain re-
pulsive and highly contagious disease.” Thev
also published the defendant’s name and
address as a public warning. One of the
cauy papers,. indeed, actually mentioned the
words prostitute” and “syphilis” ; so there
is some hope for Maoriland, after all.

Lady.

during the progress of, a “jumble sale,’’

Commissioners of the State Savings Bank of Victoria
FOR CREDIT FONCIER DEPARTMENT.
FIRST ISSUE under the Savings Bank Act 1913.
FREE OF INCOME TAX. Guaranteed by the Government of Victoria.
Debenture Loan “A” for £500,000
at 4 PER CENT.
To MATURE on 30tH APRIL, 1024
CONDITIONS FOR TENDERS.
1. Tenders will be receivable at the Commissioners’ Head Office, Elizabeth Street, Melbourne, for
the whole or any portion of the above amount, until 2 o’clock p.m. on Thursday, 29tn January, 1914,
when tenders will be opened in the presence of tenderers or their agents, who way attend.
2. Each tender must be sealed, addressed to “The Commissioners of the State Savings Dan c,
Melbourne,” and endorsed “Tender for Debentures.”
3. Debentures will be issued in denominations of £lOO or £5OO, or such larger sums as may be
desired by successful tenderers, and applicants must state in their tenders the number and value ot
the debentures they desire to receive.
4. Tenderers may apply for Credit Foncier Debenture Stock in place of debentures; such stock
and the interest, thereon will be payable only to the registered proprietor or his order, and such stock
can only be transferred on the signatures of the registered proprietor and transferee in the presence of
a witness authorised by the Commissioners.
5. Interest at 4 per cent, per annum will be payable half-yearly, on 30th April and 31st October,
the first such payment being due j> n 31st October, 1914, at the Commissioners’ Head Office or at any
branch of the State Savings- Bank of Victoria.
6. These debentures and stock are not liable to any income tax under any Acts of the Parliament
of Victoria.
7. Any tender below £99 per £lOO will not be entertained.
8. Tenderers of the, highest premium will have preference, and to tenderers of even
tures will be allotted proportionally to the applications, as nearly as may be practicable. Tenders
at a price including a fraction ’of a shilling other than sixpence will not be preferentially accepted
by reason of such fraction.
9. Any tender received from the Savings Bank department shall not be allowed to compete with
other tenders, but all other tenders shall have preference.
10. Tenders must be accompanied by a deposit in cash or by a bank draft payable to the Com-
missioners of the State Savings Bank, amounting to 5 per cent, of the face value of the debentures
applied for, and if the tender be accepted in full, or vro rata, the balance must be paid on 30th April,
1914, or may be paid at any earlier date. Interest at 4 per cent,, per annum will be allowed on
accepted tenderers’ lodgments of 5 per cent, and over from date of receipt to 30th April, 1914, and
will be pavable up to that date on the issue of the debentures.
11. The deposit of 5 per cent, will be forfeited to and become the absolute property of the Com-
missioners in the event of the balance remaining unpaid after the date above specified.
12. Commission will be allowed only to brokers who are members of recognised Stock Exchanges
at the rate of ten shillings per cent, on the face value of debentures for accepted tenders.
13 Principal will be payable at the Head Office of the Commissioners in Melbourne.
14 The due pavment of these debentures, and of all interest thereon, is a charge on all the
securities held by and the funds of the Commissioners in the Credit Foncier Department, and is also
guaranteed hy the Government of Victoria. . . ,
15 Copies of the authorising Acts may be inspected and conditions and form of tender can be
obtained upon application at the Head Office of the Commissioners or any of their branches.
16 By section 36 of Act 1481, section 5 of Act 1722, and section 13 of Act 1778, any corporate
bodv charitable or friendly society may invest any portion of the funds or moneys under their control
in the purchase of these debentures; and any executors, administrators or trustees having the power
or duty of investing any moneys mav purchase these debentures with or out of such moneys (unless
forbidden to do so by the act or instrument under which they act), and every such investment and

THEIR PERFECT BUST DEVELOPMENT.

The REXONOLA
The King of ail Talking Machines.
We introduce this splendid musical instru-
ment to the public with every confidence,
knowing that it is the finest sound-repro-
ducing instrument that has been made.
Handsome in appearance, solidly and faith-
fully built of carefully selected material, it is
beautifully finished, and will be an admired
addition to any home, no matter how well
furnished.
The Cabinet or concealed horn style is
undoubtedly the instrument of the future for
home entertainment. In appearance and
economy of space, and for convenience of
operation, it is far in advance of anything
hitherto offered.
The patented sound chamber of the
Rexonola is an entirely new design, and gives
a tonal result which is a revelation, and must
be heard to be appreciated. The tone is full
in volume, rich and clear, without noisiness,
and the quality is delightful.
The Flush Top, with removable motor
board, is a feature of the Rexonola which
is found in no other instrument. Those who
have had experience of other Cabinet instru-
ments, in which the operating parts are at
the bottom of a dark, dust-filled pit, will
appreciate this feature. They will find it a
pleasure to operate the Rexonola, in which
everything is so placed as to be clearly viewed and conveniently handled.
By a simple operation, all that is necessary being the turning of four screws, the
motor board, with motor attached, can be removed for the occasional oiling that is
necessary.
The Motor of the Rexonola is the finest quality Swiss, with perfected worm
gear governor system, ensuring steady and silent running. We. guarantee the
Rexonola motor, and will replace, free of charge, any part that is found to be
defective.
Until you have listened to Caruso or Melba singing through this
beautiful instrument in your own home, the wonderful charm of the
Rexonola is not really known to you. This delightful instrument will
soon be universally admitted as the first musical necessity in a modern
drawing-room.

IN BANANA LAND
“The public debt of Queensland is increas-
ing out of all reasonable proportion to her
population. For the last financial year, while
population only increased 2.3 per cent., the
debt was advanced by 7.5 per cent.” — Out-
burst of candor from the Brisbane Courier.
_ Brisbane is. immensely proud of its strap-
ping fire brigade and its superb new
hydrant, capable of sending a jet half-way
lip the Public Debt —if there is the water to
do it with. But mostly there isn’t. At a
recent big fire the hydrant was therefore'
dropped, and the brigade operated with
buckets and watering-cans.
Bramble Cay, the sandy islet whereon
the Tasman went ashore lately, up near
Thursday Island, got its name from the one-
time revenue-cutter Bramble, which did
survey work .on the Australian coast, and
after being dismantled in ’53 was used for
very many years as a lightship at the Sow
and Pigs in Port Jackson. The islet pro-
duces a vegetable growth which Captain
Cook used to serve up to his sailors. It
was healthy but very unpalatable, and in
those ‘ good old days” the seaman who re-
fused the stuff received a dozen lashes. Then
if he still disliked it he received another
dozen.
The passing out of Joshua Peter Bell’s
widow recalls the fact that Queensland, in a
political sense, doesn’t quite rank with Baby-
lon or even with Venice for age, and Rome
beats it by a nose. Joshua kept the cash in
the States first Ministry (that of Robert
Ceorge Wyndham Herbert) and its second
\ x Mac r , a „ lister ’ s ) for about 19 months
(18(34-66). There was very little cash, but
he kept whatever 2d. there was. He looked
after Lands for a year (’66-’67) in the
fourth Ministry (Macalister’s). (The third
one which was Herbert again, only endured
17 days.) Finally he carried the bag for
nearjy three years (1871-74) in the seventh
Ministry (that of profane old Palmer) and
then he dronoed out of the portfolio-bearing
industry. He died suddenly in a hansom
cab, of all places, in December, 1881. The
deceased lady was formerly Margaret Miller
DOrsey, daughter of Dr. ‘ McTaggart
D Ursey. She married Joshua Peter in 1862.
So she had 19 years of married life, and 32
years of widowhood.
Digby Denham, who takes F. J. McDer-
mott, the Principal Under-Sec., with him
to the English pawnbrokery, first met the
official when Digby was Minister and McDer-
mott Under-Sec. for Agriculture. The yarn
is told that the Under-Sec. was one day
examining some agricultural photos with his
Minister. Lifting up the picture of a hand
lucerne-seed sower, the Under-Sec. for
Agriculture observed that it was “a curious-
looking camera.” They got on well enough
afterwards; but for a time Digby did
wonder what McDermott was doing in that
galley.
The outcry in Bananaland against the
restrictions placed on artesian boring has
led to a revision of the regulations, but the
Government has not been so foolish as to
allow itself to be turned off its course. It
is still necessary to apply for leave to sink
a bore, or to enlarge or alter one, though
it is given out that applications won’t be
hung up so long as they have been hitherto.
The chief restriction again relates to casings.
There are to be two of them, of the best
quality, and of specified weight, etc. Also
each bore must be provided with a cap and
stop-valve so that the flow can be controlled
and measured, this being necessary to cal-
culate the shrinkage, if any. Statistics,
(hough partly guess-work, indicate that the
flow is still decreasing. Last year’s tally in
N, S. Wales showed it to be per cent,
less than it was in the previous year.

THE VISITOR: “Never mind, my poor fellow, there are sermons in stones, you know."
THE RESIDENT: “Maybe, gunner; but if you think we cracks stones all day sear chin'
fer sermons I merely has to remind you this ain't no hidyit asylum yiv got into."
The. old Poll-tax Ministry was fairly well
blessed with this world’s goods. Three of its
Ministers, Cribb, Dalrymple and John
Leahy, have passed out in the last three
years, leaving property valued for probate
at £70,000, £39,000 and £12,000 respectively.
The poorer members of that Cabinet are still
alfve. They can’t afford to die yet.
, One of the five Methodist parsons who
on January 14 celebrated the 50th anniver-
sary of their arrival in Port Jackson is
F. T. Brentnall, now and for 28 years an
M.L.C. of Queensland. He is 80 years of
age. In 1883 an affection of the throat
drove him out of the pulpit, so he bought
an interest in the Brisbane Telegraph,
joined the literary staff, and became chair-
man of the co. on the resignation of James
Cowlishaw in October, ISBS. Having got
well on his feet after quitting the ministry,
Brentnall repaid the denomination the
money it had spent on him in sending him
to Australia.
Cabled that Richard Wingfield Stuart,
pioneer, horse-breaker, company-promoter,
boxer, painter and a few* other things, one
of the last of Queensland’s early-day squat-
ters, has died in London, after a brave fight
with an extra-painful illness. He came to
Queensland when he was 19, in ’62. Amongst
his old cronies were Joshua Peter Bell,
Gordon Sandeman and W. E. Parry-Okeden
—about the three best-known old-timers on
the Downs and in the West. Dick Stuart’s
father was brother to the Earl of Castle-
stuart and his mother’s father was Viscount
Powerscourt; and despite the burden of his
pedigree he was one of the best-liked men
in the Western country. He started as a
jackeroo on Burrandowan under Parry-
Okeden (father of Queensland’s ex-Commis-
sioner of Police), and soon was Okeden’s
mate on a trip to the head of the Dawson —
a very wild part them—looking for new
country. Droving, horse-breaking and
brumby-running filled in the years till ’67,
when he and the Okedens bought Mount
Debateable. He became part-owner of Mit-
chell Downs a while afterwards. There
were a lot of evidences of “Dick’s” versati-
lity about the Mitchell Downs homestead,
lie adorned _ the walls of the dining-room
with horse-pictures, astonishingly well done
for a man who had never learned painting.
More to the purpose, the OMM herd at
Mitchell Downs was one of the finest in
the country. Having sold the place, Stuart
started out forming pastoral companies—
the Western Queensland Pastoral Co. and
the Queensland Co-operative Pastoral Co.
The latter, weighted down with big names
and capital, went into liquidation, and
Stuart went into mining. There, being
over-sanguine and easily taken down, he
fell in badly. He put in a while at selling
fish in W.A., but made a rise again, and was
visiting London about a Tasmanian tin pro-
position he had got hold of when he died.
His widow (he was married twice) is a
daughter of C. A. Lee, patriarch of the
N.S.W. Assembly. She and her two sons
are living at Tenterfield.
Bananaland is looking for a bottle on
which to celebrate the jubilee of its first
railway. Fifty years ago next month the
Arthur Griffith of the day turned the first
sod at Ipswich. Now the State has 4961
miles of track, and hasn’t paid for one of
them. Old Hansard reports show that the
House had a big fight on the question of
gauges—4ft. 81in. or the narrow 3ft. 6in.
Unfortunately for Queensland, and for Aus-
tralia, the streak received the verdict on
points. Commissioner Evans has raked the
scrapheap, and found some of the engines
that were on the rails in those earliest days,
and these he intends to exhibit alongside the
big ClB locomotives now turned out at
Ipswich. Incidentally, somebody deserves
an extra glass from the bottle for the en-
couragement that led Ipswich and Walker’s
(Maryborough) to go in for engine-building.
To-day they not only supply Queensland,
but cut in with the foreigner for southern
contracts.
The police of Cairns the other day walked
into a betting shop to see what the odds
were, and carted 34 gamblers off to gaol.
Of these 24 were Japanese, eight Chinese
and the other two Malays—all busy, of
course, doing .the “donkey-work” of the
continent. ; That’s the sort of foundation
for a White Australia that the Tommy
Tanna party of Queensland laid down. And
it is the foundation that our brilliant vis-
itor, soap-man Lever, wants to extend.
The Dividendaire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am tlie Dividendaire!
Blow with your trumpets and blare!
tiirt not in purple like monarchs of old,
Bearing no trappings of silver and gold.
Yet am 1 potent as one on the throne,
Pampered and proud as the dream of a drone,
Sucking the sap of a country afar,
Sweeping the street as a god in the car,
Mark me and hail me with ear-splitting
blare—
I am the Dividendaire!
1 am the Dividendaire!
Mine are the young and the fair!
•Mine are the nights of imperial riot,
Days that would mount into Heav’n and
defy it;
Mine are the helots that grovel in grime
Bred i’ the gutter and spawn o’ the slime;
Who shall put harriers athwart me and say
“Here is thy limit and this is thy stay!”
Who shall put bounds to the sea of my
pride—
“ Thus and thus far slialt thou be and abide?”
Who shall forbid me and ask if I dare,
Stripping my soul and her nakedness bare?
I am the Dividendaire!
I am the Dividendaire!
Women of loveliness rare,
Practised in witchery, potent in wile.
Wait on my whimsies and fawn for a smile.
Fill thou my halls with a fragrance divine
Hinting at gods, with their nectar and wine!
Why shouldst thou weep for the Virtue of
.old?
He is no niggard to whom thou wert sold !
Dost thou not slumber on thrice-driven down ?
Dost thou not sweep as a queen through the
town ?
Does not the envy of women who stare
Daily pursue thee, the favored and fair.
Pet o’ the Dividendaire?
I am the Dividendaire!
Trumpet my praise i’ the air !
I am the darling of Fate and the gifted :
I am the Calf that the Chosen have lifted;
I am the scourge that your freed has created,
Type of the lust that is ever unsated;
Curse me, ye people, and gibe if you will,
Do! in your bosoms ye worship me still;
Wherefore I smile at the grudge that ye bear.
Faugh at the hate that would raven and
tea r.
f am the Dividendaire!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, 37. 3934
My Dear Thabgomindait,-
Forgive tlie infuriatingly-cramped type-
writing, but the place is literally creeping
with teachers, and the spectacle lias got on
my nerves. They (the teachers not the
nerves) have been having a conference, and
are unanimously in favor, it transpires of
increased screws all round, with bonuses’for
specially deserving cases—a circumstance
which proves touchingly that the dominie
is as human as any other. There is a camp
at Southport for the men, and another at
Cleveland for the women. The Macgregor
has borne his tartan and claymore, to both
thus obviating the demon jealousy. By the
way, the Cleveland ladies’ camp js in reality
only partially so. Two battalions and an
army corps of lady teachers have sought the
shelter and ease and comfort which a couple
of the best pubs afford. Meanwhile away
out in the wild, amid the ravening mos-
quitoes, sand-flies and the like, Miss Henry
and Miss Jessie Lang are teaching the
learned ones to swim, dance, skip and life
save. They know everything else. Minister
Jimmy Blair spent half a day on Wednes-
day watching them skip. He would have
watched them swim also, but they felt he
was a little young for such a spectacle.
Still Jas. bore no petty malice, and lo
prove it he went along with his ornamental
wife and shook the hands of 17 tons of
homing teachers on the gangway of that
fine old marine ruin, the Lucinda. Mrs.
.Timmy wore pink, with a coatee of some
filmy floral stuff. Quantities of speeches
were made, but no serious inconvenience
was occasioned, as a powerful band had
been provided, and it duly drowned the
orators’ remarks.
The Tom O’Sullivans departed by mail
train on Monday morning to join the Orvieto
in Sydney. _ He had the usual farewell meal
handed to him by his department, also a pair
of spy glasses for use on his travels. The
man Blair, abovementioned, will attend to
Lis job in his absence.
Mr. and Mrs. William Thorne are off lo
Europe next month. She specialises in
works of charity—in fact, to her the doing
of good comes as easy as drinking or back-
biting does to others.
On Tuesday morning, Mrs. J. Forsyth fed
Ihe committee of the Lady Lamington Hos-
pital at the Cafe Eschenhagen. The com-
mittee looked as if it had been recently
boiled —a popular weather-effect about this
time of the year. Mrs. (President) Foxton
handed the little guest qf honor a motor
brooch, set with pearls and diamonds. Mrs.
Thorne has been treasurer of the hospital
since its beginning. While she is away the
Forsyth lady will wrestle with the accounts.
Mr. Thorne was mayor of this burg some
decades ago.
Mrs. Thorne was partially to blame for
y'T another light meal on the 16th inst., Mrs.
Kingsbury being _ her associate in guilt,
and Mrs. John Hicks the actual instigator
and responsible party. Mrs. Kingsbury is
going to Rome to represent Queensland at
the Quinquennial Conference of the National
Council of Women. Mrs. Hicks’s sister,
Mrs. Spencer Smith, who is from America,
helped to entertain the guests, and wore a
glad rag that left every female present filled
with envy. The pretty Hicks daughters
sang ravishingly at brief intervals.
One of our few female dentists, Miss
Martha Burns, is going to the Tooth Con-
ference in London this year. Furthermore,
Connie Paten, to whom the medical men here
entrust all such patients as require massage,
proposes to holiday shortly in Japan. She
has massaged herself to a shadow.
Mrs. James, of Melbourne Table Talk,
has been with us for a while; also her in-
tensely military husband.
Pianist Hilda Roberts and her mother
left for Sydney by the Cooma on Wednes-
day, and early next week she will be
married quietly to Mr. Presdee. Mark the
qualifying _ word “quietly.” So many of
these musical weddings are deafening in
their uproariousness. I hear that the wed-
ding dress is a perfect gem.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The Bulletin begins to suspect that our
grandfathers were not the “loyal” old insti-
tutions which the surviving few of them try
to make us believe they ail were. Anyway, in
its extracts from the files of 50 years ago
the Courier quotes this: —
The free Australian colonies threaten that, if transpor-
tation is resumed to Western Australia, they will
pass laws for transporting their criminals to Great
Britain.
Australia, however, stuck to its criminals
and sent Britain its fat landlords instead.
It recalls that when England continued to-
export convicts to Virginia against Yankee
protest, Benjamin Franklin proposed to re-
taliate by landing in Britain cargoes of
American rattlesnakes.
An awful thing has happened in Brisbane
—quite an influential qrowd of hank and
insurance clerks have been calling for a
Wages Board. There was a doubt whether
Percy Adolphus really meant the shameful
tiling, so a ballot was called for by the Indus-
trial Registrar; and, though a majority said
“No,” no less than 226 said “Yes,” and 226
constitute about one-third of the total. And
it was dead-certain that a great many more
than 226 would have said “Yes” if they
could have seen how the question of over-
time was going to be settled. On present
appearances, the clerk who muddles his cash
and has to spend tWo hours finding out the
whereabouts of the missing sixpence would
get heaps of overtime,, while the clear-headed
clerk who never let the sixpence go astray
would get no overtime at all: and until a
plan for overcoming that difficulty can he
suggested the careful clerk said the whole
business had better be hung up. Still there’s
an office management or two that has had a
de’il of a fright.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

Every third or so the Sydney papers
print a story to the effect that, Carmichael
being in poor health, he will probably drop
out of Holman’s Ministry, whereupon he
may be appointed general manager or busi-
ness manager of the N.S.W. railways. Hig
qualifications for the billet are. apparently,
that he is in poor health and is by profession
an accountant. Poor health may be the
thing N. S. Wales is looking for in the man
to whom it proposes to give just about the
most strenuous job at its disposal: but the
fact that a man is an accountant may easily
be given too much weight. Anybody can be
an accountant —there are hundreds and
hundreds of them in Sydney, and most of
them have less practical knowledge of how
to run a business than a first-class barrow-
man has.. Most times the accountant isn’t
a man with either much initiative or much
imagination; and the business manager who
hasn’t a lot of both of those things isn’t
cut out for success. Carmichael, who has
made a very good Minister for Education,
might also possibly make a railway manager;
but the people who are inspiring the para-
graphs suggesting that he should be given
the job really ought to find spme better rea-
son for it than that he is “by profession an
accountant” in poor health.

The portrait of Melbourne’s first P.M.,
the late J. A. Panton, was unveiled at the
new City Court this week. The canvas is
by Mi's. Muntz-Adams, a daughter of civil-
engineer T. B. Muntz, of Malvern. The
bird-percli half-way through the name is due
to the fact that, when taking to studio-life,
the lady resurrected her family-name to dis-
tinguish herself from the mighty army of
plain Adamses. A Muntz brother is Vic-
toria’s “court photographer,” or its equiva-
lent, his highly-superior pictures of John
Fuller in bavgy pants and of John Madden
in a tight-waisted coat being done with the
least possible showing of bagginess and
tightness.
About an Irish gentleman:—
R. W. Pennefather (please pronounce it “Penny-
feather”), who died at Perth on Friday, aged 62,
belonged to an Irish family of lawyers. One of the
crowd, Edward, was Chief Justice of the Queen’s
Bench, and presided at the trial of O’Connell, Duffy
and others in 1844. Another, Richard, was a Baron
of the Exchequer, and sat on the Bench when quite
blind and over 80 years of age. Still another,
Frederick William, grandson of the Chief Justice
alluded to, was called to the English Bar in 1877,
and came to South Australia as private secretary to
Governor Jervois in 1881, and served with him
also when he was Governor of Maoriland. The late
Westralian came to Victoria early, graduated in
Arts and Law at the Melbourne University, and
practised in Victoria and N. S. Wales for 15 years.
He went to Westralia 16 years ago, and entered the
Assembly. He was Attorney-General four years and
an acting Judge in 1903.

ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVY.
Tenders for Jam and Marmalade.
TTIENDERS will be received, subject to
JL the conditions of tendering, until
Twelve noon on Wednesday, 28th January,
1914, for the supply of Jam and Marmalade
to the Royal Australian Navy.
Tender forms, &c., can be obtained on
application to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; or to the
District Naval Officer, Naval Staff Office,
Sydney, Brisbane, Largs Bay (South Aus-
tralia), Fremantle and Hobart.
Tenders to be in all cases on the printed
form, which can be obtained as above.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.'
E. D. MILLEN,
Minister of State for Defence.

IN THE MATTER of The Trustee
Act, 1908, and
IN THE MATTER of The Public
Trust Office Act, 1908; and
IN THE MATTER of the estate of
George Tuckwell formerly of Wel-
lington in the Dominion of New
Zealand but latterly of Sydney and
Cobar in the State of New South
Wales in the Commonwealth of
Australia, Laborer, deceased.
PURSUANT to an Order made by His
Honor Mr. Justice Cooper uncfer Sec-
tion 74 of the Trustee Act, 1908, on the
second day of August, 1913, in the matter of
the above estate all creditors dnd others
(INCLUDING THEREIN THOSE
CLAIMING AS NEXT OF KIN OF THE
SAID GEORGE TUCKWELL) having
claims against the estate of the said de-
ceased are required to send in their claims
to The Public Trustee, Wellington, New
Zealand, on or before the 31'st May, 1914.
and in default of any such claims The
Public Trustee will on the last mentioned
date proceed to distribute the assets of the
said deceased having regard only to the
claims of which he shall then have notice.
DATED at Wellington, New Zealand,
this 2nd day of August, 1913.
J. W. MACDONALD,
Solicitor to the Public Trust Office.
NOTE : —The said George Tuckwell was
by an Order of the said Court on the 23rd
March. 1911, presumed to be dead. He was
a native of Wellington and left to reside in
New South Wales. Claimants must for-
ward full particulars of the parents of the
said George Tuckwell. his brothers and
sisters, the date of his birth and a r6sumd of
his life, which information will be checked
with the true facts in the possession of The

The Inky Way.
“J.D.”: The printers’ strike in the Speck has pro-
vided the unique spectacle of the most vigorously-con-
ducted Labor paper in the Commonwealth marching
arm in arm with its bitterest Tory enemy every day,
and sleeping in the same cubicle every night. The
Hobart Mercury and the Daily Post have for con-
venience been printed in the one establishment by
the working proprietors of local jobbing offices. Ihe
cable page of each has been identical, but on the
leader page the ill-assorted pair have called each
other everything but respectable. There have been
no other pages in which to quarrel, as all the rest
of the paddock has been full of advertisements. The
Post directors, suddenly finding themselves up against
an extraordinary difficulty, apparently took the only
available method of protecting the shareholders pro-
perty from destruction. To have ceased publication
would have spelt sudden death ; to have ceased publi-
cation without consulting the shareholders (all Labor-
ites or Labor leagues and unions) would have de-
served censure; to have attempted publication with-
out the aid of other offices would have been sheer
lunacy. #
By a coincidence which was probably engineered by
the Devil, in its first issue after the printers struck
work, Hobart Mercury supplied in its sermon column
a fiery harangue by the late Rev. T a Image, headed
“Workmen, to Arms!” from the text: “He that
earneth wages, earneth wages to be put into a bag
with holes”—Haggai i., 6. It was inappropriate in
various ways. In the first place, Talmage never put
his wages in a bag with holes ; he knew a lot more
than that. Nor did he put the whole of his wages in
one bag. Then the old Mercury compositors had no
wages just then to put in a bag; also, they had
got the sack. In any case, it is only the most reck-
less toiler who bags his surplus. The wise one,
when he has drawn his princely remuneration on
Saturday, and paid the rent, and bought food and
clothes and sundries for himself and the wife and
the six kiddies, packs the rest of the money away m
kegs in the cellar against a rainy day. He does that
out of profound disrespect for the bank of which
the boss is a director. Haggai, apparently, had no
keg and no cellar, and hadn’t even the price ot a
decent bag. „
“M.E.”: I turned eagerly to an article entitled
“The Australian Press” in Nov. World’s Work, by
Milner Macmaster, and found seven pages of absorb-
ing statements about the Herald, an evening paper
which I learnt was published in Melbourne. I learned,
also, that “The history of the Herald is, m brief,
the history of Victoria.” Enthusiastic descriptions
are given of that paper’s new plant; but 'while the
author devotes half a page to the fact that the
machines have a stop-press device, the press of the
rest of Australia gets little more than a page, this
is compensated for by photographs of A meeting
of the Herald’s directors,” the barn-like Herald
building, the Herald’s reporters’ room, and the
Herald’s machinery.' Also, we are informed that
“the Herald is essentially a newspaper. the
Herald is mentioned 27 times by name; the only
other papers mentioned are the Weekly limes,
apparently published by the Herald which I note
is “one of the most widely circulated of Victorian
weeklies,” the Argus, the Age and The Bulletin-
and each of these suburban rags gets mentioned but
once. Next time I go down to Melbourne I mean to
buy a Herald. # *
From* the Journal op the Institute -op
Journalists, London: —
“EDITOR-IN-CHIEF wanted for a leading Aus-
tralian Daily. Must be a thoroughly capable man
with a good grasp of public questions and an all-
round knowledge of the value of news. Will have
an ample staff both on the Editorial and News side
of the paper. Liberal salary. Age not over 40.
Apply with particulars of career, which will be
treated confidentially, to 0 723, Offices of the In-
stitute.”
Well, assuming that this cry to Macedonia comes
from the Sydney Telegraph office, that concern is at
least consistent. As a Foreign-trade paper it natu-
rally doesn’t think that much besides wool and
wheat can be raised in Australia —though, when you
come to think of it, why shouldn’t a bale of wool or
a bag of wheat edit a “Liberal” Freetrade sheet in
Australia ?
* * * *
“N or M.”: Someone, a week or two ago, com-
plained to the “Inky Way” of newspaper punctua-
tion. It is the press’s unnatural hankering after
figures that worries me. “About a hundred persons
is a roughly-estimated clump; but “about 100” is
an unsuccessful census. The poet who writes of a
thousand thousand” doesn’t mean 1,000,000, but
something uncountable, and you knock all the imagi-
native quality out of “the cattle upon a thousand
hills” when you put the mountains into figures. It
recalls the tombstone artist who, being pressed for
room, set it on record that “A virtuous woman is 5/-
to her husband.”
* * * *
“p.”: Remember the noise that William Kidston
made when, as Treasurer of Queensland, he woke
up one morning about nine years ago and found the
“strictly confidential” draft of a Bill printed in full
in the Daily Mail? The number of printers and
Government officials who were called up by William
the Great alarmed even Brisbane. But this is the
simple story of how the sacred Bill was “stolen.”
Going up Queen-street one morning I was hailed by
a member of the editorial staff of one of the dailies.
“Kidston,” he whispered, “dropped his Money Bill
last night in the House, and So-and-So and So-and-So
picked it up and are minding it for him. If you
come down to-night I can get it from them for you.
It would make a big boom for the Mail.” I hurried
down in a cab, and, having received the precious
Bill, drove furiously to the Mail office. Next after-
noon, when the “sensation” was burning hot, I met
the late John Leahy in the street. Leahy was angry.
“Why the didn’t you put that in the Courier
as well as the Mail?” he asked. I told him I had
nothing to do with the Courier, and didn’t know
they wanted it. “Didn’t that [l5 blanks here] tell
you to put it in the Courier, too?” 1 was sure he
didn’t. [Another 15 exclamatory blanks.] The Mail,
though, didn’t pay £3OO for the “use” of that
Bill, as was frequently stated by those who didn’t
know. All it paid was my cab fare.
BALLADE OF LOVE AND FOOD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Dora is my chiefest joy;
I love the ’witching grace of her,
The sweet and smiling face of her,
Her lips so red, her ways so coy
But I, a bard (unhappy fate!) —
Finances in a sorry plight—
Am driven hard to liquidate
My dainty Dora’s appetite!
When I would lunch on fish and chips,
Then caviare my love demands,
And snipe, though well she understands
These must be earned with verse and quips.
With devilled oysters doth she play ;
When conscious that her teeth so white
Are chewing up a roundelay,
It does but whet her appetite!
The benedictine that she drinks
For me means lengthy hours of toil,
Her fish is cooked in midnight oil;
Yet e’en from this she never shrinks.
She swallows sonnets ev’ry day ;
As much champagne as I can write
Goes in the customary way—
It does but wet her appetite!
L’Envoi.
Three stanzas and an envoy may
Content my love for lunch to-day ;
But much more yet have I to write,
For there’s her supper appetite!
VICTORIA.

How to
Withstand
Hot
Weather.
THE HEATED TERM.
The heated term is withstood without
a whimper by those people who have a
healthy stomach. Hot weather cannot
hurt the man or woman whose stomach
and digestive organs are perfectly
healthy. But you go into December or
January with a disordered stomach, and
see what the result will be.
SUNSTROKE.
Sunstroke attacks only the man or
woman whose stomach or digestive or-
gans are too feeble to protect the
strength, and maintain one’s physical
power to resist the depressing effects of
the summer’s sun.
SUMMER DIET.
Summer diet, with all its vegetables
and unripe fruit, adds its unstable influ-
ence to the depressing effects of hot
weather, causing a still greater decline
in one’s strength and vitality.
IF THE STOMACH IS OUT OF
ORDER.
Ninety-nine out of every one hundred
people whose health and strength run
down in summer allow their stomach or
digestive organs to get out of order.
This is easy enough—in fact, it is hard
to prevent without the use of some thor-
oughly reliable digestant.

and contribute to the tissues all the
nourishment that is contained in such

Dr. Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules lay the foundation for health and the upbuild-

stomach, and by supplying natural juices necessary to perfect digestion, assimua-
