The Devotee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN Clarice kneels in prayer
The dim and hallowed light
Falls softly on her hair,
And on her slim hands white.
Rose-hues and violet
Stream down upon her face,
So serious-sweet, and yet
So filled with youthful grace.
No one can doubt she sees
White angels as she prays,
And hears the melodies
The Hosts of Glory raise.
Her rapt eyes, upward cast,
Behold the Shining Ones,
Who throng the City vast
Lit by the Sun of suns.
For such as she were they,
And when her lips are dumb
And her warm body clay
Like them she shall become.
A chanting host, they tread
The jasper streets, white-stoled,
With halo-circled head
And harp of ourest gold.
Enrapt, removed, demure,
With round, white, slender throat
\nd raised face pale and pure,
She hears each golden note.
Her soul in sacred tryst
With solemn beauty dreams ;
The circlet on her wrist
Gives back the painted gleams.
Her arched, ripe, rosebud lips
In muted tones aspire ;
And dimmed in far eclipse,
The world’s red lures retire.
Her heart (the world remote)
Takes wings to Heaven’s gate ;
The rubies at her throat
Burn red and passionate. I
Men laud her, love her, think
Her ways an angel’s ways;
Her pearl-hung ears grow pink
To hear their song of praise.
She wings the world alone —
A white, unmated dove;
Her heart has never known
The laughing lure of love.
About her winsome charms,
Her soft and shapely form,
No lover locks his arms
With kisses soft and warm.
A still, tall candle-flame
In cloistral shadows set
She seems, untouched by shame —
And yet.... and yet... .and yet!
Like some pale lily tall,
A perfect front she shows,
And yet—in spite of all—
I wonder if God knows?
When Clarice kneels to pray,
A waste of prayer it seems;
She talks with saints by day,
With angels in her dreams.
What need has she for prayer?
Needs here —in yonder slums,
Where Sin with cheeks aflare
And rocking footsteps comes.
Needs here —in yonder den
Where, lost to hope and fear,
Daubed drabs and drunken men
Forget the world in beer.
Needs hereI—in 1 —in yonder lane
Where Want and Crime p’erwhelm,
And Sickness groans in pain,
And harlots have their realm*
When Clarice kneels in prayer,
I pray her prayer may be
To sweeten and make fair
Yon foul iniquity.
Yon drinking den, yon slums,
Yon region in Hell’s thrall —
* * * *
What Devil’s whisper comes? —
“But Clarice owns them all!”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Story of All-e-same.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ 1 LL-E-SAME” is not what they call
her at the Mission, where she may
- J — % ~ s till, for all I know, be going fur-
tively about the endless round of drudgery
which has occupied her these ten years past
—a flat-breasted, withered beldame, prema-
turely aged, even for her Island race.
Only the old-time traders and Government
officials whom whisky and blackwater fever
have spared call her by that name when
speaking of her. As regards the Government
men, at any rate, this is seldom, since such
matters are apt to get into print, and then
the gods of High Olympus frown and mutter
and issue peremptory proclamations in the
Government Gazette, forbidding the publi-
cation of official secrets, while the lion and
unicorn on top of the page wink at each
other across the coat of _ arms. Outsiders,
however, are not so particular, and so the
world is the wiser, if not the better.
The part of All-e-same’s story that is told
here belongs to the days before she went to
stay at the Mission, stepping thither, half
reluctant, from the shadow of the gallows.
To begin in the middle, it may be as well
to say that she was called “All-e-same” be-
cause of the delighted ejaculation which
burst from her when she first beheld herself,
in all the paraphernalia of alleged civilisa-
tion, in the long mirror in the room of the
Missionary’s wife at Ivapa-levi: the mirror,
I am told, being there till this day to witness
if I lie above the ordinary. “All-e-same
sinabada,” “Just like a white lady,” the
delighted little pagan cried in her “pidgin”
English; and “All-e-same” she became for
the future among white and brown alike.
The real beginning of all the trouble was
the Missionary, and when it is added that
he was a well-meaning missionary experi-
enced readers will realise that the trouble
referred to was of a genuine brand. When
Maxwell, who was something of a contractor
for pavements in Hades himself, with his
virulence tempered by a constitutional tired-
ness and a tendency to regard an ineffectual
past through smoke-flavored glasses of
whisky, blew in upon the scene, he was so
obviously a brand to be plucked from the
burning that the good Missionary immedi-
ately rose to the occasion.
Maxwell called himself a trader, but in
that part of the Pacific grit and energy com-
bined with a predatory disposition are re-
quired for success, and consequently his
traffic in beche-de-mer, copra and other odd-
ments did not assume alarming proportions.
Thus it was that when a hard-pushed Lieu-
tenant-Governor came along and offered him
the position of Government Agent for the
semi-civilised district, Maxwell jumped at
the chance. The billet would enhance his
standing with the natives, and it did not pre-
vent him carrying on business on his own
account, while he maintained Government
authority in his district as time and occa-
sion offered. He was given a small squad
of Armed Constabulary to assist him; dour
men from the Northern Rivers, who spoke
no tongue but their own and fraternised not
at all with the lively people of Ivapa-levi.
Maxwell found in his new circumstances
a decided change for the better. Trade im-
proved, and if he used his police chiefly for
the purpose of “recruiting” labor for his
plantation he did no more than every
Government Agent of his day. It is, of
course, superfluous to say that the Govern-
ment Agent system has long been abolished
in the land in which this story is cast.
Fairly settled in life, and waxing fat, it
was inevitable that Maxwell should look
about him for a helpmate. There were
good and sufficient reasons why he should
not seek a companion among his own kind
on the mainland whence he had drifted, so,
very naturally, he called to his aid a pro-
minent citizen of the large sea-coast village
near which he lived.
This headman, like most of his sort, was
a sorcerer of infamous renown, and in the
intervals of imposition and extortion
carried on a marriage-broking business (to
give it a seemly name). As a result of his
conference with the old ruffian, and after a
considerable amount of haggling, Maxwell
paid over a stiff sum in trade and received
to bed and board the young and prepossess-
ing damsel who afterwards became All-e-
-same. She was the niece of the eminent
magician who conducted the deal.
For some little time, then, matters went
well enough. The lady had been duly taken
in matrimony according to the custom of
her people, and at a very flattering figure
which enabled her to snub her dearest
friends with all the aplomb of a Society
hostess. Moreover, she was free-handed with
Maxwell’s trade tobacco, and consequently
was highly popular in her set. With
other matters, too, she was very well con-
tent. She went about clad in native inno-
cence, like her friends, or, if it pleased her,
had her pick of the excruciating trade
dresses in the store. When the tinned deli-
catessen of her white spouse palled on her,
she was free to fall back on the glutinous
taro, or the dreadful dried fish beloved of
her people, and no one said her nay. She
smoked Maxwell’s cheroots, and hauled his
muddy boots off when he staggered to bed in
the evenings, and showed the greatest good
humor and satisfaction with her lot. Re-
garded as a successful marriage of conveni-
ence, the sorcerer’s handiwork could hardly
have been bettered in our own enlightened
midst.
It was at this point in the narrative that
the Serpent made his appearance in the
wings. “Serpent” is hardly a fair descrip-
tion of the Missionary, who was a breezy,
off-hand sort of fellow, with a manner that
was taking with people until they
caught his eye. He entered the sordid little
Eden, however, quite in the best manner of
his professional opponent, but he sought the
ear of Maxwell only, and left his lady alone
for the time being. Beginning gently, he
warmed to his text as he found his hearer
sulky and recalcitrant. He referred to the
Wages of Sin, the Offence against Heaven,
and the necessity of doing the Right Thing.
Also, he cited the Governor’s displeasure, in
a tone that suggested it as being more likely
to take effect than any of the other admoni-
tions. When these failed to move Maxwell,
who was unusually pot-valiant, the Mission-
ary tried his last expedient. He recalled to
Maxwell that his people were still alive in a
West of England parsonage, and might be
interested to hear of him. Also, he re-
called a little matter or two which Maxwell
thought he had buried beyond resurrection
these 10 years past. Maxwell threw up his
hands about here, and bitterly regretting,
with maudlin tears, that lie was too drunk
to assault his spiritual adviser, consented to
anything and everything.
After a seemly interval, during which the
bride took up her quarters under the Mis-
sionary’s roof, the wedding was . cele-
brated with all pomp and flummery in the
big church, built with money earned by
Kapa-levi natives in blood and sweat on the
Thursday Island pearlers. It was on this
occasion that All-e-same bestowed her new
name upon herself. Maxwell wore a coat
for the first time since he had set foot on
the island, and with the assistance of the
Missionary’s chief of staff, a greasy Samoan,
and a more than sufficient load of whisky,
came through the ordeal without accident.
Perhaps, even then, if Mrs. Maxwell —de
jure and all the rest of it now —had been
left to her own peaceful path, with the
trade tobacco, the nudity and the delectable
dried fish, this story,i if it had ever been
written at all, might have, had a different
ending. The mischief of it was that she
was not so left in peaceful, primal depravity
to work out her own weird.
The Missionarv had a wife who was even
more well-meaning and less successful at
hitting the mark than he was himself. A
campaign of civilisation was directed by the
pair against All-e-same, who, under their
tutelage, was to lay aside the instincts of a
thousand generations of savagery in the
course of a few months. By way of a start
All-e-same was baptised, and of course she
was christened “Magdalen.” . The sacred
water dropped from the Missionary’s fingers
on to some blue tattoo-marking on the girl’s
forehead which told to. the initiated the story
of a symbolic worship which was hoary
when Christianity was unborn. All-e-same
passed over what she considered as a weari-

some and inexplicable performance placidly
enough. The blundering attempts of the
Missionary’s wife to teach her to read and
write tried her lazy good-humor more sorely,
and when she found herself, in addition,
compelled to wear the hateful European
clothing, and to observe many strange and
irritating “don’ts” and “must nots,” the situ-
ation began to get beyond her capacity. The
excommunication of her beloved betel-chew-
ing sent her stark, raving mad for naif a
day. When she had cooled down, she found
that Mrs. Missionary, a cold, lymphatic per-
son with all the deadly obstinacy of her type,
was ready to commence where she had left
off, and then for a time All-e-same fell into
a didl and despairing routine.
The change in his spouse was by no means
acceptable to Maxwell. The cheery native girl
who was clean and' comely in a grass rami
was slatternly and dirty in her ill-fitting
European clothing. Maxwell dived deeper
into drink to drown his disgust, and when he
was not snoring drunk he was nagging at All-
e-same with all the rancor of the recovery
stage. Now, nagging is a thing that no sav-
age, however civilised, can endure for long,
and after a month or two of unadulterated
misery All-e-same began to consider in her
hidden heathen heart whether there was not
a way out of it all, preferably a quick and
secret way that would make an end for ever
of the nagging and the books and the boots.
While she was in this frame of mind there
came the Man, who made the Hour for him-
self later, as all men who are Men do.
One evening, after an unusually embittered
passage with the Missionary’s wife, who
flounced off to the Mission in a state of highly
exasperated righteousness. All-e-same flung
off her hated trappings and went down the
village to see her respected uncle on the
mother’s side.
This venerable philanthropist was in an
amiable mood. He sat in the cool evening
air on the platform in front of his house,
benevolently regarding a fine pig which he
had that afternoon succeeded in extorting
from a near-by village under penalty of a
sending of devils. Beside the scorcerer sat
a stranger—young, handsome and athletic,
his bow and quiver and wooden shield shaped
like the figure “8” proclaiming him a moun-
taineer from some village far up on the
jungle-covered slopes of the great range.
For a long time All-e-same spoke with her
uncle, who later called the new-comer into
consultation. The sun went down and the
short twilight slipped into darkness. Still the
three spoke earnestly together, till the women
had ceased flitting about the cooking fires,
and the children had all gone, and only the
grunts of the scavenging pigs broke the
silence.
When All-e-same reached home that night
Maxwell was deep in his usual drunken sleep.
The woman looked at him curiously before
she blew out the kerosene lamp and slipped
down on her mat. I would not like to think
that there was no pity in that look, though
it is doubtful.
After this All-e-same w T as frequently at her
uncle’s house. Maxwell soon ceased to won-_
der at her absences, even if he noticed them/
Every day he drank solidly from noon until
evening, when he collapsed and was put to bed
by his disdainful police, or by the disrepu-
table little ruffian who officiated as cookboy.
Meanwhile, his wife was in constant confer-
ence with her mountaineer lover, who came
and went continually, or with her villainous
uncle in his dark and smoky house. Later,
other members of the village, friends and
relatives, joined the party, and the whispered
consultations were carried on far into the
night, the old man planning and the others
Nnodding acquiescence or interpolating sug-
gestions in excited whispers. During these
■days Maxwell went up and down among the
people. There was no sign nor hint of any-
thing untoward, and to the Missionary, the
Samoan teacher and the police, aliens all, no
one breathed a word of what every man,
woman and child of the village knew was in
the air. The very cookboy who brought
Maxwell his morning coffee knew, and kept
silence, waiting inscrutably for what the
. future held. The village people too, who had
no cause to hate him—who had traded with
him, stolen from him and received gifts from
him —went on trading, thieving and accept-
ing presents with the suave smiling imper-
sonal treachery that has made their name
notorious.
The end, after all this expectation, was
very simple. One evening All-e-same did not
go down to her uncle’s house. The women
and children in the village got indoors early,
and even the pigs were brought into the
houses, since matters might miscarry, and
the northern police were not ' parti-
cular as to what they emptied their
Snider carbines at, if once they were wakened
to a sense of the situation. The constables
slept soundly enough, however, in their hut
a hundred yards or so away from Maxwell’s
native-built house. Up on the hillside, half
a mile away, the white buildings of the
Mission shone wliitely in the moonlight, and
the drone of the surf on the distant reef was
a lullaby. Apparently All-e-same was the
only person in Maxwell’s quarters that night
who was disturbed by the insistent chatter of
a flying fox. She slipped down from the high
verandah into the black shadow of a group of
bread-fruit trees and the chattering stopped.
Half a dozen men were there —her lover and
some men of his following, and, lastly, her
uncle, with an ominous loop of spit rattan,
tough and flexible as steel wire, quivering in
his hand.
A few whispered words and, silent as
shadows, the men went up the steps. Savage
as she was, the girl felt her knees giving under
her, and staggering under the house she
dropped to the ground in an agony of wait-
ing. She could hear the gentle, bare footsteps
as they passed into the sleeping-room above
her. Then, suddenly, after a maddening
spell of silence, the floor of split palm-
stems squeaked and strained at its rattan
lashings as a deadly and voiceless struggle
began above her. The flimsy house shook
and swayed on its piles, till she buried her
fingers in her ears and threw herself face
downward on the ground. Presently the
sounds died away, and one by one the
shadows flitted down from the verandah.
The mountaineer stood over her. “It, is
finished,” he said simply, wiping the sweat
from his brow, while his broad chest heaved
and panted. “Quickly with the keys, and
let us to the store, for we must be in the
mountains before daybreak.”
For a moment All-e-same sat, bewildered
at the thought of what lay in the room
above her. Then, rousing herself at a
second sharp command, she drew the keys
from about her neck and led the way to the
store, which must be plundered before the
long flight for safety began.
J. B. HIGGINSON.
